Logical Fallacy and Plato's Homonymy in the Prime Matter Argument
Logical and historical analysis of the prime matter argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the syllogism used to equate place with prime matter is logically invalid and how Plato's view is reinterpreted through homonymy.

Core Idea
A flawed syllogism and a terminological confusion lie behind the claim that place is prime matter.

This lesson examines the logical structure of the argument that place is prime matter. Sanusi points out that the syllogism uses two affirmative premises in the second figure, which yields no conclusion. He then addresses an attempted correction and examines the attribution of the view to Plato, clarifying that ‘prime matter’ was used homonymously for place.

Guided Path
1
Read the broader passage
Read the Arabic passage from the logical analysis through the discussion of Plato. Pay attention to the argument structure.
2
Understand the logical fallacy
Sanusi states that the argument employs two affirmative premises in the second figure, which is invalid. Identify the premises and why they fail.
3
Examine the attempted correction
He considers a reformulation: 'Place is succeeded by located things, and everything succeeded by multiple things is prime matter.' He shows this is also false because the major premise is obviously false.
4
Analyze the attribution to Plato
The view is attributed to Plato, but Sanusi suggests Plato used 'prime matter' homonymously for place due to similarity in receiving successions.
5
Review the commentator's clarifications
The marginal comments explain that Plato's actual doctrine considered body as a simple substance, and the term 'prime matter' was applied in a specific sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه ) أي المكان ( خارج عن المتمكن) أي ليس جزءاً له ( وإلا انتقل ( المكان ) بانتقاله ضرورة امتناع انفكاك (الكل) الذي هو المتمكن ( عن الجزء) الذي هو المكان، فلا يتصور انتقال الجسم عن مكانه وليس المكان أمراً حالاً في المتمكن وإلا انتقل بانتقاله أيضاً . ولم يذكره لأنه لم يقل به أحد بخلاف الجزء فإنه ( قال بعض قدماء الحكماء : إنه ) أي المكان ) هو الهيولى فإنه ) يعني ( يقبل تعاقب الأجسام) المتمكنة فيه ( ولا يخفى) عليك ( أنَّ حاصله ) هو المكان يقبل تعاقب الأجسام والهيولى ( أيضاً ، ( تقبل تعاقب الأجسام) أي الصور الجسمية ( فهو هو ( أي القابل الأول الذي هو المكان هو بعينه القابل الثاني أعني الهيولى ( وقد عرفت بطلانه ) يعني بطلان كون المكان هو الهيولى بما مر من
المكان ليس جزءاً من المتمكن وإلا انتقل بانتقاله وعرفت أنه) أي الشأن (لا ينتج الموجبتان في الشكل الثاني وما ذكره من هذا القبيل كما ترى ولو أريد إصلاحه بأن يقال : المكان يتعاقب عليه المتمكنات وكل ما يتعاقب عليه أشياء متعددة فهو الهيولى كانت الكبرى ظاهرة الكذب ) وهذا المذهب ينسب إلى أفلاطون ولعله أطلق ) لفظ ( الهيولى عليه ) أي على المكان (باشتراك اللفظ مع وجود المناسبة المكان بین مكاناً والهيولى في توارد الأشياء عليهما، وإلا فامتناع كون الهيولى التي هي جزء الجسم لها مما لا يشتبه على عاقل فضلاً عمن كان مثله في فطانته
قوله : ( وهذا المذهب ينسب إلى أفلاطون) قيل : إِنَّ أفلاطون لم يذهب إلى تركب الجسم من الهيولى ،والصورة بل الجسم عنده جوهر بسيط يتوارد عليه الاتصال العرضي والانفصال، أعني الاتصالين . قلت : ذكر في شرح المقاصد أنَّ ذلك الجوهر المقداري يسميه هيولى من حيث توارد الهيئات المحصلة إياه وتلك الهيئات المتواردة يسميه صوراً لكونها محصلة له ومنوعة إياه . قوله : (باشتراك اللفظ أراد به المعنى اللغوي ليشمل المجاز والمنقول. من حيث هي قوله : ( وعرفت أنَّ لا ينتج الموجبات في الشكل (الثاني على أنَّ الجسم في إحدى المقدمتين بمعنى الصورة الجسمية كما صرح به الشارح وفي الأخرى بمعناه الظاهر بل التعاقب في إحداهما بمعنى الحلول، وفي الأخرى بمعنى الحصول فلا يتكرر الوسط. قوله : ( بأن يقال : المكان يتعاقب عليه المتمكنات ) لم يرد بها المتمكنات متمكنات، حتى يرد أن هذا ليس إصلاحاً للدليل لعدم تكرر الوسط إذ الأشياء المتعددة أعم من المتمكنات فيكون كقولنا : زيد يصدق عليه الإنسان، وكل ما يصدق عليه الحيوان فهو ماش ولا شك في عدم ا انتظامه بل أراد بها المتعددات، فلو بدلها بالأشياء المتعددة لكان أحسن . قوله : ( وإلا فامتناع كون الهيولى التي هي جزء الجسم إلخ) فإن قلت : إنَّ أفلاطون لا يقول : بأنَّ الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة، بل هو عنده جوهر بسيط، والهيولى عنده اسم للجسم من حيث قبوله الأعراض المحصلة للأجسام المنوعة لها والصورة اسم لتلك الأعراض فقوله : التي هي جزء للجسم غير مناسب للمقام . قلت : ظاهر قوله في الاستدلال الهيولى تقبل تعاقب الأجسام أي الصورة الجسمية يدل على أنَّ عنه نقلاً آخر غير ما هو المشهور من مذهبه أو كلامه محمول على التنزل على أنَّ كلام الشارح ليس بصريح في أن الجزئية على مذهبه تأمل .
Then it (i.e., place) is external to the located body, i.e., not a part of it. Otherwise, place would transfer with its transfer necessarily, because it is impossible for the whole (the located body) to separate from the part (the place). So it is inconceivable that a body transfers from its place. And place is not something inhering in the located body, otherwise it would also transfer with its transfer. And he did not mention it because no one holds it, unlike the part; for some ancient philosophers said that it (i.e., place) is prime matter (hayula), for it (i.e., it) receives the succession of bodies located in it. And it is not hidden from you that its result is that place receives the succession of bodies, and prime matter also receives the succession of bodies, i.e., corporeal forms. So it is identical: the first receptacle which is place is the very same as the second receptacle, namely prime matter. And you have already known its falsehood from what has passed.

Place is not a part of the located body, otherwise it would transfer with its transfer. And you know that it (the matter) does not produce two affirmatives in the second figure. And what he mentioned is of this kind, as you see. And if one wanted to correct it by saying: place is succeeded by located things, and everything that is succeeded by multiple things is prime matter, then the major premise would be clearly false. This doctrine is attributed to Plato, and perhaps he used the word prime matter for it (i.e., place) by homonymy with the presence of similarity between place and prime matter in the succession of things to them. Otherwise, the impossibility of prime matter, which is a part of the body, being that is not obscure to any rational person, let alone one of like intelligence.

His saying: 'And this doctrine is attributed to Plato' – it was said: Plato did not hold the composition of body from prime matter and form, rather body according to him is a simple substance that has accidental continuity and discontinuity, i.e., the two continuities. I say: It was mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid that that quantitative substance he calls prime matter from the perspective of the succession of formative accidents, and those successive he calls forms because they give it form and diversify it. His saying: 'by homonymy' – he intended the linguistic meaning to include metonymy and transferred usage. From the perspective of it... His saying: 'And you know that it does not produce affirmatives in the second figure' – on the basis that body in one of the premises means the corporeal form, as the commentator stated, and in the other its apparent meaning; rather, succession in one means inherence, and in the other means occurrence, so the middle term is not repeated. His saying: 'To correct it by saying: place is succeeded by located things' – he did not intend by 'located things' actual located things, so that it would be objected that this is not a correction because the middle term is not repeated, since multiple things are more general than located things, so it is like saying: Zaid is described as human, and everything described as animal is walking, and there is no doubt about its lack of order. Rather, he intended by it multiple things, so if he replaced it with 'multiple things' it would be better. His saying: 'And otherwise, the impossibility of prime matter which is a part of the body etc.' – If you say: Plato does not say that body is composed of prime matter and form, rather body according to him is a simple substance, and prime matter according to him is a name for body in terms of its reception of formative accidents that diversify bodies, and form is a name for those accidents, so his saying 'which is a part of the body' is not appropriate for the context. I say: The apparent meaning of his statement in the argument 'prime matter receives the succession of bodies, i.e., corporeal forms' indicates that there is another transmission of his doctrine other than what is well known, or his statement is interpreted as a concession, and the commentator's statement is not explicit that partiality is according to his doctrine; consider.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that place is prime matter (hayula). It begins by reaffirming that place is not a part of the located body (mumtakan) nor inhering in it, as proven by the impossibility of separation upon movement. It then presents the ancient philosophical claim: place is prime matter because both receive successive bodies. The author refutes this by noting that prime matter is a part of the body, whereas place is not; also, the argument is logically defective because it uses the second figure with two affirmative premises, which does not yield a conclusion. An attempted correction by reformulating the syllogism is shown to be false. The attribution to Plato is examined in the marginal comments: Plato did not actually hold that body is composed of matter and form; rather, he considered body as a simple substance with accidental properties, using 'hayula' in a special sense. The comments also discuss the proper understanding of the logical fallacy, focusing on the repetition of the middle term. Ultimately, the view that place is prime matter is rejected.

Key Terms
الهيولى (prime matter)
المتمكن (located thing)
الشكل الثاني (second figure)
الموجبتان (two affirmatives)
أفلاطون (Plato)
باشتراك اللفظ (by homonymy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The syllogism 'place is succeeded by bodies; prime matter is succeeded by bodies; therefore place is prime matter' is valid.
Correction: It is invalid because it uses two affirmative premises in the second figure, and the major premise is false.

Checkpoint
Why does the syllogism attributing identity between place and prime matter fail?
A) It uses two negative premises
B) It uses two affirmative premises in the second figure
C) It has an undistributed middle term
D) It is a valid syllogism

Key Takeaway
The argument that place is prime matter is both logically invalid (due to the flawed syllogism) and based on a terminological confusion (Plato's homonymous use of 'hayula').

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about God or creation based on an argument that seemed logical but later realized was invalid—like the second figure fallacy here? What did that realization cost you, and how did you correct it?
?
Plato used the term 'prime matter' for both a substance and place, causing centuries of confusion. In your own understanding of Islamic teachings, are there terms you use loosely that might be hiding deeper theological errors? What would it take for you to examine them with the same precision as this lesson?
?
When you encounter a complex theological claim, do you first check its logical structure and terminology, or do you accept it because of the authority of the one who said it? This lesson shows that even a great philosopher like Plato could be misunderstood. How will you guard against such errors in your own learning?

Why Place Cannot Be Form: A Logical Refutation
Refutation of the claim that place is the corporeal form (sura)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equating place with bodily form is logically invalid and what the correct syllogistic reasoning requires.

Core Idea
Equating place and form fails because a valid second figure syllogism needs a negative premise.

In this passage, the author examines a claim that place is the corporeal form of a body. The proponent sees similarity in that both determine and contain the thing. But the author points out a logical flaw: the argument uses an invalid syllogistic form. Let's see how he exposes the mistake and what the correct reasoning must look like.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a reason, and a criticism.
Parentheses indicate the original text has some editorial insertions.
2
Understand the claim
The proponent argues that place is form because both are determiners that contain and measure the thing. Identify the analogy.
3
Identify the logical criticism
The author says the inference is in the 'second figure' from two affirmative premises, which is invalid. Understand why a second figure syllogism requires a negative premise.
The second figure has a universal negative as a rule.
4
Examine the suggested fix
The author notes that adding a premise ('the determiner containing by essence is not multiple') could make it valid, but then it reduces to his own correct position.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why logical precision matters in theological definitions. The refutation shows that superficial similarities do not constitute valid proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم : الصورة ) الجسمية ( لأن المكان هو المحدد ( الحاصر المقدر (للشيء الحاوي له بالذات والصورة كذلك فإن صورة الشيء محددة له وحاوية له بالذات ومقدرة إياه ( وهو من النمط الأول ( لأنه استدلال بالشكل الثاني من موجبتين (إلا أن تزاد عليه والمحدد الحاوي بالذات لا يتعدد فينتج لأن الاستدلال حينئذ يرجع إلى قولنا
And some said: the corporeal form, because place is the determiner, encompassor, and measure of the thing, containing it by essence, and form is likewise, for the form of a thing determines it, contains it by essence, and measures it. And it is of the first type because it is an inference in the second figure from two affirmatives, unless there is added to it: 'And the determiner containing by essence is not multiple,' so it yields because the inference then reverts to our statement.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the view that place is the corporeal form (sura). The proponent argues that place determines and measures the located thing, just as a thing's form determines and measures it. The author criticizes this argument as an invalid syllogism of the second figure with two affirmative premises, which cannot produce a valid conclusion. He suggests that adding a premise stating that the determiner containing by essence is not multiple could save it, but then the inference would reduce to the author's own correct position. Thus, the view is logically flawed.

Key Terms
الصورة (form)
المحدد (determiner)
الشكل الثاني (second figure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because both place and form 'determine' a thing, they are identical in meaning, allowing a direct inference.
Correction: The passage shows that even if two things share a property (being a determiner), that does not logically prove they are the same—especially when the syllogistic form is invalid.

Checkpoint
How is the argument that place is form criticized?
A) It uses an invalid syllogism in the second figure
B) It assumes place is a substance
C) It confuses form with accident
D) It is self-contradictory

Key Takeaway
A valid second-figure syllogism requires a negative premise; thus equating place with form based on common properties is logically unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proponent's argument seemed plausible but was logically invalid. Which of your own cherished beliefs about Islam have you accepted without checking their logical structure? Will you examine them now?
?
The author shows that even a small logical error leads to wrong theology. How carefully do you examine the proofs you use to defend your faith? What step will you take today to improve your reasoning?

The Objection Against Interpenetration of Dimensions
Sanusi presents the objection that interpenetration leads to absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key absurdities that opponents claim follow from allowing interpenetration of dimensions.

Core Idea
Interpenetration destroys certainty about personal identity and forces similars together.

Before we can appreciate the answer, we must first grasp the force of the objection. Sanusi presents two powerful arguments: that interpenetration makes it impossible to trust our knowledge of individual identity, and that it leads to the coexistence of two similar things in one substrate, which had already been refuted. This lesson walks through these two claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the two separate points introduced by 'وأيضاً'.
2
Identify the first absurdity
The first absurdity is that interpenetration would make a single cubit possibly be two cubits, or one person be many, destroying certainty about personal identity.
The author calls this 'obvious sophistry'.
3
Identify the second absurdity
The second absurdity is the coexistence of two similar dimensions in one substrate, which the author says was already refuted earlier.
4
Reflect on the structure
Notice how the objection uses two 'وأيضاً' to build a cumulative case. The first attacks epistemological certainty, the second appeals to an established principle.
This shows the objector is trying to show multiple fatal consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأيضاً فإنه ) أي تجويز التداخل بين الأبعاد ( يرفع الأمان عن الوحدة الشخصية) ويقدح في الوثوق بها ( فإنه يجوز ) على تقدير جواز التداخل كون هذا (الذراع المعين المشخص ذراعين) بل أذرعاً كثيرة ويجوز على تقديره أيضاً، كون شخص واحد من الإنسان شخصين بل أشخاصاً متعددة فيرتفع الوثوق عن أمثال هذه البديهيات، وأنه سفسطة ظاهرة ( وأيضاً فإنه يلزم على تقدير تداخل البعدين ( اجتماع المثلين) فإن ذينك البعدين متماثلان قد اجتمعا في مادة واحدة ( وقد أبطلناه ) فيما سبق . ( والجواب عن ( الوجه ( الأول ( أنا نختار أن البعد ) الذي هو المكان ( لا يقبل الحركة الأينية ( قوله فلا يقبلها الجسم ) أيضاً ( لما فيه من البعد قلنا : ( هذا اللزوم ( ممنوع إذ البعد الذي في الجسم قائم بالمادة ( حال فيها ( و ) البعد (الذي فيه الجسم ) أعني ( قائم بنفسه ) غير حال في المادة ( وإنهما مختلفان بالحقيقة فلا يلزم حينئذ من عدم قبول أحدهما الحركة عدم قبول الآخر ،إياها، إنما يلزم ذلك على تقدير التماثل في الحقيقة
(And also, indeed it) i.e., permitting interpenetration between dimensions (removes certainty about personal identity) and impugns trust in it. (For it is possible) on the supposition of allowing interpenetration, that (this specific determined cubit be two cubits) rather many cubits, and also possible on that supposition that one human person be two persons rather multiple persons, so certainty is removed regarding such self-evident truths, and it is obvious sophistry. (And also, indeed it follows on the supposition of interpenetration of two dimensions (coexistence of two similars), for those two dimensions are similar and have come together in one substrate (and we have already refuted it) earlier. (And the answer to the first objection (is that we choose that the dimension) which is place (does not accept locative motion). (He says: so the body also does not accept it because of the dimension in it. We say: (this implication is denied, for the dimension in the body subsists in matter, (inhering in it), and the dimension in which the body is, i.e., (subsisting by itself), not inhering in matter, and they are different in reality. So it does not follow then from the non-acceptance of motion by one of them the non-acceptance of it by the other. That only follows on the assumption of similarity in reality.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that allowing interpenetration of dimensions leads to absurd consequences: first, it destroys certainty about personal identity (e.g., one cubit could be two, one person could be many); second, it entails the coexistence of two similar things in one substrate, which has already been refuted. The answer to the first objection is that the dimension which is place does not accept 'locative motion' (al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya), while the dimension in the body does accept it because it inheres in matter. These two dimensions are different in reality (one is material, the other self-subsistent). Therefore, the fact that one does not accept motion does not imply the other does not either; the objection's implication only holds if they were similar, which is false.

Key Terms
التداخل (al-tadākhul)
الوحدة الشخصية (al-waḥda al-shakhṣiyya)
السفسطة (al-safsaṭa)
اجتماع المثلين (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first absurd consequence the objector claims follows from interpenetration of dimensions?
A) It leads to the annihilation of dimensions
B) It destroys certainty about personal identity
C) It makes motion impossible
D) It proves the existence of God

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that interpenetration leads to unacceptable consequences: loss of identity and coexistence of similars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If interpenetration were possible, your identity could be multiplied—you could be two persons at once. How does the certainty of your singular identity before Allah affect your sense of personal accountability? Would you still feel responsible for your deeds if you doubted that you are one individual?
?
The objection warns against losing trust in self-evident truths. Are there basic truths about Allah's oneness or your own existence that you have allowed doubts to erode? What would it mean for your faith if you could no longer trust that you are the same person who prayed yesterday?
?
Consider the absurdity of two similar things coexisting in one place. In your spiritual life, do you ever try to hold two conflicting intentions—like love of this world and love of the Hereafter—in your heart? How does that inner contradiction resemble the absurdity described here, and what must you do to remove it?

The Answer: Distinguishing Dimensions by Reality
Sanusi answers the first objection by distinguishing two types of dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the first objection by distinguishing the place-dimension from the bodily dimension as different in reality.

Core Idea
Not all dimensions are alike; place-dimension and bodily dimension differ in essence.

Having presented the objection, Sanusi now provides a precise answer to the first point. He argues that the dimension which is place does not accept locative motion, while the dimension in the body does, because they are fundamentally different: one subsists by itself, the other inheres in matter. Therefore, the implication that the body also cannot accept motion does not follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the answer. Notice how Sanusi begins by choosing a specific premise: that the place-dimension does not accept locative motion.
2
Identify the two types of dimension
The answer distinguishes between the dimension that is place (self-subsistent) and the dimension that is in the body (inhering in matter). They are different in reality.
This distinction blocks the objection's inference.
3
Understand the denial of implication
The objector claimed that because the place-dimension does not accept motion, the body also cannot. Sanusi denies this implication because the two dimensions are not similar.
Implication would hold only if they were similar.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how Sanusi uses an objection-answer structure: he grants the opponent's premise (the place-dimension doesn't accept motion) but denies the conclusion by showing a relevant difference.
This is a classic kalam dialectical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( والجواب عن ( الوجه ( الأول ( أنا نختار أن البعد ) الذي هو المكان ( لا يقبل الحركة الأينية ( قوله فلا يقبلها الجسم ) أيضاً ( لما فيه من البعد قلنا : ( هذا اللزوم ( ممنوع إذ البعد الذي في الجسم قائم بالمادة ( حال فيها ( و ) البعد (الذي فيه الجسم ) أعني ( قائم بنفسه ) غير حال في المادة ( وإنهما مختلفان بالحقيقة فلا يلزم حينئذ من عدم قبول أحدهما الحركة عدم قبول الآخر ،إياها، إنما يلزم ذلك على تقدير التماثل في الحقيقة

(And also, indeed it follows on the supposition of interpenetration of two dimensions (coexistence of two similars), for those two dimensions are similar and have come together in one substrate (and we have already refuted it) earlier. (And the answer to the first objection (is that we choose that the dimension) which is place (does not accept locative motion). (He says: so the body also does not accept it because of the dimension in it. We say: (this implication is denied, for the dimension in the body subsists in matter, (inhering in it), and the dimension in which the body is, i.e., (subsisting by itself), not inhering in matter, and they are different in reality. Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that allowing interpenetration of dimensions leads to absurd consequences: first, it destroys certainty about personal identity (e.g., one cubit could be two, one person could be many); second, it entails the coexistence of two similar things in one substrate, which has already been refuted. The answer to the first objection is that the dimension which is place does not accept 'locative motion' (al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya), while the dimension in the body does accept it because it inheres in matter. These two dimensions are different in reality (one is material, the other self-subsistent). Therefore, the fact that one does not accept motion does not imply the other does not either; the objection's implication only holds if they were similar, which is false.

Key Terms
الحركة الأينية (al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya)
التداخل (al-tadākhul)
التماثل (al-tamāthul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All dimensions are the same kind of thing, so if one doesn't accept motion, neither does the other.
Correction: The dimension in place is self-subsistent, while the dimension in body inheres in matter; they differ in reality, so the implication does not follow.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi deny that the body cannot accept locative motion just because the place-dimension does not?
A) Because the body does not have a dimension
B) Because the dimension in the body and the place-dimension are different in reality
C) Because locative motion is impossible for all dimensions
D) Because the objection is based on a false premise about motion

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the first objection by distinguishing two kinds of dimensions: one self-subsistent, the other inhering in matter, which are different in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi refused to accept a seemingly logical inference because he recognized a hidden difference. When was the last time you accepted a conclusion about your faith or life based on a superficial similarity, without investigating the underlying realities? What might be the cost of continuing to do so?
?
The distinction between what inheres and what is self-subsistent is crucial here. Think about your relationship with Allah—do you sometimes treat His attributes as if they were like human attributes, inhering in Him as accidents inhere in bodies? How can you train yourself to distinguish the Creator from creation in your thoughts and worship?
?
Sanusi's answer demands precision in defining terms. In your own spiritual life, are there areas where you use words like 'love,' 'mercy,' or 'justice' loosely for both Allah and humans, blurring the essential difference? What step will you take today to correct this?

Refutation of Place as Self-Subsistent: The Substrate Argument
Sanusi presents and refutes the substrate argument against a self-subsistent place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that dimensions necessarily require a substrate is refuted by distinguishing material from immaterial dimensions and by allowing interpenetration.

Core Idea
Dimensions are not all alike; some are self-subsistent.

This lesson examines a key objection against the idea that place could be a self-subsistent dimension. The objection claims that every dimension requires a substrate, but the author shows this rests on an assumption about similarity between material and immaterial dimensions. We will see the objection, its critique, and the answer to a related concern about two dimensions meeting.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'وما يقال' to 'بل محتاجاً' to grasp the objection: a dimension by itself necessitates a substrate, so it cannot be self-subsistent.
Notice the logical structure: if it needs a substrate, it cannot be independent; if independent, it cannot inhere.
2
Identify the assumed similarity
The author points out that the objection assumes all dimensions are similar (tamāthul). Find the phrase 'على تماثل الأبعاد' and understand why this assumption is rejected.
This is the first key refutation: material and immaterial dimensions are not alike.
3
Follow the answer to the second objection
Read from 'وأما الجواب عن الوجه الثاني' to the end. The answer concerns two dimensions meeting in a body; the author distinguishes between a dimension inhering in the body and one that is self-subsistent, denying the impossibility of their interpenetration.
This shows how the opponent's claim of necessary impossibility is denied.

And what is said in refuting that place is a self-subsistent dimension (is that the dimension has necessitated) inasmuch as it is itself, i.e., by its essence, (subsistence in a substrate and need for it, (otherwise it would be independent) in itself (of it i.e., of the substrate and subsistence in it, for there is no middle between need and its absence but independence. So there are two dimensions, material and immaterial; one has penetrated into the other and they interpenetrated, and the impossibility of that) i.e., the impossibility of penetration and interpenetration between the material and immaterial dimension (is denied), and the claim of necessity is not [accepted].
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against place being a self-subsistent dimension and provides two answers. The objection argues: the dimension by its very essence requires a substrate (mahall) to subsist in; otherwise it would be independent. Since dimensions are observed to inhere in bodies (substrates), they cannot be self-subsistent. If all dimensions by essence require a substrate, then no dimension can be self-subsistent, so place cannot be a self-subsistent dimension. The author points out that this reasoning assumes similarity between material dimensions (which inhere in matter) and immaterial dimensions (which would be self-subsistent). This similarity is denied, as previously established. Then, regarding the second objection about two dimensions meeting in a body, the answer is that when the body's dimension penetrates into the place-dimension, there is not 'meeting in a body' in the sense of inherence; rather, one is material (inhering in body) and the other is immaterial (self-subsistent and not inhering). Their interpenetration is not necessarily impossible, contrary to the claim.

Key Terms
المحل (al-maḥall)
القيام (al-qiyām)
التماثل (al-tamāthul)
الأبعاد المجردة (al-abʿād al-mujarrada)
النفوذ (al-nufūdh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All dimensions have the same ontological status; they all require a substrate.
Correction: Material dimensions inhere in matter, but immaterial dimensions can be self-subsistent and do not require a substrate.

Checkpoint
On what key assumption does the objection against a self-subsistent dimension rest, according to the author?
A) That dimensions cannot interpenetrate
B) That material and immaterial dimensions are similar
C) That place is a surface
D) That dimensions require motion

Key Takeaway
The refutation of place as a self-subsistent dimension hinges on denying the similarity between material and immaterial dimensions, and on allowing interpenetration between them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever assume that all kinds of knowledge work the same way? This lesson shows that confusing material and immaterial dimensions leads to error. Are there areas of your faith where you have mistakenly treated spiritual realities as if they were material? What would it mean for you to truly accept that God is not like His creation in any way?
?
The lesson argues that some things are self-subsistent and independent by essence. You, as a created being, are entirely dependent on Him. Have you ever acted as if you were self-sufficient? Take a moment to list three things you do each day that reveal a sense of independence from Allah, and consider how you can replace them with conscious dependence.
?
When the objector's argument seemed plausible, Sanusi did not accept it without scrutiny. Are there beliefs you hold about your religion that you have never critically examined because they 'seem obvious'? Commit now to studying one such belief this week with the same rigor Sanusi applies here.

The Argument That Place Is Not Surface
Al-Jurjani presents the argument that if place were surface, paradoxes follow, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument against defining place as surface, the objection redefining motion as change of relation, and the reply that change of relation is caused by motion.

Core Idea
If place were surface, everything would be paradoxical. Motion is not substitution of places but change of relation.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a key argument from the Ash'ari tradition: if place (al-makān) were simply the surface surrounding a body, then absurd consequences follow. A stationary bird would be moving, and the moving moon would be at rest. The objection attempts to save the surface definition by redefining motion, but al-Jurjani counters that this redefinition only pushes the problem back. This lesson walks through the argument, the objection, and the final answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage from the start through the examples of the bird and the moon. Notice the claim that if place is surface, then motion is substitution of surfaces.
2
Understand the paradox
The argument shows that a stationary bird would have to be considered moving because its surrounding surfaces are constantly replaced. The moon, though moving, would be stationary because its surrounding surface doesn't change. This is presented as a reductio ad absurdum.
The bird example relies on the wind blowing surfaces past it.
3
Examine the objection
The objector denies the entailment by redefining motion as 'change of relation to fixed things' rather than substitution of surfaces. According to this, the bird is not moving, the moon is moving.
This redefinition seems to avoid the paradox.
4
Analyze the reply
Al-Jurjani replies that change of relation is itself caused by motion, so the definition of motion as substitution remains foundational. The objection merely shifts the issue.
The final exchange clarifies that the arguer only claims necessity, not actual motion.

Commentary
This passage is a combination of an argument, an objection, and an answer, along with explanatory glosses. It begins by stating the topic: place as continuous quantity. The main argument: if place is the surface, then a stationary thing would move and a moving thing would be stationary, which is absurd. The proof uses examples: a stationary bird in the air (interpreted as wind) constantly has its surrounding surfaces replaced, so if motion is substitution of places, it would be moving; the moon, while moving in its sphere, does not substitute its surrounding surface, so it would be stationary. Similarly, a person transported in a box has surfaces change around him. The objection denies the entailment by redefining motion: motion is not substitution of places but change of relation to fixed things. The bird does not change its relation to fixed things, so it remains stationary; the moon and box do change relation, so they move even without surface change. The response to this objection is that change of relation is caused by motion, so the definition of motion as substitution stands. The glosses explain the interpretation of 'air' (al-hawā') as wind to justify the feminization of 'blowing', and explain the box condition. A final exchange clarifies that the arguer only claims necessity, not actual motion.

Misconception: The argument claims that the bird is actually moving in reality.
Correction: The argument only shows that under the definition of place as surface, the bird would necessarily be considered moving, which is absurd. It does not claim actual motion.

Key Takeaway
The definition of place as surface leads to contradictions unless motion is redefined, but that redefinition itself depends on motion.

Motion as Continuous State and Substitution as Concomitant
Al-Jurjani refines the answer by distinguishing motion from substitution of places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between motion as a continuous state and substitution of places as a necessary concomitant, and why the presence of substitution does not prove motion.

Core Idea
Motion is a continuous state; substitution of places is only a concomitant, not its essence.

Al-Jurjani now presents the correct answer to the second aspect of the earlier objection. He defines motion as a continuous state inherent in the moving body throughout its trajectory, and clarifies that substitution of places is merely a necessary concomitant (lāzima) of that state, not its essence. This distinction is crucial: the existence of substitution does not automatically imply motion, as the bird example shows.

Guided Path
1
Read the core passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the definitions of motion and substitution of places.
Notice the terms 'ḥāla mustamirra' (continuous state) and 'lāzima' (concomitant).
2
Analyze the bird argument
The passage uses the stationary bird as an example. Ask yourself: Why does the fact that the bird's surface changes (substitution) not mean the bird is moving? The answer lies in the generality of the concomitant.
The concomitant (substitution) can arise from an external cause, not necessarily from motion.
3
Reflect on the logical relationship
Contrast the relationship of cause-effect (motion → substitution) with the relationship of essence-concomitant. Understand that the concomitant is more general, so its presence does not entail the specific cause.
This refines the definition of motion: it is not the same as substitution.

أنَّ
قوله : ( فإن استبدال الأمكنة إذا كان ناشئاً إلخ أراد أن ينشأ منه منشأ قريباً. فلا يرد شخصاً إذا دار على نفسه غير خارج عن مكانه فلا شك أنه نشأ منه تحرك الهواء لمشايعته فقد تبدل السطح المحيط به مع أنه ليس بمتحرك حركة أينية هكذا قيل . لكن إذا قيل : يلزم أن يكون . إنسان محفوف بكرباس مثلاً بحيث لم يبق من ظاهر بدنه جزء غير محفوف إذا سافر من بلد إلى بلد لزم أن يكون ساكناً لأنه لم ينقل من مكانه وهو باطن الكرباس وكذا الحوت في الماء الجاري إذا تحرك حركة مساوية لحركة الماء بحيث لم يفارق سطح الماء الملاصق لزم أن يكون ساكناً، وذلك سفسطة فلا مدفع له. وأقول : أما الجواب عن الثاني فظاهر لأن فرض تساوي حركة الحوت
134 The second observation - the ninth topic: place, and it is of continuous quantity. (And when that change is caused by motion, then its absence is caused by its absence) i.e., the absence of change, which is the permanence of relation, is caused by the absence of motion, which is rest. And when the existence of change is caused by the existence of motion and its absence by its absence, it is not the same as motion; and to that he pointed by his saying: (not that it is the reality of it), i.e., change is caused by motion, not that it is the reality of motion. So the denial falls, and it is established that motion is substitution of places, and the mentioned entailment (mulāzama) is correct. And it may be said: Indeed, that motion is an expression of change of relation is a support for the denial of the entailment, so your invalidation of it does not benefit you unless its equivalence to the denial is established. (And the truth) in the answer concerning the second aspect (is that motion) existing (according to them) externally (is a continuous state (ḥāla mustamirra) for the moving body) from the beginning of the distance to (its end), i.e., constant for it in every limit of its limits lying between the starting point and the end point. And it is known that this state is not the same as substitution of places which (is also called al-tawajjuh and al-wasaṭ) (and substitution of place is one of its concomitants) i.e., one of the concomitants of the state which is motion, not its essence. (So the proof is not complete, for the existence of the concomitant in the stationary bird does not necessitate the existence of the necessitating thing in it, namely motion, because it is possible that the concomitant is more general. For substitution of places, if it arises from the one situated in them, it is motion, and if it arises from other than it... Rather it is from this. His saying: (And it may be said, etc.) i.e., we do not accept the fall of the denial of entailment because it is an invalidation of the support, and it does not entail the removal of the denial unless it is equivalent to it. And here it is likewise, for it is possible that it is based on the fact that motion is an expression of substitution of places from the one situated in them. His saying: (For substitution of places, etc.) in al-Shifā': As for that he is not moving, it is because he is not the origin of substitution in it; and the moving in reality is the one in whom the origin of substitution is, and he is the one whose first perfection is for what is potential in him from himself, so that even if all other things were in their state, his state would change, I mean if the things surrounding him and accompanying him were fixed as no accident befell them, what befell him would change his relation in them. But this is not so. End quote. And by what we have transmitted, it becomes clear the refutation of what is in the new commentary on al-Tajrīd: that if it is said: Indeed, a person enveloped in a cloth, for example, such that no part of his outer body remains uncovered, if he travels from country to country, it would necessitate that he is stationary because he did not move from his place, which is the interior of the cloth. Likewise the one sitting in... That... His saying: (For substitution of places if it arises, etc.) he intended that it arises from him as a proximate origin. So it does not apply to a person who turns around himself without leaving his place, for there is no doubt that from him arises the motion of the air by following him, so the surface surrounding him changes while he is not moving with a motion of 'where'. Thus it is said. But if it is said: It necessitates that a person enveloped in a cloth, for example, such that no part of his outer body remains uncovered, if he travels from country to country, it would necessitate that he is stationary because he did not move from his place, which is the interior of the cloth. Likewise the fish in flowing water if it moves with a motion equal to the motion of the water such that it does not leave the adjacent water surface, it would necessitate that it is stationary, and that is sophistry, with no outlet. And I say: As for the answer to the second, it is apparent because the assumption of equality of the fish's motion...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion from the previous lesson. It begins by restating that change of relation is caused by motion, so absence of change (permanence of relation) is caused by absence of motion (rest). It clarifies that change is not identical to motion. Then it addresses a possible objection: if motion is defined as change of relation, that could support the denial of entailment, but this is rebutted unless equivalence is proven. The true answer (al-ḥaqq) is that motion is a continuous state (ḥāla mustamirra) in the moving body throughout its trajectory, and substitution of places is a necessary concomitant (lāzima) of motion, not its essence. Therefore, the presence of substitution (surface change) in the stationary bird does not imply motion, because the concomitant may be more general: substitution arising from the body itself is motion; substitution arising from an external cause is not. The gloss then cites al-Shifā' to explain the origin of substitution: a moving body is the origin of substitution, whereas the bird in the air is not. Further glosses discuss examples: a person turning around himself (motion without translation) and a man in cloth or fish in water (apparent stasis due to external motion). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is that the final answer refutes the objection by distinguishing motion from its concomitant.

الحالة المستمرة
استبدال الأمكنة
اللازمة
التوجه
الوسط
المبدأ
المنتهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substitution of places is identical to motion.
Correction: Substitution is a necessary concomitant of motion, not its essence. It can arise from external causes, as with a stationary bird in moving air.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani's refined answer, what is the relationship between motion and substitution of places?
A) Motion is identical to substitution.
B) Substitution is a necessary concomitant of motion.
C) Motion is a necessary concomitant of substitution.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Motion is a continuous state inherent in the moving body, while substitution of places is a necessary concomitant that can arise from other causes, so its presence does not prove motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you performed a religious act but felt no inner change. Were you 'substituting' action for genuine movement toward Allah? What will you do to ensure your external actions are rooted in an inner state?
?
The stationary bird shows that change in position can occur without true motion. In your spiritual life, are you being moved by external pressures (like people’s expectations) or by sincere intent? Identify one area where your 'movement' is merely external and commit to aligning your heart today.
?
Al-Jurjani distinguishes essence from concomitant. Are your acts of worship merely concomitants of habit, or do they spring from the essence of faith? What is one concrete step you will take this week to transform a ritual into a genuine act of devotion?

Proof from the Stone and Air
A concise argument from necessary knowledge that place is the void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from the stone and air supports the void theory of place.

Core Idea
The persistent void, not the vanishing surface, is true place.

The author presents a concrete example from our necessary knowledge. When a stone moves from one point in the air, the space it vacated remains even though air fills it, while the original boundary surface disappears. This shows that place is the void dimension, not the surface. We'll examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This argument supports the view that place is void. Recall that the debate is whether place is the inner surface of the containing body or the void dimension occupied by the body.
2
Examine the example
Consider a stone at rest in the air. When it moves, the void it occupied is filled by air, but the void itself does not cease. The surface that surrounded the stone, however, disappears entirely.
The example appeals to necessary knowledge—something we know with certainty through ordinary experience.
3
Analyze the inference
Since the void persists while the surface vanishes, the true place must be the void. If place were the surface, it would have vanished with the stone's movement.
This conclusion directly supports the void theory of place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يؤيد هذا المذهب) وهو كون المكان هو البعد أنا نعلم بالضرورة ) أنَّ المكان الذي خرج عنه الحجر) المسكن في الهواء (فملأه الهواء لم يبطل والسطح) الذي كان محيطاً بذلك الحجر ( قد بطل بالكلية، فدل على أنَّ المكان هو البعد الذي لم يبطل دون السطح الذي بطل
And among what supports this doctrine—that place is void—is that we know necessarily that the place from which a stone, still in the air, departed and was filled by air did not cease to exist, while the surface that was surrounding that stone ceased entirely. This indicates that place is the void that did not cease, not the surface that ceased.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This is a positive argument for the void theory. The author appeals to necessary knowledge: when a stone is at rest in air and then moves, the void space it occupied is filled by air, but that void space itself remains. Meanwhile, the surface that surrounded the stone disappears. Therefore, place cannot be that surface; it must be the void that persists.

Key Terms
البعد (void)
السطح (surface)
المعلوم بالضرورة (necessarily known)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disappearance of the surface implies the void also disappears.
Correction: The argument explicitly states that the void does not cease, while the surface does. The void persists even when filled by air, so it is the true place.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what happens to the place of a stone when it moves from air?
A) Place disappears entirely
B) Place remains as void
C) Place becomes the new surface
D) Place merges with the air

Key Takeaway
The void that persists when a stone moves is the true place, proving place is not the surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the void that remains after the stone moves, do you realize that most of what you think of as 'place' is actually just empty space that Allah sustains? How does that change your awareness of His constant preservation of creation?
?
The surface vanished, but the void persisted. What in your life have you clung to as a 'surface'—something outward and temporary—while neglecting the inner, persistent reality that Allah placed? Will you reexamine your priorities today?
?
If the true place is the void that remains, not the surface that disappears, then what is the true foundation of your existence? Is it the outward appearances that will vanish, or the deep, unseen reality that Allah knows? Commit to one specific area where you will stop chasing surfaces and instead anchor yourself in what truly remains.

The Stone and Air Argument: Place as Dimension
Presents a necessary knowledge argument that place is dimension, not surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a necessary knowledge argument based on the stone and air example proves that place is dimension, not surface.

Core Idea
Place is the dimension that remains, not the surface that vanishes.

In this passage, the author presents a concrete, everyday observation to settle a foundational question: what is 'place'? By watching what happens when a stone moves through air, we can know with certainty that place is a dimension (buʿd) and not a surface. This argument relies on necessary knowledge—knowledge so obvious it cannot be denied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Let the author's observation sink in without analysing yet.
Pay attention to the key terms: 'necessary knowledge', 'dimension', 'surface'.
2
Identify the two candidates for 'place'
The passage contrasts two possible definitions of place: dimension (buʿd) and surface (saṭḥ). Which one the argument favours will become clear.
Dimension refers to the three-dimensional volume; surface is the boundary.
3
Analyse the observation
The argument states two facts: when a stone moves out of air, the dimension (the space) remains and is filled by air, but the surface that was surrounding the stone disappears completely.
This is presented as something we know necessarily (bi-l-ḍarūra).
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since the dimension persists while the surface perishes, place must be the persistent dimension—not the perishable surface.
The conclusion follows directly from the observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يؤيد هذا المذهب وهو كون المكان هو البعد أنا نعلم بالضرورة أنَّ المكان الذي خرج عنه الحجر المسكن في الهواء فملأه الهواء لم يبطل والسطح الذي كان محيطاً بذلك الحجر قد بطل بالكلية، فدل على أنَّ المكان هو البعد الذي لم يبطل دون السطح الذي بطل
And among what supports this doctrine—that place is dimension—is that we know necessarily that the place from which the stone dwelling in the air departed, and which the air filled, did not become null, while the surface that was surrounding that stone became completely null. So this indicates that place is the dimension that did not become null, not the surface that became null.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents an argument based on necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) to support the view that place is dimension (al-buʿd). The argument: When a stone moves out of a volume of air, the space (makān) it occupied remains and is filled by the surrounding air—it does not cease to exist. However, the surface (al-saṭḥ) that surrounded the stone is completely gone once the stone leaves (since the surface was the stone's outer boundary, which disappears when the stone moves). Since the dimension persists while the surface perishes, place must be the persistent dimension, not the perishable surface. The argument relies on immediate, undeniable observation: we 'know necessarily' that the location (the dimension) continues to exist as a reference for the air that replaces the stone.

Key Terms
العلم الضروري
الحجر
الهواء
السطح
البعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument merely shows that 'place' refers to the void space, but the author intends it as a direct proof that place is a dimension (buʿd) as opposed to a surface.
Correction: The argument explicitly contrasts dimension and surface, concluding that place is the dimension that persists, not the surface that perishes. The void space itself is a dimension, so the argument directly supports the dimension view.

Checkpoint
What happens to the surface that surrounded the stone when the stone moves away?
A) It remains the same.
B) It becomes part of the air.
C) It becomes completely null.
D) It turns into dimension.

Key Takeaway
Place is the dimension that persists; the surface is merely a boundary that comes and goes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that the surface vanishes while the dimension endures. What 'surfaces' in your life—outward appearances, temporary roles, or material boundaries—are you clinging to as if they were real, while neglecting the enduring 'dimension' of your soul's orientation toward Allah? Are you willing to let those surfaces go to stand in the truth of your place before Him?
?
When the stone moves, the air immediately fills the void, and the dimension remains. In your heart, when something you relied on is removed, what do you allow to fill that space? Does it become filled with trust in Allah's plan, or with anxiety and worldly attachments? What does your reflex say about what you truly believe is real?
?
The author says we know necessarily that the place does not become null. Yet many today deny objective reality, claiming everything is subjective or illusory. Have you ever compromised your conviction in obvious truths—like the reality of change and permanence—because of intellectual fashion? Will you now affirm what your mind knows necessarily, even if it challenges modern assumptions?

The Necessity of a Continuous Surface Between Openings
Proof that a continuous surface must exist between openings, with discussion of angles and refutation of scattered parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that a continuous surface must exist between openings in a body, and why infinite actual division is impossible.

Core Idea
Between every two openings, a continuous surface is necessary — otherwise absurdity follows.

This passage from the Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a central argument in kalam physics: that a body's surface cannot be composed of scattered parts with gaps. The author, al-Jurjani, provides a rigorous proof that a continuous surface must exist between openings, and he refutes the possibility of infinite actual division. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the detailed commentary on each phrase within the passage.
2
Identify the main claim and the two alternatives
The passage states that a continuous surface is necessary between openings. If not, the surface would be scattered parts (absurd) or angles would exist requiring infinite division.
These two alternatives are the core of the proof.
3
Understand the 'angle' argument
The text considers angles between parts: if angles exist, we can insert smaller parts. Either the angles disappear (desired) or they become smaller, leading to a process that either ends or goes to infinity.
The process of inserting parts is key to showing that infinite actual division is impossible.
4
Grasp the distinction between actual and imaginary division
The commentary clarifies that 'actual division' produces externally distinct parts, while 'imaginary division' does not. Angles are only susceptible to imaginary infinite division, not actual.
This distinction is crucial for refuting the possibility of infinite actual division.
5
Consider the objection about hypothetical gaps
An objection is raised: what if a hypothetical gap remains that cannot be filled? The author responds that any real gap is external, and a hypothetical one does not affect the argument because air does not become trapped.
A further nuance allows extremely small gaps for subtle bodies like air.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لابد أن يكون بين كل منفذين أو بين منفذين فقط من منافذها سطح متصل هو نحن بصدده، وإلا كانت الصفحة عبارة عن أجزاء متفرقة متفاصلة في الحقيقة، وأنه باطل بالبديهة، وإما لوجود الزوايا بين أجزائها فنصنع فيها أجزاء أخرى فإن انتفت الزوايا حصل المطلوب، وإلا صارت أصغر مما كانت فنضع فيها أجزاء أخرى فإما أن تنتفي أو تذهب الزوايا في الانقسام بالفعل إلى غير النهاية والثاني باطل فتعين الأول وصارت. قوله : ( سطح متصل أي لا منفذ فيه سواء كان متصلاً في نفسه، أو بلصوق جزء بجزء من غير منفذ . قوله: (وإلا ) أي إن لم يكن بين منفذين من منافذها سطح متصل كانت الصفحة عبارة عن أجزاء لا تتجزأ متفرقة بينها منافذ إذ لو كانت من جهة من الجهات الثلاث منقسمة تحقق الصفحة المتصلة . قوله: (وأنه باطل بالبديهة) يعني بديهة العقل تشهد بأن الصفحة ليست أجزاء متفرقة فإن فيها حالة مانعة عن تفكك الأجزاء بخلاف الأجزاء المتفرقة . قوله : ( في الانقسام الفعلي إلى غير النهاية لأنه يبقى في كل مرتبة بعضها خاليا فينقسم إلى جزئين مملوء وخال والمراد بالانقسام الفعلي الانقسام الذي تتميز الأجزاء فيه في الخارج كاختلاف عرضين فإنه عده الشيخ من الانقسام الفعلي لا ما ينفك به الأجزاء في الخارج. وإنما قيد الانقسام بالفعلي لأن الزوايا قابلة للقسمة الوهمية إلى غير النهاية لكونها اسطحاً . على تقدير فرض تساوي وضع الأجزاء مما لا وجه له لأن وجود الزوايا لا يجامع التساوي قلت : فرض التساوي لا يستلزم تحققه في نفس الأمر، ومعنى قوله : فإن كانت ملساء أنها كانت ملساء في نفس الأمر كما هو كذلك على الفرض فلا محذور. قوله : ( فنضع فيها أجزاء أخرى هذا جار في المسام أيضاً. وإنما لم يذكره هناك لعدم الاحتياج إليه . فإن قلت: لم لا يجوز أن يبقى فرجة وهمية لا يمكن أن يوضع فيه جزء خارجي؟ قلت : الفرجة الواقعة في الخلال فرجة خارجية البتة ولو سلم فالفرجة الوهمية لا يقدح في المقصود إذ لا يحتقن فيه الهواء بحسب الخارج، كما لا يخفى فلا محذور. اللهم إلا أن يصار إلى أن ما أشرت إليه من الفرجة لغاية صغرها يدخل فيه الهواء للطاقته دون غيره من الأجسام التي لا تقبل التخلخل فتدبر
It is necessary that between every two openings, or between only two of its openings, there be a continuous surface which we are dealing with. Otherwise the surface would be composed of separate, actually distinct parts, and that is absurd by intuition. Or because of the existence of angles between its parts, we make other parts in them. If the angles disappear, the desired result is obtained. Otherwise, they become smaller than they were, so we place other parts in them. Then either they disappear or the angles go in actual division to infinity. The second is false, so the first is determined and it becomes [smooth]. His statement: 'a continuous surface' means no opening in it, whether it is continuous in itself or by adhesion of part to part without an opening. His statement: 'Otherwise' means if there is not between two of its openings a continuous surface, the surface would consist of indivisible parts scattered with openings between them, because if it were divisible in one of the three directions, a continuous surface would be realized. His statement: 'It is absurd by intuition' means the intuition of the mind testifies that the surface is not scattered parts, for it has a state preventing the disintegration of parts unlike scattered parts. His statement: 'Or because of the existence' is coordinated with his statement 'for lack of continuity'. His statement: 'If the angles disappear' means that the angles are like the indivisible parts. His statement: 'The desired result is obtained' is the equality of parts in position with continuity meaning absence of openings. His statement: 'Otherwise they become smaller' is in the case when the angles are larger than the indivisible parts. His statement: 'Then either they disappear' means that the angles after placing the first parts become equal to the parts. His statement: 'Or the angles go' meaning each one of them in actual division to infinity because there remains in each order a part of it empty, so it divides into two parts, filled and empty. By actual division is meant the division in which the parts are distinguished in the external world, like the difference of two accidents, which the Master counted as actual division, not the division that separates the parts in the external world. He only qualified division as actual because the angles are susceptible to imaginary division to infinity because they are surfaces. On the assumption of postulating equality of the position of the parts, there is no ground for it because the existence of angles does not accompany equality. I said: The postulate of equality does not necessitate its realization in actual fact. The meaning of his statement: 'if it were smooth' is that it was smooth in actual fact just as it is on the postulate, so there is no harm. His statement: 'We place other parts in it' also applies to the pores. But he did not mention it there because there is no need for it. If you say: Why is it not permissible that a hypothetical gap remains in which an external part cannot be placed? I say: The gap occurring in the interstices is an external gap absolutely. Even if granted, the hypothetical gap does not harm the intended purpose, since air does not become trapped in it externally, as is obvious, so there is no harm. Unless one goes to the extent that what I referred to, due to its extreme smallness, air enters into it because of its subtlety, unlike other bodies that do not accept rarefaction, so reflect.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that a continuous surface must exist between openings in a body, and it refutes the possibility of the surface being composed of scattered indivisible parts. The argument begins by stating the necessity of a continuous surface between every two openings; otherwise, the surface would actually be separate parts, which is intuitively false. The text then considers angles between parts: if angles exist, we can insert smaller parts to fill them. If the angles disappear, the desired continuity is achieved; if not, the angles become smaller, and we repeat the process. This leads to two possibilities: either the angles terminate (by becoming equal to the indivisible parts), or they go on dividing actually to infinity. The second is absurd, so the first must hold, resulting in a smooth surface. The commentary then explains each phrase: 'continuous surface' means no openings, and 'otherwise' implies that without continuity the surface would be indivisible parts, which is impossible. The 'absurd by intuition' is explained as the mind's immediate recognition that a surface is not scattered. The discussion of angles and the process of filling them is clarified: if angles are larger than indivisible parts, we place parts; if they become equal, the surface becomes smooth. The distinction between actual and imaginary division is crucial: actual division produces externally distinct parts (like two accidents), while imaginary division does not, and angles are susceptible only to imaginary infinite division. The text then addresses an objection about a hypothetical gap that cannot be filled: the commentator argues that any gap in the interstices is an external gap, and a hypothetical one does not harm the purpose because air does not become trapped. A further nuance allows that if the gap is extremely small, subtle air may enter, but not other bodies. The overall argument establishes that a continuous surface must exist and that infinite actual division is impossible.

منفذ
زاوية
جزء لا يتجزأ
انقسام فعلي
انقسام وهمي
مسام
فرجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that infinite division is possible for any magnitude, including surfaces.
Correction: The text clarifies that actual infinite division is impossible; only imaginary division can go to infinity. Angles are not subject to actual infinite division.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of not having a continuous surface between two openings?
A) The surface would be perfectly smooth.
B) The surface would consist of scattered indivisible parts, which is absurd.
C) The angles would disappear.
D) The surface would become curved.

Key Takeaway
A continuous surface between openings is rationally necessary; otherwise, the surface would reduce to scattered parts or infinite actual division, both of which are absurd.

Imam al-Razi's Argument on the Necessity of Small Straight Surfaces
Imam al-Razi argues that unevenness must resolve to small straight surfaces to avoid infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument that uneven surfaces must resolve to small straight surfaces to avoid infinite regress, and the two interpretations of his reasoning.

Core Idea
Uneven surfaces must terminate in small straight surfaces or infinite regress results.

This passage presents Imam al-Razi's sophisticated argument from his work al-Arba'in on the nature of surfaces. He shows that any unevenness in a surface must ultimately be composed of small straight surfaces, otherwise we fall into an impossibility. The argument involves two potential causes of unevenness and two interpretations of the infinite regress. Let us examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic and its literal meaning. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
The passage includes both the original matn and commentary.
2
Identify the two causes of unevenness
Al-Razi states that unevenness is either due to differences in height and lowness or due to pores in the surface. Note how he divides the possibilities.
3
Follow the argument for each cause
For the first cause, he argues that unevenness leads to small surfaces at angles, which must terminate at straight surfaces. For the second, he argues that pores require continuous surfaces between openings. See how each leads to the necessity of straight surfaces.
4
Understand the two interpretations
The commentary distinguishes between the infinite division of a single angle and the infinite number of angles existing actually in the surface. Grasp the difference.
This clarifies potential misunderstandings of the infinite regress.
5
Grasp the key terms
Understand what 'straight' (مستوية) and 'angle' (زاوية) mean in this context. The text clarifies that 'straight' means continuous without height or lowness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفحة ملساء. قال الإمام الرازي في الأربعين عدم الاستواء في السطح إما بسبب اختلاف أجزائه في الارتفاع والانخفاض أو بسبب حصول المسام فيه، أما الأول فلا بد أن يكون بسبب سطوح صغار يتصل بعضها ببعض لا على الاستقامة، بل على الزاوية ولا بد من الانتهاء إلى سطوح صغار مستوية وإلا لذهبت الزاوية إلى غير النهاية وهو محال . وأما حصول المسام في أجزاء السطح فإنه وإن جاز إلا أنه لا بد أن يحصل بين كل منفذين سطح متصل، وإلا لزم كون السطح مركباً من نقط متفرقة، وذلك محال فوجب القول بسطوح مستوية ) ولا يمتنع مماستها ، لمثلها وإلا لم يكن التماس. قوله : ( والثاني (باطل لأنه يستلزم في الجسم اشتمال المتناهي أعني الزاوية على أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل متميزة بعضها عن بعض في الخارج وإن لم تكن منفكة . قوله : (قال الإمام الرازي الفرق بين التوجيهين أن مبنى التوجيه الأول أن المراد بذهاب الزوايا إلى غير النهاية ذهاب كل واحدة منها في الانقسام إلى غير النهاية . ومبنى هذا التوجيه أنَّ المراد بذهاب جميع الزوايا في العدد إلى غير النهاية مع تحققها في الصفحة بالفعل. قوله : ( لا على الاستقامة) أي على وضع واحد سواء كانت مستقيمة أو مستديرة كما يدل عليه الإضراب . قوله : ( وهو محال ) إذ وجود الزوايا الغير المتناهية في السطح المتناهي محال بالضرورة . قوله: (مستوية) أي متصلة لا انخفاض ولا ارتفاع فيها .
The surface is smooth. Imam al-Razi said in al-Arba'in: Unevenness in a surface is either due to difference of its parts in height and lowness, or due to the occurrence of pores in it. As for the first, it must be due to small surfaces connected to each other not in a straight line, but at an angle, and it is necessary to end at small straight surfaces, otherwise the angle would go to infinity, which is impossible. As for the occurrence of pores in the parts of the surface, although permissible, there must occur between every two openings a continuous surface, otherwise the surface would be composed of scattered points, and that is impossible. So it is necessary to assert straight surfaces, and their contact with each other is not impossible, otherwise there would be no contact. His statement: 'The second is false because it necessitates that the finite body, namely the angle, contains actually infinite parts distinct from each other in external existence, even if not separated.' His statement: 'Imam al-Razi said: The difference between the two interpretations is that the foundation of the first interpretation is that by 'going of angles to infinity' is meant the going of each one of them in division to infinity. The foundation of this interpretation is that by 'going of all angles' is meant the going of their number to infinity with their actual existence in the surface.' His statement: 'Not in a straight line' means in one position, whether straight or curved, as indicated by the disjunction. His statement: 'It is impossible' because the existence of infinite angles in a finite surface is necessarily impossible. His statement: 'Straight' means connected, having no lowness or height in it.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's argument from his work al-Arba'in on the necessity of small straight surfaces to avoid infinite regress. The passage first identifies two causes of unevenness in a surface: differences in height/lowness or pores. For the first cause, al-Razi argues that unevenness due to height differences must arise from small surfaces connected at angles, and this series must terminate at small straight surfaces; otherwise, the angle would undergo infinite division, which is impossible. For pores, even though they are permissible, between every two openings there must be a continuous surface; otherwise, the surface would be composed of scattered points, which is impossible. Therefore, al-Razi concludes that straight surfaces must exist and they can be in contact. The commentator then clarifies al-Razi's words: the second option (infinite regress) is false because it would require a finite angle to contain actually infinite distinct parts. The commentator then distinguishes two interpretations of al-Razi's argument: the first concerns infinite division of a single angle (going to infinity in division), while the second concerns an infinite number of angles existing actually in the surface. The gloss on 'not in a straight line' explains that al-Razi means 'in one position' whether straight or curved, and the impossibility of infinite angles in a finite surface is asserted. Finally, 'straight' is glossed as 'connected, without height or lowness', i.e., smooth and continuous.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might equate 'straight' (مستوية) with geometrically straight lines, thinking it excludes curved surfaces.
Correction: The text clarifies that 'straight' here means 'without angle' and 'connected' — i.e., without height or lowness, so it includes curved surfaces as long as they are continuous and without angular breaks.

Checkpoint

B) Existence of angles and lack of continuity.
C) Infinite division and infinite number.
D) Presence of openings and absence of contact.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi proves that uneven surfaces must terminate in small straight surfaces to avoid infinite regress, and distinguishes two interpretations of the regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam al-Razi insists that infinite regress is impossible; there must be an end. Where in your own life—your habits, your repentance, your pursuit of truth—have you accepted an infinite deferral rather than seeking a decisive end? What one 'straight surface' can you establish today to break that cycle?
?
This lesson shows that between two openings there must be a continuous surface; otherwise the surface collapses into scattered points. Spiritually, are there gaps or 'pores' in your connection with Allah—moments of negligence or sin—that break the continuity? What small, consistent practice (like a daily dhikr) could serve as that continuous surface?
?
Al-Razi's argument forces us to confront the absurdity of actual infinity in a finite world. Consider your own mind: do you harbor an infinite chain of excuses for not changing? How does the logical impossibility of infinite regress challenge you to stop deferring and take actual, finite steps toward Allah?
