Redefining 'Straight': Al-Razi's Argument Refined
Commentator clarifies al-Razi's argument, redefines 'straight' as 'without angle'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator reinterprets al-Razi's term 'mustawi' to mean 'without angle', thereby resolving an objection about curved surfaces.

Core Idea
'Straight' means 'without angle', not 'geometrically straight'.

Imam al-Razi's argument for the possibility of contact between two surfaces faced an objection: why must we end at straight surfaces rather than curved ones? The commentator resolves this by showing that al-Razi's term 'mustawi' should be understood as 'without angle'—a meaning that includes curved surfaces. This subtle shift preserves the argument's force without requiring geometrically straight surfaces, as illustrated by the example of nested bowls and the void.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Look for the objection: why can't we end at small curved surfaces instead of straight ones?
The objection is in the phrase 'لم لا يجوز أن ينتهي إلى سطوح صغار منحنية'.
2
Find the commentator's reinterpretation
Notice how the commentator redefines 'mustawi' (straight) as 'what has no angle in it' (ما لا زاوية فيه). This is the key move.
The commentator says this is based on context (بقرينة السياق).
3
Understand the example of nested bowls
The commentator gives the example of two bowls (طاسين) nested and then separated, creating a void. This shows that a curved surface (the bowl) can allow contact without angles.
This demonstrates the possibility of a smooth (continuous) surface even if curved.
4
Analyze the response to the smooth surface objection
An objector says that if this works, the earlier demonstration of a smooth surface becomes superfluous. The commentator responds by distinguishing al-Razi's use of 'smooth surface' in different works.
The response emphasizes that al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhas did not use 'smooth surface' in the same sense.
5
Reflect on the correct meaning
The commentator concludes that with this reinterpretation, the desired result is established even if the surface is not geometrically straight. Reflect on why this is considered a correct meaning.
The passage ends with 'فتأمل' (so reflect), inviting deeper thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن التماس إلخ لا يخفى أنَّ إمكان التماس بين الصفحتين بديهي وما ذكره في بيانه مدخول فيه لأنه إن أريد به التماس بينهما لأجزاء لا تتجزأ بحيث لا يكون بينهما عن هذه قوله : (قال الإمام إلخ) الظاهر أنَّ المحذور فيما ذكره الإمام لعدم تناهي الزوايا بحسب العدد وفيما ذكره الشارح، أو لا انقسام زاوية واحدة بالفعل إلى غير النهاية . لكن في قوله : ولا بد من الانتهاء إلى سطوح مستوية بحث لم لا يجوز أن ينتهي إلى سطوح صغار منحنية ولا ينتهي إلى سطوح مستوية ولا تذهب الزوايا إلى غير النهاية قيل وكان الشارح، إنما عدل الطريقة لهذا الاختلال ويمكن أن يوجه كلام الإمام بعد تسليم أن السطح المنحني لا زاوية فيه بأنه أراد بالمستوي ما لا زاوية فيه بقرينة السياق لا ما يقابل الانحناء بالاستواء بهذا المعنى يحصل المطلوب، لأنا إذا فرضنا طاسين طبق وأدرج أحدهما في الآخر ثم رفع العالي دفعة يحصل الخلاء. فإن قلت : إذا حصل به المطلوب يلغو بيان إمكان الصفحة الملساء . قلت : الإمام لم يذكر في الملخص الصفحة الملساء بالمعنى المذكور هاهنا بل قال : إِنَّ سطحاً إذا لقي سطحاً آخر ثم ارتفع عنه دفعة واحدة إلخ . فلو كان ذكرها في الأربعين في عنوان البحث لجاز أن يقال : معنی آخر كلامه أنه إذا لم يتحقق الاستواء في السطح بسبب اتصال سطوحها على الزاوية فلا بد إلى سطوح صغار لا زاوية فيها. وبه ثبت المطلوب وإن لم يكن سطحاً ملساء وهذا معنی صحیح فتأمل.
His statement: 'Otherwise there would be no contact, etc.' It is not hidden that the possibility of contact between two surfaces is intuitive, and what he mentioned in its explanation is open to objection, because if by it is meant the contact between them by indivisible parts such that there is no [gap] between them... His statement: 'Imam al-Razi said, etc.' It is apparent that the problematic point in what Imam al-Razi mentioned is the non-finiteness of angles with respect to number, and in what the commentator mentioned is the non-finiteness of division of a single angle actually to infinity. But in his statement: 'And it is necessary to end at straight surfaces' there is a point: why is it not permissible to end at small curved surfaces without ending at straight surfaces, and the angles would not go to infinity? It was said: The commentator only deviated from the method because of this defect. And it is possible to direct the words of Imam al-Razi after acknowledging that a curved surface has no angle in it, by [saying] that he meant by 'straight' what has no angle in it, by context, not what is opposite of curvature in the sense of straightness. With this meaning, the desired result is obtained. For if we suppose two bowls nested, one inserted into the other, then the upper is raised suddenly, a void occurs. If you say: If the desired result is obtained by this, the demonstration of the possibility of the smooth surface becomes superfluous. I say: Imam al-Razi did not mention in al-Mulakhkhas the smooth surface in the sense mentioned here, but rather said: 'A surface when it meets another surface, then rises from it suddenly, etc.' So if he had mentioned it in al-Arba'in in the heading of the investigation, it could be said: Another meaning of his words is that if straightness is not realized in the surface due to the connection of its surfaces at an angle, then there must be small surfaces having no angle in them. By this the desired result is established, even if it is not a smooth surface. This is a correct meaning, so reflect.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on Imam al-Razi's argument, addressing an objection and providing a refined interpretation. The commentator first notes that the possibility of contact between two surfaces is intuitive, and the explanation given is flawed if it assumes contact by indivisible parts. Then, the commentator examines al-Razi's statement that 'it is necessary to end at straight surfaces' and raises a question: why can we not end at small curved surfaces that do not have angles, thus avoiding infinite regress? The commentator explains that this prompted the commentator (the author of the Sharh) to adjust the method. The key insight is that al-Razi's term 'mustawi' (straight) should be understood in context as meaning 'without angle' rather than 'geometrically straight' as opposed to curved. Since a curved surface has no angle, it qualifies as 'mustawi' in this sense. Consequently, the argument does not require geometrically straight surfaces, but only surfaces without angles, which includes curved ones. To illustrate, the commentator gives the example of two nested bowls: when separated suddenly, a void appears. This shows that a smooth (continuous) surface is possible even if curved. An objection is then raised: if this works, the earlier demonstration of the possibility of a smooth surface becomes unnecessary. The commentator responds by clarifying that al-Razi, in his work al-Mulakhkhas, did not use 'smooth surface' in the same sense as here; he only spoke of two surfaces meeting and then separating. Therefore, if in al-Arba'in he had meant 'smooth' in that sense, the interpretation would be that when straightness (i.e., absence of angles) is not achieved due to angular connections, we must resort to small surfaces with no angles. This establishes the necessary result, even if the overall surface is not 'smooth' in the sense of being perfectly level. The commentator urges reflection on this correct meaning.

Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'mustawi' (straight) is used in this context to mean 'without angle', which includes curved surfaces, thus avoiding the need for geometrically straight surfaces.

Al-Razi's argument required letting go of a rigid definition of 'straight'. What personal assumptions or expectations about how Allah should work in your life are you holding onto too tightly? Can you trust Him even when the path seems curved, knowing it still fulfills His purpose?

Proof that Ascent Requires Time
Proof that ascent requires time because it is motion over a divisible distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ascent of a page cannot be instantaneous but must occur over a divisible distance in time.

Core Idea
Ascent is a motion over a divisible distance, so it cannot happen in an instant.

This passage establishes the foundational principle that any motion, including the ascent of a page, must occur over a distance that can be divided into parts. Since cutting some parts precedes cutting all, motion requires time, not an instant. This directly implies that during ascent, air moves to prevent void.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a conditional argument (motion over divisible distance implies time) followed by a conclusion (air moves, no void).
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the key premise
The premise is that motion must be over a divisible distance. Why is this necessary? Because if the distance were indivisible, motion would be instantaneous, but that is impossible for a continuous process.
3
Connect premise to conclusion
Since the distance is divisible, cutting parts takes time. Therefore ascent takes time, and in that time air moves from the side to the middle, so no void arises.
This refutes the idea that ascent could be instantaneous and imply void.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument is a building block for later discussions. It shows that even a simple physical event like a page rising involves time and motion, aligning with a consistent view of the physical world.
Think about how this connects to broader kalām discussions on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنده في زمان ) إذ لا بد أن تكون الحركة على مسافة منقسمة وقطع بعضها مقدم على قطع جميعها فلا يتصور وقوع الحركة في آن بل في زمان ) وأنه ( أي الزمان ( منقسم إلى غير النهاية أي لا ينتهي في الانقسام إلى حد يقف عنده ( ففي زمان ارتفاعها يسلك الهواء من طرفها إلى الوسط ) فلا يلزم خلوه لا يقال : لا إذا رفعنا الصفحة حصل اللامماسة التي هي آنية عندهم ويلزم الخلو لأنَّ الحركة تدريجية فيصح الإلزام لأنا نقول : اللامماسة وإن كانت آنية كالمماسة إلا أنها لا تحصل إلا بعد
قوله : ( فإن الارتفاع حركة قال الشارح قُدس سره في حواشي شرح المطالع توضيح هذا المنع أنه إذا فرض زوال الانطباق على أي وجه يمكن أن يتصور فيه كانت العليا مرتفعة عن السافلة بينهما . إما أن يكون منقسماً في جهة الارتفاع أو لا والثاني محال وإلا لم يكن فاصلاً فتعين الأول فيكون مسافة بتجزئه لا يمكن قطعها إلا بحركة في زمان . فظهر أن الارتفاع لا يكون
دفعياً.
قوله: (ففي زمان (ارتفاعها فإن الانطباق مبدأ حركة الارتفاع وحركة السلوك وزمان الحركتين واحد وإن كان حركة الارتفاع متقدمة بالذات على حركة السلوك فالارتفاع والوصول إلى الوسط كلاهما زمانيان بمعنى أنهما حاصلان في أي آن يفرض في زمان تينك الحركتين ولا يتعين حصولهما في آن معين وكما أن قطع الهواء لأجزاء المسافة الصفحة الذي يحصل به الوصول إلى الوسط تدريجي كذلك قطع الصفحة لأجزاء المسافة الذي يحصل به الارتفاع تدريجي بلا تفاوت فتدبر فإنه مما زل فيه الأقدام وعرض دون فهمه الأوهام . قوله : (لا يقال الخ) يعني أنَّ الإلزام المذكور إنما لا يتم إذا لم يتعرض في للأمماسة واكتفى بأن الارتفاع دفعي أما لو تعرض لها وقيل : إذا رفعنا الصفحة حصل اللامماسة فهي متأخرة عن الرفع وإلا لكانت حاصلة حال المماسة فيجتمع المتقابلان وهي آنية فلا يمكن حصول حركة السلوك في ذلك الآن فتكون متأخرة عنها لامتناع السلوك حال المماسة للزوم الاستدلال التداخل فيكون الوسط في آن اللامماسة خالياً عن الهواء ليتم الإلزام. قوله : (لأنا نقول إلخ) حاصله أنَّ السلوك ليس متأخراً عن اللامماسة لأنها وإن كانت آنية التوسط وحاصل كلامه أنَّ حركة الارتفاع واقعة على مسافة منقسمة، وكذلك حركة الهواء من الطرف إلى الوسط، وقطع إحدى المسافتين مع قطع الأخرى زماناً، وإن كان حركة الارتفاع متقدمة بالذات على الحركة من الطرف إلى الوسط، وليس تقدمها بالزمان حتى يرد عليه أنَّ الحركة من الطرف إلى الوسط تكون على مسافة منقسمة فقطع النصف الأول مقدم على قطع النصف الآخر ففي زمان قطع النصف الأول يكون الوسط خاليا بالضرورة هكذا قيل : وفيه نظر لأنا سلمنا اتحاد القطعين المذكورين زماناً . لكن نقول زمان السلوك إلى الوسط إن كان عين زمان السلوك إلى الطرف لم ينطبق الزمان على الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة وإن كان بعده وإن لطف فقد خلا الوسط ولا يخفى أن هذا لا يندفع بادعاء اتحاد حركتي الارتفاع زماناً، فتأمل. الحركة، كما أنَّ المماسة حصلت في آن بعد الحركة وابتداء الحركة الموجبة اللامماسة في آن يوجد فيه المماسة فلا يوجد اللامماسة إلا في آن آخر ولا بد أنَّ الآنين زمان ففي ذلك الزمان يتحرك الجسم من الطرف إلى الوسط فلا إلزام.
With him in time (since motion must be over a divisible distance, and cutting some of it is prior to cutting all of it, so motion cannot occur in an instant but in time), and that (i.e., time) is divisible infinitely (i.e., it does not end in division at a limit at which it stops). So in the time of its ascent, the air travels from its side to the middle, so no void follows. It is not said: No, if we lift the page, contactlessness occurs, which is instantaneous according to them, and void follows because motion is gradual, so the objection is valid. For we say: contactlessness, even if it is instantaneous like contact, only occurs after motion. (Then the commentator explains: the statement that ascent is a motion, and he clarifies that the prevention is that if the loss of coincidence is assumed in any conceivable way, the upper body is raised above the lower, and between them either there is something divisible in the direction of ascent or not; the second is impossible, otherwise it would not be a separator, so the first is determined, and it becomes a divisible distance that cannot be traversed except by motion in time. So it appears that ascent is not instantaneous. (Then:) His statement: 'in the time of its ascent' – indeed coincidence is the beginning of the motion of ascent and the motion of traversal, and the time of the two motions is one, even if the motion of ascent is essentially prior to the motion of traversal. So ascent and reaching the middle are both temporal, meaning they occur in any instant assumed in the time of those two motions, and they are not determined to occur in a specific instant. And just as the air's cutting of the parts of the distance of the page, by which reaching the middle occurs, is gradual, likewise the page's cutting of the parts of the distance by which ascent occurs is gradual without difference. So ponder, for it is something in which feet have slipped and understanding of it has been difficult. (Then:) His statement: 'it is not said' etc. means that the aforementioned objection only fails if the questioner does not mention contactlessness and is satisfied that ascent is instantaneous. But if he mentions it and says: If we lift the page, contactlessness occurs, and it is subsequent to the lifting, otherwise it would have occurred during contact and the two opposites would meet, and it is instantaneous, so the motion of traversal cannot occur at that instant, so it is subsequent to it, because traversal is impossible during contact due to the necessary overlap, so the middle at the instant of contactlessness is void of air to complete the objection. (Then:) His statement: 'for we say' etc. Its conclusion is that traversal is not subsequent to contactlessness because although it is instantaneous... (Then:) The gist of his statement is that the motion of ascent occurs over a divisible distance, and likewise the motion of air from the side to the middle, and the cutting of one distance together with the cutting of the other is time, even if the motion of ascent is essentially prior to the motion from the side to the middle, and its priority is not by time such that it would be objected that motion from the side to the middle is over a divisible distance, so cutting the first half is prior to cutting the second half, so in the time of cutting the first half the middle is necessarily void. Thus it is said, and there is a consideration: for we have conceded that the two mentioned cuttings are simultaneous in time. But we say: the time of traversal to the middle, if it is identical to the time of traversal to the side, then time does not apply to the motion applied to the distance; and if it is after it, even subtly, then the middle becomes void. And it is not hidden that this is not repelled by claiming the unity of the two motions of ascent in time, so reflect. The motion, just as contact occurred in an instant after motion, and the beginning of motion that causes contactlessness is in an instant in which contact exists, so contactlessness only occurs in another instant, and the two instants must be time, and in that time the body moves from the side to the middle, so no objection.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the ascent (irtifāʿ) of a page cannot be instantaneous but takes time, and it answers an objection that would imply the existence of void (khalāʾ). The argument begins by stating that motion must occur over a divisible distance, and cutting some parts of the distance is prior to cutting all of it, so motion cannot happen in an instant but only in time. Therefore, in the time it takes for the upper page to rise, the air from its side travels to the middle, so no void occurs. The objection says: if we lift the page, contactlessness (lā-mumāssa) occurs, which is instantaneous; thus the middle would be void at that instant. The answer is that contactlessness only occurs after motion, and during that motion the air moves, so no void. The commentator then elaborates: the ascent is motion over a divisible distance, and the time of ascent and the time of the air's traversal are the same; both processes are gradual. He warns that this point is subtle and often misunderstood. The objection is examined in detail: if contactlessness is instantaneous, then the air's traversal cannot happen at the same instant, but it can happen in the time that includes that instant and the following motion. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the ascent of a page could be instantaneous (dafʿī) without requiring any time.
Correction: The passage shows that since ascent is motion over a divisible distance, it cannot be instantaneous; it necessarily takes time.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that motion cannot occur in an instant?
A) Because time is infinite.
B) Because motion must be over a divisible distance, and cutting parts takes time.
C) Because air moves during motion.
D) Because the void is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Ascent is a motion over a divisible distance, so it requires time, and during that time air moves to prevent void.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever reflect on the fact that even the simplest movements, like a bird taking flight, occur over time and space? How does this awareness of divine precision affect your experience of everyday events?
?
The argument shows that time is needed for motion to prevent void. In your own life, do you rush through actions without considering the space and time Allah has provided? How can you better appreciate the intervals that allow for order?
?
This proof demonstrates that creation follows rational laws. Are you quick to attribute events to 'coincidence' or do you see the consistent, just design? What change can you make today to live more consciously under Allah's wise order?

Refuting the Void Objection via Contactlessness
Objection that contactlessness causes void and its refutation by temporal ordering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that lifting the page creates instantaneous contactlessness and thus void is refuted by showing that contactlessness occurs after motion.

Core Idea
Contactlessness occurs only after motion, so void does not arise at the instant of lifting.

The objection claims that when the page is lifted, contactlessness (lā-mumāssa) occurs instantaneously, leaving the middle void. The answer is that contactlessness is not simultaneous with the start of lifting; it only happens after motion. Therefore, during the motion, air moves and fills the space, so no void occurs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states: if we lift the page, contactlessness occurs instantaneously. At that instant, the middle becomes void because the air has not moved yet. This would be a problem for the earlier proof that ascent takes time.
The objection is based on the assumption that contactlessness is instantaneous.
2
Analyze the answer
The answer says: contactlessness, even if instantaneous, only occurs after motion. That is, the page must first move a little before losing contact. So the instant of contactlessness is after the motion has begun, and during that motion air is already moving.
The key is that contactlessness is not at the very first instant of lifting.
3
See the consequence
Since contactlessness occurs after motion, there is no instant where the middle is void while the air is stationary. The air moves during the same time as the ascent, so void does not follow.
This preserves the conclusion of the earlier proof.
4
Reflect on the subtlety
The author warns that this point is subtle and often misunderstood. It requires careful thinking about the order of events: the beginning of motion, contact, and contactlessness.
This lesson sharpens understanding of temporal sequences in physical processes.

عنده في زمان ) إذ لا بد أن تكون الحركة على مسافة منقسمة وقطع بعضها مقدم على قطع جميعها فلا يتصور وقوع الحركة في آن بل في زمان ) وأنه ( أي الزمان ( منقسم إلى غير النهاية أي لا ينتهي في الانقسام إلى حد يقف عنده ( ففي زمان ارتفاعها يسلك الهواء من طرفها إلى الوسط ) فلا يلزم خلوه لا يقال : لا إذا رفعنا الصفحة حصل اللامماسة التي هي آنية عندهم ويلزم الخلو لأنَّ الحركة تدريجية فيصح الإلزام لأنا نقول : اللامماسة وإن كانت آنية كالمماسة إلا أنها لا تحصل إلا بعد
قوله : ( فإن الارتفاع حركة قال الشارح قُدس سره في حواشي شرح المطالع توضيح هذا المنع أنه إذا فرض زوال الانطباق على أي وجه يمكن أن يتصور فيه كانت العليا مرتفعة عن السافلة بينهما . إما أن يكون منقسماً في جهة الارتفاع أو لا والثاني محال وإلا لم يكن فاصلاً فتعين الأول فيكون مسافة بتجزئه لا يمكن قطعها إلا بحركة في زمان . فظهر أن الارتفاع لا يكون
دفعياً.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Second Proof for Void: Collision and Circularity
Proof that no void leads to collision and infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the existence of void, which argues that without void any motion would cause an endless chain of collisions, leading to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Without void, even a bug's motion would force the whole universe to collide endlessly.

This lesson covers the second proof for the possibility of void. The author demonstrates that if no empty space existed between bodies, any motion—however small—would set off an unending chain of pushes and collisions. This argument is a cornerstone of kalam physics and shows the logical necessity of void.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the technical terms like taṣādum (mutual collision) and tasalsul (serial chain).
The passage includes an objection and response, so pay attention to the structure.
2
Understand the concept of collision
The author defines collision (ṣadam) as pushing and mutual collision (taṣādum) as mutual pushing. The absence of void forces all bodies to push each other because every motion displaces another body with no empty space to receive it.
This is the core idea: without void, bodies cannot move without colliding.
3
Follow the serial chain argument
If the push reaches the edge of the universe and there is no void, the last body cannot move away, so it pushes the next, and so on endlessly. This results in a perpetual motion of all bodies, which is absurd.
The serial chain (tasalsul) is impossible because it leads to circularity or infinite regress.
4
Examine the objection and response
The objection concerns contactlessness (lā-mumāssa): if a body moves from the side to the middle, there must be a moment without contact. But any such moment is preceded by another, ad infinitum. The response is that the intellect judges generally that without contact the motion is impossible, even if we cannot specify a first moment.
This resolves the problem of infinite regress of contactless moments.
5
Reflect on the proof's conclusion
The proof concludes that void must exist because without it, any motion leads to an impossible chain. The overall argument is that void is a necessary condition for coherent motion.
Compare this with the first proof (if it were covered) to see two independent arguments.

قوله : ( تصادمت إلخ ) الصدم الدفع والتصادم التدافع فاللازم عن عدم الخلاء تدافع أجسام العالم كلها لأنه إذا انتهى الدفع إلى منتهى الطرف الآخر ولا يندفع ذلك لعدم المكان فيدفع ما بعده ثم، وثم إلى آخر الأجسام وهكذا إلى أن ينتفي لامتناع التداخل فمعنى قوله : ويتسلسل أنه يلزم عدم انقطاع حركات الأجسام . قوله : ففي ذلك الزمان يتحرك الجسم من الطرف إلى الوسط فلا إلزام فيه بحث لأن المسافة التي يتحقق فيها حركة الجسم إنما تتحقق في أن اللامماسة فما لم يحصل اللامماسة لم يتصور الحركة من الطرف إلى الوسط وإلا لزم التداخل والحركة الزمانية لا تتحقق في ذلك الآن بل بعده زماناً فيخلو الوسط في ذلك الزمان فإن قلت : كل لا مماسة تفرض فهي مسبوقة بلا مماسة أخرى لا إلى نهاية ولا يوجد لا مماسة الأولى حتى يقال : الحركة من الطرف إلى الوسط متأخرة عنها واقعة في زمان يكون آن تلك اللامماسة مبدأ ذلك الزمان فيلزم الخلو قلت : يكفي في إثبات المطلوب أنَّ العقل يجزم إجمالاً بأنه ما لم يحصل اللامماسة لم يتصور الحركة من الطرف إلى الوسط وإن لم يمكن أن يشير إلى لامماسة معينة بأنها متقدمة على تلك الحركة. قوله : أي) في هذا الجسم الثالث إرجاع الضمير إلى الجسم الثالث وحمل الأول على لنا هي
His statement: 'they would collide' etc. – collision (ṣadam) is pushing, and mutual collision (taṣādum) is mutual pushing. So the necessary consequence of the absence of void is the mutual collision of all bodies of the world, because if the push reaches the extreme of the other side and it is not pushed due to lack of place, it pushes what comes after, and then what comes after, up to the last bodies, and so on until it ceases due to the impossibility of interpenetration. So the meaning of his statement 'and it would be serial' is that there necessarily results non-interruption of motions of bodies. His statement: 'so in that time the body moves from the side to the middle, so no objection' – there is a discussion: because the distance in which the motion of the body occurs is only realized in that contactlessness, so as long as contactlessness has not occurred, motion from the side to the middle is inconceivable, otherwise interpenetration would be necessary, and temporal motion is not realized in that instant but after it in time, so the middle would be void in that time. If you say: every contactlessness assumed is preceded by another contactlessness ad infinitum, and there is no first contactlessness such that it is said: the motion from the side to the middle is subsequent to it and occurs in a time whose beginning is the instant of that contactlessness, so void would follow. I say: it suffices for proving the required that the intellect judges in general that unless contactlessness occurs, motion from the side to the middle is inconceivable, even if it cannot point to a specific contactlessness as being prior to that motion. His statement: 'i.e., regarding this third body' – the pronoun refers to the third body, and the first ('the first') is interpreted as 'for us' (or 'our').
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second of two proofs) for the possibility of void (khalāʾ). The author argues that if there were no void between bodies, then when any body moves even a tiny amount (like a bug), all bodies in the universe would be forced to collide and move in a chain (tasalsul), because each body must move into the place of another, which in turn must move, etc. This leads to an infinite regress or circularity, which is absurd. Therefore void must exist. The commentary then clarifies: the necessary consequence is mutual pushing (tadāfuʿ) of all bodies, because if the push reaches the end and there is no empty space, it cannot be repelled, so it pushes the next, and so on without end. The commentator also discusses a subtle objection about contactlessness (lā-mumāssa) and time, stating that the intellect can judge generally that without contactlessness the motion is impossible, even if a specific contactlessness cannot be identified. The final part refers to the third body in a circular argument.

Key Terms
الخلاء
التصادم
التدافع
التداخل
الدور
التسلسل
الحركة
الجسم
اللامماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof only applies to the motion of a bug; it is not a general argument about any motion.
Correction: The argument is general: any motion—even the smallest—would cause the chain of collisions if no void existed, because every motion displaces a body that must move into another body's place.

Checkpoint
What is the necessary consequence of the absence of void according to the second proof?
A) Bodies remain stationary.
B) All bodies would collide and move in a chain.
C) Void would still exist.
D) Interpenetration would occur.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that without void, any motion would lead to an infinite regress of collisions, proving void's necessity.

Reductio on Motion and Resistance
Al-Jurjani presents a reductio argument against the proportionality of resistance to time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument that disproves the simplistic notion that time differences in motion are solely proportional to resistance.

Core Idea
If resistance alone determined time, void and thin medium would yield equal time — a contradiction.

This is a classic reductio ad absurdum from theological physics. Al-Jurjani sets up a scenario with three motions to show that assuming time is only proportional to resistance leads to an absurd result. The argument is a careful logical exercise that challenges oversimplified causal connections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three motions
Read the passage and identify the three hypothetical motions: one in void, one in thick medium, one in thin medium. Note their respective times.
The first motion in void is given an hour; the second in thick medium is ten hours; the third in thin medium is also an hour.
2
Spot the proportionality assumption
Notice that the argument assumes time differences are entirely due to resistance (consistency of the medium). The thin medium has a tenth the resistance of the thick, so its time is a tenth (one hour).
This assumption is the key premise that will be reduced to absurdity.
3
Follow the reductio to contradiction
From the premises, it follows that motion in void (zero resistance) and motion in the thin medium (some resistance) both take one hour. This would mean resistance makes no difference to time — contradictory to intuition.
The contradiction shows the initial assumption (time proportional solely to resistance) is false.
4
Reflect on what is disproved
Understand that the argument does not disprove the existence of void, but rather disproves a simplistic proportionality between resistance and time. The relationship is more complex.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل أو قسرية أو طبيعية ( في مسافة خالية فهي في زمان لأن كل حركة إنما هي على مسافة منقسمة فقطع بعضها مقدم على قطع كلها فلا يتصور وقوعها في آن بل في زمان ( وليكن) ذلك الزمان ( ساعة و ) لنفرض حركة ( أخرى مثلها ) أي مساوية للأولى في القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك ومقدار المسافة ) في ملء ) غليظ القوام كالماء ( فتكون هذه الحركة الثانية في زمان أكثر من زمان الحركة الأولى ( ضرورة وجود المعاوق ) الذي يقتضي بطء الحركة المستلزم لطول الزمان ( ولتكن) الحركة الثانية ( في عشر ساعات ( مثلا ) ونفرض ( حركة ثالثة ( مثلها ) أي مثل الأولى أيضاً في القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك مقدار المسافة (في ملء آخر) رقيق كالهواء قوامه عشر قوام الملء (الأول) فتكون ( هذه الحركة الثالثة ( في ساعة أيضاً ) كالحركة الأولى ) لأن تفاوت الزمان ) في الحركات إنما هو ( بحسب تفاوت المعاوق ) فكلما كان المعاوق أكثر ، كانت الحركة أبطأ والزمان ،أطول وكلما كان أقل كانت الحركة أسرع والزمان أقصر (وهو) أي المعاوق (القوام) يعني قوام الجسم المالئ للمسافة الذي يخرقه المتحرك ) فإن كان المعاوق عشراً ) من معاوق آخر كالملء الثاني بالقياس إلى الملء الأول ( كان الزمان ( الواقع بإزاء المعاوق الأقل ( عشراً ) أيضاً من زمان المعاوق الأكثر ، كما في مثالنا هذا ( وإذا ثبت هذه المقدمات لزم أن تكون الحركة في الخلاء مع أنه لا معاوق ( عن الحركة في هذه المسافة ) والحركة في الملء الرقيق وهو معاوق ( عن الحركة فيه لاحتياج المتحرك إلى خرقه ودفعه ( كلاهما في ساعة ) كما ذكرناه ( فيكون وجود المعاوق وعدمه سواء ) حيث لم يتفاوت بهما حال الحركة في السرعة والبطء وإلا اختلف الزمان أيضاً ( هذا خلف ) لأن البديهة تشهد بأن الحركة مع المعاوقة وإن كانت قليلة تكون أبطأ أكثر زماناً من الحركة التي لا معاوقة معها أصلاً )
The second observatory, the ninth topic: place, which is of continuous quantity, whether forced or natural (in an empty distance, it is in time because every motion is only over a divisible distance, so the cutting of some of it precedes the cutting of all of it, so it is not conceivable that it occurs in an instant but in time. (Let) that time (be an hour and) let us suppose another motion (similar to it) i.e. equal to the first in motive force, moving body, and distance magnitude (in a plenum) thick in consistency like water (so this second motion will be in a time greater than the time of the first motion) necessarily due to the existence of resistance which necessitates slowness of motion entailing length of time. (Let) the second motion (be in ten hours, for example) and we suppose a third motion (similar to it) i.e. also like the first in motive force, moving body, and distance magnitude (in another plenum) thin like air whose consistency is a tenth of the consistency of the (first) plenum (so this third motion will be) in an hour also (like the first motion) because the difference in time in motions is only according to the difference of resistance: whenever resistance is greater, motion is slower and time longer; whenever it is less, motion is faster and time shorter. (And it) i.e. the resistance (is consistency) meaning the consistency of the body filling the distance which the moving object penetrates. So if the resistance is a tenth of another resistance, like the second plenum relative to the first plenum (the time corresponding to the lesser resistance will be a tenth) also of the time of the greater resistance, as in this example of ours. (And when these premises are established, it follows that the motion in the void, despite having no resistance to motion through this distance, and the motion in the thin plenum which is a resistance to motion through it due to the need of the moving object to tear and push it, are both in an hour) as we mentioned. (Thus the existence of resistance and its non-existence are equal) since the state of motion in speed and slowness does not differ between them, otherwise time would also differ. (This is a contradiction) because intuition testifies that motion with resistance, even if little, is slower and more time-consuming than motion with no resistance at all.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument. It begins by defining place as a continuous quantity and then sets up three hypothetical motions: one in void, one in a thick medium, and one in a thin medium. The argument assumes that time differences in motion are entirely due to resistance (consistency of the medium). Given that proportion, motion in void (no resistance) and motion in a medium with a tenth of the thick medium's resistance should take the same time (one hour). This would mean resistance makes no difference to time, which contradicts common sense. The conclusion is that the assumption (that time differences are solely proportional to resistance) is false.

Key Terms
خلاء (void)
ملء (plenum)
معاوق (resistance)
قوام (consistency)
زمان (time)
حركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that void cannot exist because it leads to contradictions.
Correction: The argument does not disprove void; it disproves the assumption that time differences are solely proportional to resistance. The contradiction arises from the proportionality, not from the existence of void.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would be the time of motion in a void if the time difference were solely due to resistance?
A) Zero time (instant)
B) Same time as in the thin medium
C) Twice the time of the thick medium
D) Half the time of the thick medium

Key Takeaway
The reductio shows that the assumption that time differences stem solely from resistance leads to an absurdity — thus the relationship between resistance and time is more complex.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The reductio shows that a seemingly obvious proportionality assumption leads to an absurd contradiction. Think of a belief or assumption you hold about your own spiritual life (e.g., 'if I sin, Allah is displeased with me in a fixed way') that might be an oversimplification. How can you use the method of logical scrutiny, as demonstrated here, to test that assumption against the Quran and Sunnah? What would it mean for you to adjust it if found wanting?
?
Al-Jurjani relied on an undeniable intuition: motion with resistance is slower than without. In matters of faith, what undeniable truths (like the oneness of Allah or the reality of the afterlife) must you never allow any rationalization to compromise? How will you guard your heart against accepting a clever line of reasoning that contradicts these foundational intuitions?

Abu al-Barakat's Answer: Motion Itself Requires Time
Al-Jurjani expounds Abu al-Barakat's response that motion has intrinsic temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Barakat's critique that motion intrinsically necessitates time, and how this preserves proportionality without leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
Motion itself demands time; resistance only adds extra.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents Abu al-Barakat's powerful rebuttal to an earlier argument. He shows that the argument's premise—that time differences are solely due to resistance—is false because motion by its very nature unfolds in time. This insight resolves the apparent absurdity while maintaining the proportional relationship between resistance and time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time to grasp each part.
2
Identify the key premise and the critique
Notice that Abu al-Barakat begins by stating the premise of the original argument. Then he points out that this premise is only valid if motion does not intrinsically require time. His critique is that motion does require time essentially.
The phrase 'إنما يصح لو لم تكن الحركة لذاتها تقتضي زماناً' is crucial.
3
Understand the numerical example
Follow the example: motion in void takes 1 hour (intrinsic), motion in thick plenum takes 1 hour + 9 hours (resistance), motion in thin plenum takes 1 hour + 0.9 hours. This shows that the intrinsic time is constant, and only the added time varies.
Work through the arithmetic to see why equality does not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب ) عن هذا الوجه كما ذكره أبو البركات ( أنه مبني على مقدمة واحدة وهي أن تفاوت زماني الحركتين الأخيرتين إنما هو (بحسب تفاوت المعاوقين حتى يجب أنه لما كان المعاوق عشراً كان الزمان أيضاً عشراً . ( وذلك ) أعني كون تفاوت الزمانين كتفاوت المعاوقين ( إنما يصح لو لم تكن الحركة لذاتها ) من حيث هي هي ( تقتضي زماناً واقعاً بإزائها لكنها تقتضيه لأن الحركة لا تتحقق إلا على مسافة منقسمة يكون قطع نصفها الأول مقدماً على قطع النصف الآخر ، فلا يتصور وجود الحركة من حيث هي إلا في زمان وذلك الزمان الذي تقتضيه ماهيتها يكون محفوظاً في جميع الحركات وما زاد عليه يكون بحسب المعاوق وحينئذ لا تتم تلك المقدمة التي بنى عليها الدليل وإليه أشار بقوله : ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم تكن الحركة غير مقتضية لذاتها زماناً بل كانت مقتضية له ( كان الزائد على ذلك القدر) الذي تقتضيه ماهية الحركة من الزمان ( هو الواقع بإزاء المعاوق ) لا جميع الزمان ( فيكون تفاوت ذلك القدر) الزائد (بحسب تفاوت المعاوقين) في المثال المذكور ( لا أصل (الحركة أي لا زمان أصلها فإنه لا ينفاوت بتفاوت المعاوقين بل هو محفوظ في الحركات كلها لأن مقتضى ذات الشيء لا يختلف ولا يتخلف عنه (ففي المثال المفروض وهو الحركة في الملء الغليظ ( تكون ساعة لأصل الحركة لا تعلق لها بالمعاوق أصلاً كما في الحركة الواقعة في الخلاء فإن ساعتها بإزاء الحركة دون المعاوق ( وتسع ساعات بإزاء المعاوق ) الذي هو الملء الغليظ فهذه التسع تتفاوت بحسب تفاوت المعاوق ) وتكون حصة القوام الرقيق ) من هذه التسع ( عشرا منها وهو عشر تسع ساعات وهي ( أي عشر تسع ساعات ( تسعة أعشار ساعة ) واحدة ( فيضاف ) تسعة الأعشار ) إلى ما تقتضيه الحركة لذاتها وهي ساعة فتكون حركته في الملء الرقيق ( في ساعة وتسعة أعشارها فلا يلزم المساواة ) بين وجود المعاوق وعدمه
The answer to this point, as mentioned by Abu al-Barakat, is that it is based on one premise: that the difference of the times of the last two motions is only according to the difference of the resistances, so that when the resistance is a tenth, the time is also a tenth. But this (i.e., the time difference being like the resistance difference) is only valid if motion in itself (as such) does not necessitate a time corresponding to it. However, it does necessitate it, because motion is only realized over a divisible distance, the cutting of its first half being prior to the cutting of the other half. So the existence of motion as such is not conceivable except in time, and that time which its essence necessitates is preserved in all motions, and what is added to it is according to the resistance. In that case, the premise on which the proof is built is not established. And he referred to this by his saying: 'Otherwise' – i.e., if motion were not not necessitating time in itself, but rather necessitating it – 'the excess over that amount' of time which the essence of motion necessitates 'is what corresponds to the resistance', not all the time. 'So the difference in that amount' (the excess) 'is according to the difference of the resistances' in the mentioned example, 'not the origin' of the motion, i.e., not the time of its origin, for it does not differ with the difference of resistances but is preserved in all motions, because what the essence of a thing necessitates does not vary and does not fail to accompany it. 'Thus in the supposed example' – motion in the thick plenum – 'one hour is for the origin of motion, having no connection with the resistance at all, just as in the motion occurring in the void, for its hour corresponds to the motion, not the resistance. And nine hours correspond to the resistance' which is the thick plenum. So these nine differ according to the difference of resistance. 'And the share of the thin consistency' of these nine 'is a tenth of them, which is a tenth of nine hours, and it' (i.e., a tenth of nine hours) 'is nine-tenths of an hour. So they are added' (the nine-tenths) 'to what motion necessitates in itself, which is an hour, so its motion in the thin plenum is in one hour and nine-tenths of it. Thus equality does not follow' between the existence of resistance and its absence.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the answer of Abu al-Barakat. He points out that the previous argument (which led to absurdity) is based on the premise that time differences are solely due to resistance differences. This premise is valid only if motion in itself does not require any time. But in fact, motion as such requires time because it involves traversing a divisible distance (one half before the other). This intrinsic time is constant for all motions over the same distance, regardless of resistance. The resistance only adds extra time. In the example, the motion in the void has an intrinsic time of one hour (with no resistance). The motion in the thick plenum also has the same intrinsic hour, plus nine hours due to resistance. The motion in the thin plenum (with a tenth the resistance) has the same intrinsic hour plus nine-tenths of an hour (since nine hours is the resistance time for the thick, and the thin resistance is a tenth, so its resistance time is 0.9 hours). So the total time for the thin plenum is 1.9 hours, not equal to the void's 1 hour. Hence, the absurdity (equality) is avoided.

Key Terms
أبو البركات (Abu al-Barakat)
حركة لذاتها (motion in itself)
ماهية الحركة (essence of motion)
زائد على ذلك القدر (excess over that amount)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu al-Barakat claims that motion does not require time by its nature.
Correction: Abu al-Barakat actually argues that motion does necessitate time intrinsically; his critique is that the original argument mistakenly assumed otherwise.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Barakat's answer, why does motion in the thin plenum take more than one hour?
A) The thin plenum has more resistance than the void
B) The intrinsic time of motion is unchanged, but resistance adds extra time
C) The thin plenum reduces the intrinsic time
D) The motion is slower because of the moving body

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Barakat shows that motion's essence necessitates a fixed time, so only the added time from resistance varies, preserving proportionality without absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed external circumstances (like lack of time or distractions) for slow progress in your spiritual journey, without acknowledging that the very nature of drawing closer to Allah requires a fixed, non-negotiable time of sincere effort? What would change if you accepted that intrinsic time as necessary rather than wasting it on impatience?
?
In your daily worship, are you trying to 'cut corners' to save time, thinking that the goal is efficiency? But this lesson shows that motion in itself demands a certain duration. Could it be that by rushing through acts of devotion, you are missing the essential time your soul needs to truly move toward Allah? What would it mean to honor that intrinsic time today?
?
Abu al-Barakat distinguished between the constant intrinsic time and the variable added time from resistance. In your life, what are the 'resistances' (sins, attachments, laziness) that you allow to add unnecessary delay to your spiritual growth? Are you willing to remove them to let your soul's motion proceed at its essential pace?

The Continuous Nature of Time and Motion
The author argues for the continuity of time and motion from the denial of the atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in classical kalam, time and motion are considered continuous and infinitely divisible by supposition, and how this follows from denying the atom (the indivisible part).

Core Idea
Time and motion are continuous streams, not discrete units — division is only in the mind.

This passage tackles a foundational issue in natural philosophy: the nature of time and motion. The author argues that they are continuous, not made of actual indivisible moments or steps. Instead, any division we make is purely hypothetical. This connects to the denial of the atom (the indivisible part) and has profound implications for how we understand change and causality. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the technical terms: الزمان (time), الحركة (motion), المسافة (distance).
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding everything yet.
2
Focus on the core claims about time and motion
Identify the claims: time is one continuous thing with no actual division; motion is continuous by its conformity to distance and time. They are only divided by supposition.
3
Trace the argument from denying the indivisible part
See how the author says these are 'necessary rulings from the denial of the part which has no part'. If you accept that there is no smallest unit, then any part of time is still time, containing a part of motion, and so on infinitely.
This is the key logical link.
4
Understand the analogy with Democritean bodies
The author compares this to atoms (Democritean bodies) which are divisible in imagination but not in actual separation. Time-parts are purely hypothetical, not actual, so the analogy of similars doesn't force actual division.
This nuance prevents a counterargument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل الزمان ونحن نقول : الزمان عندهم متصل واحد لا انقسام فيه بالفعل، وإنما ينقسم بالفرض إلى أجزاء هي أزمنة انقساماً لا يقف عند حد وكذلك الحركة متصلة بانطباقها على المسافة والزمان ولا ينقسم إلا إلى أجزاء هي حركات كما أن المسافة لا تنقسم إلا إلى أجزاء منقسمة كل واحد منها مسافة وهذه أحكام لازمة من نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فإن سلمت لزمك الاعتراف بأنَّ زمان أية حركة فرضت من الحركات إذا جزء على أي وجه أريد كان كل جزء منه زماناً وكان ظرفاً لجزء من أجزاء تلك الحركة وذلك الجزء أيضاً حركة واقعة في جزء من أجزاء المسافة وهو في نفسه أيضاً مسافة، فيظهر من ذلك أن ماهية الحركة من حيث هي هي صالحة، لأن الوهمية لجواز القسمة الانفكاكية إنما أثبتوا في الأجسام الديمقراطية لكونها متفقة بالماهية قابلة للقسمة الوهمية دون الانفكاكية بأن حكم الأمثال واحد فيجوز على كل منها ما يجوز على الآخر نظراً إلى الماهية وأجزاء الزمان ليست موجودة بالفعل بل فرضية محضة فلا يمكن أن يقال هاهنا : إن حكم الأمثال واحد على أنه يجوز أن يكون تشخصها مانعاً من قبول القسمة الانفكاكية.
The Second Observatory - The Ninth Purpose: Place, which is of continuous quantity. Time, and we say: Time according to them is one continuous thing, having no actual division; it is only divided by supposition into parts that are times, division without limit. Similarly, motion is continuous by its conformity to distance and time, and is not divided except into parts that are motions, just as distance is not divided except into divisible parts each one of which is a distance. And these are necessary rulings from the denial of the part which has no part. For if you admit it, you must acknowledge that the time of any motion supposed, if divided in any way intended, each part of it is a time and is a container for a part of the parts of that motion, and that part is also a motion occurring in a part of the parts of the distance, and it is in itself a distance. So it appears from that that the essence of motion, insofar as it is, is suitable [?] because the imaginary [?] for the permissibility of disjunctive division, they only established it in Democritean bodies because they are accordant in essence, capable of imaginary division without disjunctive division, because the judgment of similars is one, so whatever is permissible for one is permissible for the other with respect to the essence. And the parts of time are not actually existent but purely hypothetical, so it cannot be said here: the judgment of similars is one, while it may be that their individuation prevents acceptance of disjunctive division.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an argument (istidlal) about the nature of time and motion. The author states that time is continuous and only divisible by supposition (bi-al-fard). Similarly, motion is continuous by its conformity (inṭibāq) to distance and time. These are necessary consequences (aḥkām lāzima) of denying the indivisible part (nafy al-juzʾ alladhī lā yatajazzaʾ). If one admits that denial, then one must accept that any part of the time of a motion is itself a time and contains a part of that motion, and so on infinitely. This shows that the essence (māhiyya) of motion does not inherently require a specific time or distance; rather, it is suitable for any part. The author then compares this to Democritean bodies (al-ajsām al-dīmuqrāṭiyya), which are divisible in imagination (al-qisma al-wahmiyya) but not in disjunctive actuality (al-infikākiyya). Since the parts of time are only hypothetical (fardiyya maḥḍa), the analogy of similars (ḥukm al-amthāl wāḥid) does not apply because individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) may prevent disjunctive division.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That time and motion are composed of actual indivisible units (atoms of time and motion).
Correction: The passage argues that time and motion are continuous and only divisible by supposition; they have no actual parts. Denying the indivisible part leads to infinite divisibility, not atomism.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a necessary consequence of denying the indivisible part?
A) Time and motion are composed of actual indivisible moments.
B) Time and motion are continuous and infinitely divisible by supposition.
C) Motion requires a specific time and distance.
D) Distance is not required for motion.

Key Takeaway
The continuity of time and motion entails that they are infinitely divisible by supposition, a necessary consequence of denying the existence of an indivisible part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider that time, according to this passage, is one continuous whole without actual division. In your daily life, you segment time into hours, minutes, tasks. How might the knowledge that these divisions are only mental constructs change the way you experience the present moment? What would it mean to live without mentally breaking time into pieces?
?
The passage argues that motion is continuous and divisible only by supposition. Think of a single action you perform repeatedly, like speaking or walking. How does the idea that each 'part' of that action is itself a full motion and time affect your sense of responsibility for even the smallest fragment of your behavior? Are you aware that every instant of your movement is a complete act in itself?

Objection and Response: Fast Motion in Imaginary Time
Objector argues dilemma; author denies implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that fast motion in an imaginary time leads to contradiction and the author's response that the assumption itself is impossible.

Core Idea
Time is continuous; imaginary parts cannot contain real motion.

This passage presents a classic objection in kalām about motion and time. The objector argues that if a slow mover moves in an indivisible (imaginary) time, a fast mover would also move there, leading to a contradiction. The author counters by denying the implication and offering a deeper analysis. Understanding this exchange clarifies the relationship between motion, time, and the nature of divisible vs. indivisible parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and the use of examples like the sphere of fixed stars.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objector presents two alternatives for fast and slow movers in the same time. Identify each and see why one is rejected.
The first horn is equality of motion (impossible), the second is faster covering more distance (leads to contradiction).
3
Understand the response and denial
The author responds by denying the implication that the scenario is even possible. He introduces the caveat that the motion in that time must be the fastest motion.
This step is key: the objection's premise is shown to be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فكيف تقع الحركة المحققة إلخ ) وما قيل : إن متحركاً بطيئاً كفلك الثوابت، مثلاً إذا تحرك في زمان لا ينقسم إلا وهماً، فلا شك أنَّ المتحرك السريع كفلك الأفلاك متحرك فيه أيضاً فأما أن تتساوى الحركتان في السرعة والبطء ومقدار المسافة المقطوعة وهو بين البطلان أو يقطع السريع بسرعته أكثر مما قطعه البطيء فلا محالة يقع مقدار ما قطعه البطيء في جزء وهمي من الزمان فقد وقع الحركة المحققة في جزء وهمي من الزمان فوهم لأن الزمان متصل واحد لا جزء فيه بالفعل، وكذلك الحركة والانقسام لهما إنما هو في الوهم فالجزء الوهمي للحركة وقع في الجزء الوهمي للزمان على أن فرض وقوع حركة فلك الثوابت في جزء لا ينقسم فعلاً، بل وهماً محال . لأنه يستلزم أن يكون تلك الحركة أسرع الحركات فالحركة الواقعة في ذلك الجزء لا تكون إلا حركة المحدد . قلت : مراد المصنف منع ذلك الاستلزام فلا بد من إثباته وهاهنا بحث آخر وهو أن متحركاً بطيئاً كفلك الثوابت مثلاً إذا تحرك في زمان لا ينقسم إلا وهماً فلا شك أن المتحرك السريع مثلاً كفلك الافلاك يتحرك فيه أيضاً فإما أن يتساوي الحركتان في السرعة والبطء ومقدار المسافة المقطوعة وهو بين البطلان أو يقطع السريع بسرعته أكثر مما قطعه البطيء فلا محالة يقع قطع مقدار ما قطعه البطيء في جزء وهمي من الزمان فقد وقع الحركة المحققة في جزء وهمي من الزمان اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنَّ الحركة التي تقع في ذلك الزمان، وتقطع مسافة ما لا تكون إلا أسرع الحركات، ولا يوجد ما هو أسرع منها حتى يلزم قطعه مقدار تلك المسافة في جزء ذلك الزمان فليتأمل.
His saying: 'How does verified motion occur, etc.' And what was said: A slow mover, like the sphere of the fixed stars, if it moves in a time that is not divided except in estimation, there is no doubt that the fast mover, like the sphere of spheres, also moves in it. Either both motions equal in speed and slowness and the amount of distance traversed—which is [obviously] false—or the fast mover cuts with its speed more than what the slow mover cut. Then inevitably the amount that the slow mover cut occurs in an imaginary part of time. So verified motion has occurred in an imaginary part of time. This is fallacious, because time is one continuous thing having no actual part. Likewise motion and division for them are only in estimation, so the imaginary part of motion occurs in the imaginary part of time. Moreover, supposing the motion of the sphere of the fixed stars occurring in a part that is not actually indivisible, but only in estimation, is impossible, because it would necessitate that that motion be the fastest of motions. So the motion occurring in that part can only be the motion of the outermost sphere.' I said: The author intends to deny that implication, so it must be proven. And here is another investigation: A slow mover, like the sphere of the fixed stars, if it moves in a time that is divided only in estimation, then the fast mover, e.g., the sphere of spheres, also moves in it. Either the two motions are equal in speed and slowness and the amount of distance—which is false—or the fast mover cuts more than the slow, then inevitably the cutting of the amount cut by the slow occurs in an imaginary part of time. So verified motion occurs in an imaginary part of time. Except that it may be said: The motion that occurs in that time and cuts some distance must be the fastest motion, and there is nothing faster than it so that it would necessitate cutting that amount of distance in a part of that time. So let it be considered.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its response. The objector quotes the author's phrase 'How does verified motion occur...' and then presents an argument: If a slow mover (e.g., the sphere of fixed stars) moves in a time that is only indivisible in estimation, then a fast mover (e.g., the sphere of spheres) also moves in that same time. Either the two motions are equal (false) or the fast mover covers more distance. In the latter case, the slow mover's distance would be covered in an imaginary part of time, so a real motion occurs in an imaginary part—a problem. The objector then says this is fallacious because time is continuous and has no actual parts; division is only mental. He adds that supposing the slow mover's motion in an indivisible part (even in estimation) is impossible because it would require that motion to be the fastest, so only the motion of the outermost sphere (ḥarakat al-muḥaddad) could be in such a part. The responder (qultu) says the author intends to deny that implication (manʿ dhālika al-istilzām) and must prove it. Then the responder presents another investigation (baḥth ākhar) repeating the same dilemma, but with the caveat that the motion occurring in that time is necessarily the fastest motion, and if nothing faster exists, then the contradiction is avoided. The passage ends with 'let it be considered' (falyatʾamal).

Correction: The author denies the implication and shows that the assumption itself is impossible; the objection fails.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by denying that the scenario is even possible, showing that time is continuous with no actual parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector assumed a scenario that seemed logically forced, but it rested on an impossible assumption. What assumptions about time, motion, or causation do you hold that might be equally imaginary? How will you test them against revealed truth?
?
The author shows that only the fastest motion can occupy an imaginary time part. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes settle for less than the 'fastest' commitment to Allah, imagining that your slow efforts are sufficient in a time that may be only imaginary? What would it mean for you to pursue the fastest motion in obedience?

Motion's Essence Does Not Require Specific Time or Distance
Commentary on motion's essence and critic's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motion in itself requires only absolute time and distance, not specific amounts, and why the objection concerns particular motion.

Core Idea
Motion in itself is universal; only particular motion demands specific time.

This lesson unpacks a key distinction in the commentary: motion's essence (al-ḥaraka li-dhātihā) versus particular motion (al-ḥaraka al-mukhtaṣṣa). The commentator argues that motion as such does not require a specific time or distance—only absolute time and distance are required. The critic's objection, however, is about a specific motion occurring over a specific distance, which does require a determined time due to factors like moving power, body, and distance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on motion's essence
The opening lines state that motion occurs in any part of time and distance, so it does not necessitate a defined quantity of either.
Focus on 'لا تقتضي الحركة لذاتها قدرا معينا'.
2
Identify the two claims
First claim: motion requires only absolute time/distance. Second claim: therefore we avoid the problem of 'fastest motion.'
3
Understand the critic's objection
The commentator says the critic speaks about 'that particular motion' (al-ḥaraka al-mukhtaṣṣa), not motion in general.
The objection is not about the essence of motion but about motion with specific parameters.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does the commentator separate absolute motion from particular motion? How does this protect the author's argument?
This distinction is a common defense in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل تقع في أي جزء كان من الأجزاء المفروضة للزمان والمسافة فلا تقتضي الحركة لذاتها قدرا معينا من الزمان ولا من المسافة بل تقتضي مطلق الزمان والمسافة الموجودة في كل جزء من أجزائها فلا حاجة بنا إلى دعوى أنَّ اقتضاء الحركة لذاتها زماناً يستلزم أسرع الحركات حتى نحتاج في إبطال اللازم إلى بيان وقوع الحركة في نصف زمان الأسرع مع اتحاد المسافة. وإن لم نسلم نفي الجزء كان هو الجواب في الحقيقة ( وأيضاً فإِنَّ (الكلام من المعترض إنما هو ( في تلك الحركة المخصوصة لا في مطلق الحركة ) أي ليس اعتراضه بأن ماهية الحركة من حيث هي تقتضي زماناً . حتى يدفع بأنه باطل إما لاستلزامه وجود أسرع الحركات أو لأن ماهية الحركة موجودة في ضمن أي جزء من الحركة يوجد في أي جزء كان من أجزاء الزمان على ما قررناه . بل بأن الحركة المخصوصة التي توجد في مسافة مخصوصة تقتضي ذلك، إذ هي باعتبار القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك والمسافة المعينة تقتضي قدراً من الزمان. فإن بديهة العقل تحكم بذلك مع قطع النظر عن معاوقة المخروط، ثم إنَّ الزمان يزداد بسبب. قوله: (ونحن نقول الخ) إثبات لعدم اقتضاء ماهية الحركة قدراً من الزمان بحيث لا يرد بحث المصنف . ( قوله : ( كان هو الجواب في (الحقيقة لأن الحركة الخلائية والملائية حينئذ كلتاهما واقعتان في الآن والتفاوت بينهما بقلة السكنات المتخللة وكثرتها فإن الحركة عند أصحاب الجزء هو الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني والأجزاء والآنات والأكوان عندهم متتالية . قوله : ( بأن الحركة المخصوصة (الخ يعني فمعنى قوله لذاتها مع قطع النظر مع المعاوق لا لماهيتها . قوله : ( باعتبار القوة المحركة) بحسب اشتدادها وضعفها والجسم المتحرك باعتبار عظم مقداره وصغره وباختلاف شكله فإنَّ المربع إذا تحرك سطحه كان أبطأ من المخروط إذا تحرك المخروط . قوله : (ثم إنَّ الزمان يزداد الخ أقول : كما أنه يزداد الزمان بازدياد المعاوقة ينقص بانتقاصها ففي مراتب انتقاص المعاوقة، إما أن يمكن معاوقة يكون زمان حركتها مساوياً لحركة قوله : ( وإن لم نسلم نفي الجزء كان هو الجواب لأن مبنى الاستدلال على أنَّ الحركة لا ) تقع إلا على مسافة منقسمة ليثبت كونها في زمان البتة. وقد أشرنا فيما سبق إلى أن القائلين بالجزء لا يشترطون المسافة في الحركة بل إذا انتقل جزء من مكانه إلى جزء آخر يليه تتحقق الحركة. ولذا قالوا الخروج من الحيز السابق عين الدخول في اللاحق كما سيحققه الشارح في مباحث الأكوان، وبالجملة على تقدير ثبوت الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لا يقوم دليل على امتناع خلاء يوازيه مع أن المدعى هو السلب الكلي. أعني امتناع جميع أفراد الخلاء إلا أن يثبت أن إمكان الفرد من الخلاء يستلزم إمكان جميع أفراده.
The Second Observatory - The Ninth Purpose: Place, which is of continuous quantity. It occurs in any part of the supposed parts of time and distance. So motion in itself does not require a specific amount of time or distance, but only requires absolute time and distance existing in every part of its parts. So we have no need for the claim that motion's requirement of time by itself necessitates the fastest motion, so that we need to refute that by showing that motion occurs in half the time of the fastest with the same distance. And if we do not accept the denial of the part, that is the true answer. Also, the critic's speech is only about that particular motion, not about absolute motion. That is, his objection is not that the essence of motion insofar as it is requires time, so that it would be refuted as false either because it necessitates the existence of the fastest motion or because the essence of motion exists within any part of motion that exists in any part of time as we established. Rather, the particular motion that occurs in a particular distance requires that, because it, by virtue of the moving power, the moving body, and the specified distance, requires an amount of time. For the intuition of reason judges that, regardless of resistance from the cone. Then time increases due to... His saying: 'We say' etc. is a proof that the essence of motion does not require a specific amount of time, so that the author's investigation does not apply. His saying: 'It is the answer in reality' because the motion in void and in plenum both occur in the moment, and the difference between them is by the fewness and multitude of intervening rests. For motion according to the partisans of the part is the second being in the second place, and parts, moments, and beings are successive for them. His saying: 'By particular motion' etc. means the meaning of his saying 'by itself' ignoring resistance, not for its essence. His saying: 'By virtue of the moving power' according to its intensity and weakness, and the moving body according to the magnitude of its size and smallness, and by difference of its shape; for if a square moves, its surface is slower than a cone when the cone moves. His saying: 'Then time increases' etc. I say: just as time increases with increase of resistance, it decreases with decrease. So in the degrees of decrease of resistance, either a resistance can have its motion time equal to the motion of... His saying: 'And if we do not accept the denial of the part, it is the answer' because the basis of the argument is that motion only occurs over a divisible distance to prove that it is in time absolutely. And we have indicated previously that those who affirm the part do not require distance in motion; rather, if a part moves from its place to another adjacent part, motion is realized. So they said: exiting from the previous space is the same as entering the next, as the commentator will verify in the investigations of beings. And in summary, on the supposition of the affirmation of the indivisible part, there is no proof for the impossibility of a void equal to it, while the claim is universal negation, i.e., the impossibility of all individuals of the void, unless it is proven that the possibility of one individual of the void entails the possibility of all its individuals.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the author's initial argument and on the objection. It begins by restating that motion in itself (li-dhātihā) does not require a specific time or distance, only absolute time and distance. Therefore, we need not claim that motion's requirement of time necessitates the fastest motion. Then it addresses the critic: his objection concerns a particular motion (al-ḥaraka al-mukhtaṣṣa), not motion in general. A particular motion, due to the moving power (al-quwwa al-muḥarrika), the moving body (al-jism al-mutaḥarrik), and the specified distance (al-masāfa al-muʿayyana), does require a specific time—this is judged by the intuition of reason (badīhat al-ʿaql), regardless of resistance (muʿāwaqa). The commentary then glosses various phrases: 'We say' proves that motion's essence does not require a specific time so the author's investigation does not apply; 'It is the answer in reality' because motion in void and plenum both occur instantaneously (al-ān), differing by intervening rests (sakanāt mutakhallila); 'By particular motion' means 'by itself ignoring resistance'; 'By virtue of the moving power' depends on intensity/weakness, body size, and shape (e.g., a square's surface is slower than a cone). Finally, it addresses the alternative if the atom is affirmed (in lam nusallim nafy al-juzʾ): the argument that motion requires a divisible distance fails, because partisans of the part (al-qāʾilūn bi-l-juzʾ) do not require distance; motion is simply transition from one place to an adjacent place. Hence, no proof stands against the void (khalāʾ) unless it is proven that the possibility of one void entails all.

المسافة المعينة
معاوقة المخروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion in itself must occur over a specific time and distance, and that any objection to the author applies to motion in general.
Correction: The passage clarifies that motion's essence only requires absolute time/distance, and the critic's objection targets only particular motion.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the author not need to claim that motion requires a fastest motion?
A) Because motion does not require any time at all.
B) Because motion in itself does not require a specific amount of time, only absolute time.
C) Because the fastest motion is impossible.
D) Because the critic's objection is about particular motion.

Key Takeaway
Motion's essence does not demand a specific time or distance; only particular motion does, due to its specific parameters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your spiritual life, do you often use 'not enough time' as an excuse to delay good deeds? Realise that the essence of those deeds does not require a specific amount of time—only your particular circumstances do. What would you do differently today if you truly believed that the act itself is always possible, and only your excuses limit it? Commit to one action right now.
?
The critic confused motion in itself with particular motion. In what area of your faith have you confused the universal principle (e.g., the obligation of prayer) with your particular ability (e.g., 'I can't pray with full concentration, so I won't pray at all')? How can you separate the essence of the act from the barriers you add yourself? Write down one specific change.
?
The commentator says that particular motion requires time due to moving power, body, and distance. Consider your own 'moving power'—your spiritual strength and will. When you fail to act, do you blame external resistance (the 'cone') instead of your own weakness? What would it mean for you to take full responsibility for your spiritual effort, and increase your 'power' rather than complain about obstacles?

Atomist Alternative: Motion Without Distance
Commentary on the atomist alternative: motion without distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how partisans of the atom define motion as transition between adjacent places without requiring a divisible distance, and why this undermines arguments against the void.

Core Idea
For atomists, motion is just leaving one place and entering the next.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage, where the commentator provides an alternative answer if the existence of the indivisible atom is affirmed. The earlier argument assumed motion occurs over a divisible distance, but atomists (ashāb al-juzʾ) do not require distance at all. Instead, motion is simply the transition from one place to an adjacent place—'exiting from the previous space is the same as entering the next.' This shift has implications for the void: without a proof that motion requires distance, the impossibility of the void cannot be established.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the atomist view
Start from 'وإن لم نسلم نفي الجزء' to the end. This section explains that if the atom is affirmed, motion does not require distance.
2
Understand the atomist definition of motion
The commentator says that for atomists, motion is 'exiting from the previous space' which is the same as 'entering the next.' There is no need for a continuous distance.
This is called 'tahawwul' or transition.
3
Connect to the void issue
The argument against the void relies on motion requiring a divisible distance. If atomists deny that, the proof of void's impossibility fails.
The commentator says: 'no proof stands against the void.'
4
Reflect on the bigger picture
Ask yourself: Why does the commentator offer this alternative? How does it protect the author's position?
This shows flexibility: if the opponent accepts atoms, adjust the argument accordingly.
