( قوله : ( كان هو الجواب في (الحقيقة لأن الحركة الخلائية والملائية حينئذ كلتاهما واقعتان في الآن والتفاوت بينهما بقلة السكنات المتخللة وكثرتها فإن الحركة عند أصحاب الجزء هو الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني والأجزاء والآنات والأكوان عندهم متتالية . قوله : ( بأن الحركة المخصوصة (الخ يعني فمعنى قوله لذاتها مع قطع النظر مع المعاوق لا لماهيتها . قوله : ( باعتبار القوة المحركة) بحسب اشتدادها وضعفها والجسم المتحرك باعتبار عظم مقداره وصغره وباختلاف شكله فإنَّ المربع إذا تحرك سطحه كان أبطأ من المخروط إذا تحرك المخروط . قوله : (ثم إنَّ الزمان يزداد الخ أقول : كما أنه يزداد الزمان بازدياد المعاوقة ينقص بانتقاصها ففي مراتب انتقاص المعاوقة، إما أن يمكن معاوقة يكون زمان حركتها مساوياً لحركة قوله : ( وإن لم نسلم نفي الجزء كان هو الجواب لأن مبنى الاستدلال على أنَّ الحركة لا ) تقع إلا على مسافة منقسمة ليثبت كونها في زمان البتة. وقد أشرنا فيما سبق إلى أن القائلين بالجزء لا يشترطون المسافة في الحركة بل إذا انتقل جزء من مكانه إلى جزء آخر يليه تتحقق الحركة. ولذا قالوا الخروج من الحيز السابق عين الدخول في اللاحق كما سيحققه الشارح في مباحث الأكوان، وبالجملة على تقدير ثبوت الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لا يقوم دليل على امتناع خلاء يوازيه مع أن المدعى هو السلب الكلي. أعني امتناع جميع أفراد الخلاء إلا أن يثبت أن إمكان الفرد من الخلاء يستلزم إمكان جميع أفراده.
The Second Observatory - The Ninth Purpose: Place, which is of continuous quantity. It occurs in any part of the supposed parts of time and distance. So motion in itself does not require a specific amount of time or distance, but only requires absolute time and distance existing in every part of its parts. So we have no need for the claim that motion's requirement of time by itself necessitates the fastest motion, so that we need to refute that by showing that motion occurs in half the time of the fastest with the same distance. And if we do not accept the denial of the part, that is the true answer. Also, the critic's speech is only about that particular motion, not about absolute motion. That is, his objection is not that the essence of motion insofar as it is requires time, so that it would be refuted as false either because it necessitates the existence of the fastest motion or because the essence of motion exists within any part of motion that exists in any part of time as we established. Rather, the particular motion that occurs in a particular distance requires that, because it, by virtue of the moving power, the moving body, and the specified distance, requires an amount of time. For the intuition of reason judges that, regardless of resistance from the cone. Then time increases due to... His saying: 'We say' etc. is a proof that the essence of motion does not require a specific amount of time, so that the author's investigation does not apply. His saying: 'It is the answer in reality' because the motion in void and in plenum both occur in the moment, and the difference between them is by the fewness and multitude of intervening rests. For motion according to the partisans of the part is the second being in the second place, and parts, moments, and beings are successive for them. His saying: 'By particular motion' etc. means the meaning of his saying 'by itself' ignoring resistance, not for its essence. His saying: 'By virtue of the moving power' according to its intensity and weakness, and the moving body according to the magnitude of its size and smallness, and by difference of its shape; for if a square moves, its surface is slower than a cone when the cone moves. His saying: 'Then time increases' etc. I say: just as time increases with increase of resistance, it decreases with decrease. So in the degrees of decrease of resistance, either a resistance can have its motion time equal to the motion of... His saying: 'And if we do not accept the denial of the part, it is the answer' because the basis of the argument is that motion only occurs over a divisible distance to prove that it is in time absolutely. And we have indicated previously that those who affirm the part do not require distance in motion; rather, if a part moves from its place to another adjacent part, motion is realized. So they said: exiting from the previous space is the same as entering the next, as the commentator will verify in the investigations of beings. And in summary, on the supposition of the affirmation of the indivisible part, there is no proof for the impossibility of a void equal to it, while the claim is universal negation, i.e., the impossibility of all individuals of the void, unless it is proven that the possibility of one individual of the void entails the possibility of all its individuals.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the author's initial argument and on the objection. It begins by restating that motion in itself (li-dhātihā) does not require a specific time or distance, only absolute time and distance. Therefore, we need not claim that motion's requirement of time necessitates the fastest motion. Then it addresses the critic: his objection concerns a particular motion (al-ḥaraka al-mukhtaṣṣa), not motion in general. A particular motion, due to the moving power (al-quwwa al-muḥarrika), the moving body (al-jism al-mutaḥarrik), and the specified distance (al-masāfa al-muʿayyana), does require a specific time—this is judged by the intuition of reason (badīhat al-ʿaql), regardless of resistance (muʿāwaqa). The commentary then glosses various phrases: 'We say' proves that motion's essence does not require a specific time so the author's investigation does not apply; 'It is the answer in reality' because motion in void and plenum both occur instantaneously (al-ān), differing by intervening rests (sakanāt mutakhallila); 'By particular motion' means 'by itself ignoring resistance'; 'By virtue of the moving power' depends on intensity/weakness, body size, and shape (e.g., a square's surface is slower than a cone). Finally, it addresses the alternative if the atom is affirmed (in lam nusallim nafy al-juzʾ): the argument that motion requires a divisible distance fails, because partisans of the part (al-qāʾilūn bi-l-juzʾ) do not require distance; motion is simply transition from one place to an adjacent place. Hence, no proof stands against the void (khalāʾ) unless it is proven that the possibility of one void entails all.

Key Terms
أصحاب الجزء
الخروج من الحيز السابق
الدخول في اللاحق
الخلاء
السلب الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all definitions of motion require a continuous, divisible distance, and that affirming the atom automatically disproves the possibility of the void.
Correction: Atomists define motion without distance, so arguments against the void that rely on distance are not effective against them.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the definition of motion for those who affirm the indivisible part (atom)?
A) Motion is the traversal of a divisible distance.
B) Motion is exiting the previous place which is also entering the next adjacent place.
C) Motion is a series of jumps through the void.
D) Motion is impossible without a void.

Key Takeaway
For atomists, motion does not require distance; it is immediate transition to an adjacent place, undermining proofs of the void's impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How would your reliance on Allah (tawakkul) shift if you truly believed that transformation could be instantaneous, like the atomist's motion? ?
The atomist view denies that motion across distance is needed for change. Is there a belief you hold about how the world works—about cause and effect, or progress—that you have never questioned? If that belief were false, how would it affect your spiritual discipline?
?
The source shows that a universal claim (all voids are impossible) becomes unproven once one specific case (atom-sized void) is possible. What universal claim do you hold about yourself or others—'I can never change' or 'that person will never improve'—that might be invalid because one exception could exist? Will you actively look for that exception today?

The Narrow-Necked Vessel: A Sensory Proof Against Void
Al-Jurjani describes the first sensory proof using a narrow-necked vessel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the narrow-necked vessel (al-saraqat) provides a sensory proof for the impossibility of void (al-khala').

Core Idea
Water stops in a sealed vessel to avoid creating a void.

Al-Jurjani begins by presenting the first of several sensory arguments for the impossibility of void. This argument, known as al-saraqat (the 'water-thief'), uses a simple vessel with a narrow neck and a small hole to demonstrate that water cannot flow out if the top is sealed, because doing so would create a void. The physical behavior of water here is said to reveal a metaphysical truth: nature abhors a vacuum.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the vessel
Read the Arabic text describing the vessel and the experiment. Note the two conditions: narrow neck and narrow hole.
2
Understand why water stops
The passage explains that the water stops not due to any physical barrier but because flowing would necessitate a void. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the possibility of void?
3
Consider the design reasons
Why are the neck and hole narrow? The passage gives two reasons: to allow a complete seal and to prevent air from entering from below. Connect these to the argument against void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكماء على امتناع الخلاء بعلامات حسية الأولى السراقات جمع سراقة وهي الآنية الضيقة الرأس في أسفلها ثقبة ضيقة وتسمى في الفارسية آب دزد فإنه إذا ملئت تلك الآنية ماء وفتح المدخل خرج الماء من الثقبة الضيقة وإذا سد المدخل وقف الماء عن الخروج والنزول وليس ذلك الوقوف الماء مع أن طبعه يقتضي نزوله إلا لأنه لو خرج الماء مع كون المدخل مسدوداً لزم الخلاء وأما إذا كان المدخل مفتوحاً فلا يلزم خلاء إذ بمقدار ما يخرج من الماء يدخل فيه الهواء، وإنما اعتبر ضيق رأس الآنية ليمكن صدها بحيث لا يدخل فيه الهواء أصلاً. واعتبر ضيق الثقبة في أسفلها لأنها إذا كانت واسعة نزل الماء من جانب منها ودخل الهواء من جانب آخر. قوله بعلامات حسية كل منها يوجب الظن لعدم الخلاء في صورة جزئية لا على عدمه مطلقاً فما قيل إنّ كل واحد من الوجوه إنما يدل على امتناع الخلاء في الجملة لا على المدعى الذي هو امتناع الخلاء مطلقاً وهم. قوله وإذا سد المدخل وقف إلخ قيل على تقدير القول بالجزء يمكن أن يقال إذا سد المدخل يجوز أن ينزل جزء من الماء، ولا يحس به لغاية صغره، ويبقى حيز ذلك الجزء خلاء، ثم يقف الماء، وبطلان هذا إنما يثبت إذا ثبت أنَّ إمكان شيء من الخلاء يستلزم إمكان كل من أفراده المفروضة إلا أن يبنى الكلام على الإلزام فإن القائلين بإمكانه لا يفرقون بين فرد وفرد. قوله لزم الخلاء فإن قلت لم لا يجوز التخلخل قلت الطبيعة تقتضي الأسهل فالأسهل فربما كان وقف الماء أسهل عليها من تعظيم حجمه. قوله ودخل الهواء من جانب آخر يدل عليه البقابق واضطراب نزول الماء لمزاحمة صعود الهواء في الجرة الموضوعة في الماء.
The philosophers [affirm] the impossibility of void by sensory signs. The first: al-saraqat, plural of sarqa, which is a narrow-necked vessel with a narrow hole in its base, called in Persian 'āb duzd. For if that vessel is filled with water and the opening is opened, water comes out of the narrow hole, and if the opening is sealed, the water stops from exiting and descending. And that stopping of water, despite its nature requiring its descent, is only because if water came out while the opening was sealed, a void would necessarily occur. But if the opening is open, no void occurs, because as much water as comes out, air enters into it. And the narrowness of the vessel's neck was considered so that it can be sealed such that no air enters it at all. And the narrowness of the hole in its base was considered because if it were wide, water would descend from one side and air would enter from the other side. His statement: 'by sensory signs, each of them gives probability (ẓann) of the absence of void in a particular case, not of its absence absolutely; so what is said that each of the proofs indicates the impossibility of void in general, not the claimed impossibility of void absolutely, is a misunderstanding.' His statement: 'and when the opening is sealed it stops, etc.' It was said: on the assumption of the [indivisible] part, it can be said: when the opening is sealed, it is possible that a part of water descends, and it is not perceived due to its extreme smallness, and the space of that part remains a void, then the water stops. The invalidity of this is only established if it is established that the possibility of any void entails the possibility of every one of its supposed individuals, unless the discussion is built on lizām (obligation), for those who affirm its possibility do not distinguish between one individual and another. His statement: 'a void would necessarily occur' – for if you say: why is rarefaction (takhalkhul) not permissible? I say: nature requires the easier then the easier; perhaps stopping the water is easier for it than expanding its volume. His statement: 'and air enters from the other side' is indicated by the bubbling and the disturbance of the water's descent due to the competition of the rising air in the jar placed in water.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a proof from sensory signs for the impossibility of void (khala'), specifically the first proof using a narrow-necked vessel (al-saraqat). The author, al-Jurjani, begins by stating that the philosophers argue for the impossibility of void by means of sensory signs. He then describes the first sign: a narrow-necked vessel with a small hole at the bottom (like a traditional 'āb duzd). When the vessel is filled with water and the top opening is open, water flows out of the bottom hole. But when the top is sealed, the water stops. The author explains that the water stops not because of any physical barrier, but because if it continued to flow with the top sealed, a void would be left behind. When the top is open, air enters to replace the water, preventing void. The narrowness of the neck ensures a complete seal, and the narrowness of the bottom hole prevents air from entering simultaneously from the bottom. Then the author addresses a potential objection about the nature of these proofs: each sensory sign only gives probability (ẓann) in a particular case, not absolute certainty. Some might say that each proof only indicates impossibility in general, not the absolute claim. The author dismisses this as a misunderstanding (wahm). Next, a more specific objection is raised concerning the indivisible part (al-juz'): if one assumes atoms, it could be said that when the top is sealed, a tiny part of water might descend imperceptibly, leaving a void for that part, then the water stops. The author responds that this objection only holds if the possibility of any void entails the possibility of all supposed voids, but proponents of void do not distinguish between individuals; they accept the possibility in general. Alternatively, one might ask why rarefaction (takhalkhul) of water does not occur instead of stopping. The author answers that nature follows the easier path; it may be easier for water to stop than to increase its volume. Finally, he notes that the entrance of air from the other side is evidenced by bubbling and disturbance, confirming that void prevention drives the motion.

Key Terms
السراقات
الخلاء
الثقبة
المدخل

C) Because the vessel is made of a material that absorbs water.
D) Because the water is too heavy to flow out.

Key Takeaway
The narrow-necked vessel demonstrates that nature prevents void by stopping water flow when the air supply is cut off.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What changes in your attitude toward unseen truths when you realize your senses can point beyond themselves? Commit to one area where you will let reason, not just observation, guide your certainty.

The philosophers used this simple vessel to prove something fundamental. What simple signs around you are you ignoring that point to deeper truths? Ask yourself today: what do I take for granted that actually reveals the structure of reality? Write down one such sign and how it demands a response from you.

Are Sensory Proofs Certain? The Probability Objection
Al-Jurjani dismisses the objection that sensory signs are merely probable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that sensory signs only yield probability (ẓann) and al-Jurjani's response that this is a misunderstanding.

Core Idea
Sensory signs give probability in particular cases, not certainty, but the objection misses the point.

After presenting the sensory proof, al-Jurjani addresses a potential criticism: that each sensory sign only gives probable knowledge (ẓann) about a specific case, not absolute certainty about the impossibility of void in general. Some might argue that the proofs only show impossibility 'in general' (fī l-jumla), not the absolute claim (al-mudda'ā). Al-Jurjani dismisses this as a misunderstanding (wahm). This short but important passage clarifies the epistemological status of empirical arguments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The passage states that each sensory sign gives probability of absence of void in a particular case, not absolutely. Consider: does this weaken the argument?
2
Understand the misunderstanding
Al-Jurjani says that the claim that each proof indicates impossibility in general (fī l-jumla) but not the absolute claim is a misunderstanding. Why? Because the absolute claim is built from these cases.
3
Reflect on the role of probability
Think about whether sensory proofs need to be certain to be valid in kalam. The author implies that they are sufficient as indicators.

الحكماء على امتناع الخلاء بعلامات حسية الأولى السراقات جمع سراقة وهي الآنية الضيقة الرأس في أسفلها ثقبة ضيقة وتسمى في الفارسية آب دزد فإنه إذا ملئت تلك الآنية ماء وفتح المدخل خرج الماء من الثقبة الضيقة وإذا سد المدخل وقف الماء عن الخروج والنزول وليس ذلك الوقوف الماء مع أن طبعه يقتضي نزوله إلا لأنه لو خرج الماء مع كون المدخل مسدوداً لزم الخلاء وأما إذا كان المدخل مفتوحاً فلا يلزم خلاء إذ بمقدار ما يخرج من الماء يدخل فيه الهواء، وإنما اعتبر ضيق رأس الآنية ليمكن صدها بحيث لا يدخل فيه الهواء أصلاً. واعتبر ضيق الثقبة في أسفلها لأنها إذا كانت واسعة نزل الماء من جانب منها ودخل الهواء من جانب آخر. قوله بعلامات حسية كل منها يوجب الظن لعدم الخلاء في صورة جزئية لا على عدمه مطلقاً فما قيل إنّ كل واحد من الوجوه إنما يدل على امتناع الخلاء في الجملة لا على المدعى الذي هو امتناع الخلاء مطلقاً وهم. قوله وإذا سد المدخل وقف إلخ قيل على تقدير القول بالجزء يمكن أن يقال إذا سد المدخل يجوز أن ينزل جزء من الماء، ولا يحس به لغاية صغره، ويبقى حيز ذلك الجزء خلاء، ثم يقف الماء، وبطلان هذا إنما يثبت إذا ثبت أنَّ إمكان شيء من الخلاء يستلزم إمكان كل من أفراده المفروضة إلا أن يبنى الكلام على الإلزام فإن القائلين بإمكانه لا يفرقون بين فرد وفرد. قوله لزم الخلاء فإن قلت لم لا يجوز التخلخل قلت الطبيعة تقتضي الأسهل فالأسهل فربما كان وقف الماء أسهل عليها من تعظيم حجمه. قوله ودخل الهواء من جانب آخر يدل عليه البقابق واضطراب نزول الماء لمزاحمة صعود الهواء في الجرة الموضوعة في الماء.
The philosophers [affirm] the impossibility of void by sensory signs. The first: al-saraqat, plural of sarqa, which is a narrow-necked vessel with a narrow hole in its base, called in Persian 'āb duzd. For if that vessel is filled with water and the opening is opened, water comes out of the narrow hole, and if the opening is sealed, the water stops from exiting and descending. And that stopping of water, despite its nature requiring its descent, is only because if water came out while the opening was sealed, a void would necessarily occur. But if the opening is open, no void occurs, because as much water as comes out, air enters into it. And the narrowness of the vessel's neck was considered so that it can be sealed such that no air enters it at all. And the narrowness of the hole in its base was considered because if it were wide, water would descend from one side and air would enter from the other side. His statement: 'by sensory signs, each of them gives probability (ẓann) of the absence of void in a particular case, not of its absence absolutely; so what is said that each of the proofs indicates the impossibility of void in general, not the claimed impossibility of void absolutely, is a misunderstanding.' His statement: 'and when the opening is sealed it stops, etc.' It was said: on the assumption of the [indivisible] part, it can be said: when the opening is sealed, it is possible that a part of water descends, and it is not perceived due to its extreme smallness, and the space of that part remains a void, then the water stops. The invalidity of this is only established if it is established that the possibility of any void entails the possibility of every one of its supposed individuals, unless the discussion is built on lizām (obligation), for those who affirm its possibility do not distinguish between one individual and another. His statement: 'a void would necessarily occur' – for if you say: why is rarefaction (takhalkhul) not permissible? I say: nature requires the easier then the easier; perhaps stopping the water is easier for it than expanding its volume. His statement: 'and air enters from the other side' is indicated by the bubbling and the disturbance of the water's descent due to the competition of the rising air in the jar placed in water.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a proof from sensory signs for the impossibility of void (khala'), specifically the first proof using a narrow-necked vessel (al-saraqat). The author, al-Jurjani, begins by stating that the philosophers argue for the impossibility of void by means of sensory signs. He then describes the first sign: a narrow-necked vessel with a small hole at the bottom (like a traditional 'āb duzd). When the vessel is filled with water and the top opening is open, water flows out of the bottom hole. But when the top is sealed, the water stops. The author explains that the water stops not because of any physical barrier, but because if it continued to flow with the top sealed, a void would be left behind. When the top is open, air enters to replace the water, preventing void. The narrowness of the neck ensures a complete seal, and the narrowness of the bottom hole prevents air from entering simultaneously from the bottom. Then the author addresses a potential objection about the nature of these proofs: each sensory sign only gives probability (ẓann) in a particular case, not absolute certainty. Some might say that each proof only indicates impossibility in general, not the absolute claim. The author dismisses this as a misunderstanding (wahm). Next, a more specific objection is raised concerning the indivisible part (al-juz'): if one assumes atoms, it could be said that when the top is sealed, a tiny part of water might descend imperceptibly, leaving a void for that part, then the water stops. The author responds that this objection only holds if the possibility of any void entails the possibility of all supposed voids, but proponents of void do not distinguish between individuals; they accept the possibility in general. Alternatively, one might ask why rarefaction (takhalkhul) of water does not occur instead of stopping. The author answers that nature follows the easier path; it may be easier for water to stop than to increase its volume. Finally, he notes that the entrance of air from the other side is evidenced by bubbling and disturbance, confirming that void prevention drives the motion.

Key Terms
الظن
الخلاء
الجملة
المدعى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory proofs are only probable and therefore cannot prove universal impossibility of void.
Correction: Al-Jurjani states that this is a misunderstanding; the proofs indeed demonstrate impossibility in each case, and the universal claim follows.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jurjani call the claim that each sensory sign only indicates impossibility in general, not absolutely?
A) A valid critique
B) A misunderstanding (wahm)
C) A necessary distinction
D) A logical fallacy

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani dismisses the objection that sensory proofs are merely probable as a misunderstanding; they provide sufficient evidence for the absolute claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a compelling truth because you felt each piece of evidence was only 'probable'? How does al-Jurjani's dismissal of the objection challenge your standard for certainty?
?
What 'wahm'—what misunderstanding—might be currently causing you to doubt a clear proof in your faith? What would it take for you to see the accumulation of signs as sufficient?
?
Are there areas in your spiritual life where you settle for 'probability' instead of seeking certainty through careful study and reflection on the evidence? What would change if you took the sensory signs of Allah's existence more seriously?

Responding to the Indivisible Parts Objection and Rarefaction
Al-Jurjani responds to two objections: indivisible parts and rarefaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's response to the objection that an indivisible part of water could descend imperceptibly and create a tiny void, and his argument against rarefaction.

Core Idea
If a part of water could descend without perception, it would still create a void; nature prefers the easier path.

Al-Jurjani addresses a more technical objection based on the assumption of indivisible parts (atomism). The objector suggests that when the vessel is sealed, a single indivisible part of water could descend without being perceived, leaving a void for that part. Al-Jurjani responds that this only works if the possibility of any void implies the possibility of all, which proponents of void do not deny. He then anticipates a second objection: why not rarefaction (takhalkhul) instead of stopping? His answer is that nature follows the path of least resistance, and stopping is easier than expanding. Finally, he points to empirical evidence of air bubbling up as confirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the indivisible parts objection
The objector says that if we assume indivisible parts (atoms), a tiny part of water could descend imperceptibly, leaving a void for that part. Understand the logic.
2
Examine the response
Al-Jurjani says the objection only stands if possibility of one void entails possibility of all; but proponents of void do not distinguish. Consider what this means.
3
Understand the rarefaction question
The second part asks: why doesn't water rarefy (expand) instead of stopping? Al-Jurjani's answer: nature chooses the easier option, and stopping is easier.
4
Note the empirical evidence
The bubbling water confirms that air enters from below, supporting the argument that void is prevented.

الحكماء على امتناع الخلاء بعلامات حسية الأولى السراقات جمع سراقة وهي الآنية الضيقة الرأس في أسفلها ثقبة ضيقة وتسمى في الفارسية آب دزد فإنه إذا ملئت تلك الآنية ماء وفتح المدخل خرج الماء من الثقبة الضيقة وإذا سد المدخل وقف الماء عن الخروج والنزول وليس ذلك الوقوف الماء مع أن طبعه يقتضي نزوله إلا لأنه لو خرج الماء مع كون المدخل مسدوداً لزم الخلاء وأما إذا كان المدخل مفتوحاً فلا يلزم خلاء إذ بمقدار ما يخرج من الماء يدخل فيه الهواء، وإنما اعتبر ضيق رأس الآنية ليمكن صدها بحيث لا يدخل فيه الهواء أصلاً. واعتبر ضيق الثقبة في أسفلها لأنها إذا كانت واسعة نزل الماء من جانب منها ودخل الهواء من جانب آخر. قوله بعلامات حسية كل منها يوجب الظن لعدم الخلاء في صورة جزئية لا على عدمه مطلقاً فما قيل إنّ كل واحد من الوجوه إنما يدل على امتناع الخلاء في الجملة لا على المدعى الذي هو امتناع الخلاء مطلقاً وهم. قوله وإذا سد المدخل وقف إلخ قيل على تقدير القول بالجزء يمكن أن يقال إذا سد المدخل يجوز أن ينزل جزء من الماء، ولا يحس به لغاية صغره، ويبقى حيز ذلك الجزء خلاء، ثم يقف الماء، وبطلان هذا إنما يثبت إذا ثبت أنَّ إمكان شيء من الخلاء يستلزم إمكان كل من أفراده المفروضة إلا أن يبنى الكلام على الإلزام فإن القائلين بإمكانه لا يفرقون بين فرد وفرد. قوله لزم الخلاء فإن قلت لم لا يجوز التخلخل قلت الطبيعة تقتضي الأسهل فالأسهل فربما كان وقف الماء أسهل عليها من تعظيم حجمه. قوله ودخل الهواء من جانب آخر يدل عليه البقابق واضطراب نزول الماء لمزاحمة صعود الهواء في الجرة الموضوعة في الماء.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If void is impossible, water cannot move at all; but the experiment shows it does.

According to al-Jurjani, why does water not rarefy instead of stopping when the vessel is sealed?
A) Water cannot expand.
B) Stopping is easier for nature than expanding.
C) Rarefaction would create a void.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Jurjani says nature always chooses the easier path. When you are confronted with a choice between a difficult truth and a comfortable falsehood, do you tend to follow nature's principle of ease? Can you name a specific situation where you chose the easier path away from truth, and what stopped the water in you?
?
The bubbling in the jar is evidence that air rushes in to avoid a void. In your life, when you create a void by ignoring a duty or a truth, what 'air' rushes in to fill it? Are you aware of the compromises you make to avoid confronting emptiness?

The Copper Tube Experiment Against Void
Al-Jurjani describes the copper tube and two experiments demonstrating impossibility of void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the copper tube experiment was used to argue against the existence of void.

Core Idea
Observation of water motion disproves void inside the tube.

After the first proof based on interpenetration, al-Jurjani presents a more direct sensory proof: the copper tube. This experiment uses a specially designed tube and a wooden rod to demonstrate that void cannot exist. The observable behavior of water provides a clear refutation of the void theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing the apparatus
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the description of the tube: one part narrow with a tight cavity, the other wide. Also a wooden rod that fits the wide part.
The apparatus is designed to test the behavior of water when a rod is inserted.
2
Follow the first experiment
When the tube is filled with water and the rod seals the wide end, water does not exit from the narrow end. As the rod is pushed in, water exits forcefully. The argument: if void existed, water would move into the void instead of exiting.
Observation contradicts the void hypothesis.
3
Understand the second experiment
If the rod is inserted from inside to the narrow hole and placed on water, pulling the rod causes water to rise into the tube, again due to impossibility of void.
This second experiment reinforces the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the sensory evidence
The author calls the conclusion 'false by sensory evidence,' meaning the senses directly refute the void claim.
Sensory proof is considered strong in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية الزراقات وهي أنبوبة معمولة من نحاس يجعل أحد شطريها دقيقاً وتجويفه ضيقاً جداً ويجعل شطرها الآخر غليظاً وتجويفه واسعاً ويسوى خشب طويل بحيث يكون غلظه مالئاً لتجويفه الواسع فإنه إذا ملئت تلك الأنبوبة ماء ووضع الخشبة على مدخلها بحيث تسده لم يخرج الماء من الطرف الآخر ثم إنه بقدر ما يدخل الخشب فيها يخرج الماء من التجويف الضيق خروجاً بقوة ويقطع مسافة ولو وجد في داخل تلك الأنبوبة خلاء لكان الماء ينتقل إليه بقدره أي لكان ينتقل الماء إلى ذلك الخلاء بقدر ما يدخل الخشب فيها فلا يخرج عنها وهو باطل بشهادة الحس وأيضاً إذا أوصل الخشبة من داخل إلى الثقبة الضيقة ووضعت على الماء، ثم جذبت الخشبة من الأنبوبة ارتفع الماء في الأنبوبة لامتناع الخلاء. نقل عن الشارح أن هذا الوجه أوفق بامتناع الخلاء. والأول بامتناع التداخل والحق أن الوجه الأول لا يدل على نفي مذهب الخصم أعني مثبت الخلاء لأنه لا يدعي وجوده في جميع الأشياء بل إمكانه ووجوده في الجملة وذلك الوجه إنما يدل على أن لا خلاء في داخل تلك الأنبوبة لا على المدعى الذي هو امتناع الخلاء مطلقاً.
The second: al-zuraqat, which is a tube made of copper, one part of which is made narrow and its cavity very tight, and its other part is made thick and its cavity wide, and a long piece of wood is made such that its thickness fills its wide cavity. For if that tube is filled with water, and the wood is placed at its entrance such that it seals it, water does not come out from the other end. Then, as much as the wood enters it, water comes out from the narrow cavity with force and traverses a distance. And if there were a void inside that tube, the water would transfer to it by its amount, i.e., water would transfer to that void by as much as the wood enters it, and so it would not come out of it. But this is false by sensory evidence. Also, if the wood is inserted from inside to the narrow hole and placed on water, then the wood is pulled from the tube, water rises in the tube due to the impossibility of void. It is narrated from the commentator that this proof is more in accordance with the impossibility of void, while the first proof is about the impossibility of interpenetration. The truth is that the first proof does not refute the doctrine of the opponent, I mean the affirmers of void, because he does not claim its existence in all things, but rather its possibility and existence in general. And that proof only indicates that there is no void inside that tube, not the claimed absolute impossibility of void.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents the second sensory proof against void: the copper tube (al-zuraqat). The author first describes the apparatus: a tube with a narrow section and a wide section, and a wooden rod that fits the wide cavity. When the tube is filled with water and the rod seals the wide end, water does not come out of the narrow end. As the rod is pushed in, water exits from the narrow side with force. The argument: if there were a void inside the tube, the water would move into that void instead of exiting, but observation shows it exits, so no void exists. A second experiment supports this: if the rod is inserted from inside to the narrow hole and then pulled, water rises into the tube, again preventing void. The author then quotes a commentator who notes that this second proof better supports the impossibility of void, while the first proof actually addresses the impossibility of interpenetration. He adds a critical evaluation: the first proof does not refute the opponent's position (those who affirm void) because the opponent does not claim void exists everywhere, only its possibility and occasional existence. The first proof only shows no void inside that specific tube, not the absolute impossibility of void. This evaluation is important for understanding the strength of the arguments.

Key Terms
الزراقات (al-zuraqat)
الأنبوبة (al-ubnūba)
الخشب (al-khashab)
الخلاء (al-khala')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first experiment with the copper tube demonstrate?
A) Water can exit from the narrow end only if void exists.
B) Water exits from the narrow end when the rod is pushed in, which would not happen if void existed.
C) The wooden rod dissolves in water.
D) The tube cannot hold water.

Key Takeaway
The copper tube experiment uses sensory evidence to show that void cannot exist inside the tube, because water behaves as if void is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the author says 'this is false by sensory evidence,' do you trust your own senses enough to accept a truth that contradicts a common belief? Where in your own life are you holding onto an idea despite what your senses or experience tell you?
?
This experiment uses a physical object (a tube and water) to argue about an unseen reality (void). In your daily life, what physical signs or patterns do you rely on to confirm your belief in the unseen? Are you consistent in using this method, or do you sometimes dismiss evidence that doesn't fit your assumptions?
?
If someone asked you to prove that the universe is not empty but filled with God's creation, could you point to a similar 'experiment' from your own experience? How does this lesson challenge you to look for concrete signs of Allah's existence in the world around you?

Critical Evaluation of the Proofs Against Void
Al-Jurjani evaluates the second proof and compares its strength to the first, noting the first proof's limited scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of the first proof compared to the second, and why the first does not refute the opponent's claim about void.

Core Idea
The first proof only disproves void in a specific tube, not void in general.

After presenting the second proof, al-Jurjani evaluates its relative strength. He notes that the second proof is more in line with the impossibility of void, while the first proof actually addresses interpenetration. Crucially, he points out that the opponent does not claim void exists everywhere, only its possibility. Thus, the first proof is insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the evaluation passage
Read from 'نقل عن الشارح' to the end. Note the commentator's distinction between the two proofs.
The first proof is about impossibility of interpenetration, not void.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent affirms void in general, not in all things. So disproving void in one tube does not disprove the general claim.
This is a key point in argumentation.
3
Recognize the limitation of the first proof
The first proof only shows no void inside that specific tube, not absolute impossibility. Therefore, it does not refute the opponent's position.
This evaluation shows the need for arguments with universal scope.

الثانية الزراقات وهي أنبوبة معمولة من نحاس يجعل أحد شطريها دقيقاً وتجويفه ضيقاً جداً ويجعل شطرها الآخر غليظاً وتجويفه واسعاً ويسوى خشب طويل بحيث يكون غلظه مالئاً لتجويفه الواسع فإنه إذا ملئت تلك الأنبوبة ماء ووضع الخشبة على مدخلها بحيث تسده لم يخرج الماء من الطرف الآخر ثم إنه بقدر ما يدخل الخشب فيها يخرج الماء من التجويف الضيق خروجاً بقوة ويقطع مسافة ولو وجد في داخل تلك الأنبوبة خلاء لكان الماء ينتقل إليه بقدره أي لكان ينتقل الماء إلى ذلك الخلاء بقدر ما يدخل الخشب فيها فلا يخرج عنها وهو باطل بشهادة الحس وأيضاً إذا أوصل الخشبة من داخل إلى الثقبة الضيقة ووضعت على الماء، ثم جذبت الخشبة من الأنبوبة ارتفع الماء في الأنبوبة لامتناع الخلاء. نقل عن الشارح أن هذا الوجه أوفق بامتناع الخلاء. والأول بامتناع التداخل والحق أن الوجه الأول لا يدل على نفي مذهب الخصم أعني مثبت الخلاء لأنه لا يدعي وجوده في جميع الأشياء بل إمكانه ووجوده في الجملة وذلك الوجه إنما يدل على أن لا خلاء في داخل تلك الأنبوبة لا على المدعى الذي هو امتناع الخلاء مطلقاً.
The second: al-zuraqat, which is a tube made of copper, one part of which is made narrow and its cavity very tight, and its other part is made thick and its cavity wide, and a long piece of wood is made such that its thickness fills its wide cavity. For if that tube is filled with water, and the wood is placed at its entrance such that it seals it, water does not come out from the other end. Then, as much as the wood enters it, water comes out from the narrow cavity with force and traverses a distance. And if there were a void inside that tube, the water would transfer to it by its amount, i.e., water would transfer to that void by as much as the wood enters it, and so it would not come out of it. But this is false by sensory evidence. Also, if the wood is inserted from inside to the narrow hole and placed on water, then the wood is pulled from the tube, water rises in the tube due to the impossibility of void. It is narrated from the commentator that this proof is more in accordance with the impossibility of void, while the first proof is about the impossibility of interpenetration. The truth is that the first proof does not refute the doctrine of the opponent, I mean the affirmers of void, because he does not claim its existence in all things, but rather its possibility and existence in general. And that proof only indicates that there is no void inside that tube, not the claimed absolute impossibility of void.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents the second sensory proof against void: the copper tube (al-zuraqat). The author first describes the apparatus: a tube with a narrow section and a wide section, and a wooden rod that fits the wide cavity. When the tube is filled with water and the rod seals the wide end, water does not come out of the narrow end. As the rod is pushed in, water exits from the narrow side with force. The argument: if there were a void inside the tube, the water would move into that void instead of exiting, but observation shows it exits, so no void exists. A second experiment supports this: if the rod is inserted from inside to the narrow hole and then pulled, water rises into the tube, again preventing void. The author then quotes a commentator who notes that this second proof better supports the impossibility of void, while the first proof actually addresses the impossibility of interpenetration. He adds a critical evaluation: the first proof does not refute the opponent's position (those who affirm void) because the opponent does not claim void exists everywhere, only its possibility and occasional existence. The first proof only shows no void inside that specific tube, not the absolute impossibility of void. This evaluation is important for understanding the strength of the arguments.

Key Terms
الشارح (al-shāriḥ)
التداخل (al-tadākhul)
الخلاء (al-khala')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the first proof completely refutes the existence of void.
Correction: The first proof only demonstrates the absence of void in a specific container, not its absolute impossibility.

Checkpoint
Why does the first proof fail to refute the opponent's position?
A) Because the opponent denies the experiment
B) Because the opponent only claims void is possible, not that it exists everywhere
C) Because the first proof is too complicated
D) Because the tube is made of copper

Key Takeaway
A valid argument against void must address the opponent's actual claim of possibility, not just show absence in a specific instance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a strong conviction you hold, whether in theology or daily life. Is the evidence you rely on limited like the tube example? How can you test whether your proof truly addresses the claim in its full scope?
?
Al-Jurjani honestly admits the first proof's limitation, even though it weakens his own argument. When have you admitted a flaw in your reasoning, even when it was costly? If you haven't, what held you back?

Sensory Proofs of Void Impossibility: Cupping Glass and Water
Al-Jurjani presents two additional sensory proofs: the cupping glass and water rising in a tube, both relying on suction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the cupping glass and water-in-tube experiments demonstrate the impossibility of void through sensory observation.

Core Idea
Suction reveals nature's horror of void — objects move only to fill emptiness.

Al-Jurjani continues his series of sensory demonstrations that emptiness (void) is impossible. In this passage, he presents two everyday phenomena — the cupping glass pulling up flesh, and water rising in a straw — both explained by the same principle: surfaces of bodies must remain contiguous, so when air is removed, the surrounding matter rushes in to prevent a vacuum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Let the author's descriptions shape your understanding.
Notice that both proofs involve suction removing air.
2
Analyze the cupping glass proof
Identify the two scenarios: flesh rises with suction, iron does not unless completely sealed. Understand why the iron behaves differently.
The anvil rising shows the force of void prevention.
3
Understand the water-in-tube proof
Water rises despite its natural downward tendency. The key is the contiguity of air and water surfaces.
This defies natural motion, indicating an external necessity.
4
Synthesize the common principle
Both proofs rely on the impossibility of void. Suction removes air, and since surfaces must be contiguous, matter moves to fill the space.
The cause is not suction itself, but the avoidance of vacuum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة ارتفاع اللحم في المحجمة بالمص فإنا نشاهد أن المحجمة إذا وضعت على اللحم من أعضاء الإنسان ثم مصت فإنه يرتفع اللحم في داخل المحجمة وما هو إلا لأنه بقدر ما يمص من الهواء ويخرج منها يستتبع ذلك الهواء الممصوص المخرج منها ما يملؤها من اللحم قسراً ضرورة دفع الخلاء ووجوب تلازم سطوح الأجسام، وإذا ألقينا المحجمة على الحديد بحيث لا يكون بينهما منفذ يدخل فيه الهواء ثم مصصناها لم يرتفع الحديد إما لأن الهواء لا يخرج منها أو لأنه يخرج منها بعضه وينبسط الباقي فيملؤها، وإذا وضعت المحجمة على السندان وضعاً لا يبقى معه منفذ ثم مصت مصاً قوياً ورفعت المحجمة فإنه يرتفع السندان بارتفاعها. الرابعة وكذلك يرتفع الماء في الأنبوبة، فإنه إذا غمس أحد طرفيها في الماء ومص الآخر ارتفع الماء إلى فم الماص مع ثقله واقتضاء طبعه النزول دون الارتفاع وما ذلك الارتفاع إلا لأن سطح الهواء ملازم لسطح الماء بسبب امتناع الخلاء فإذا ارتفع سطح الهواء بالمص تبعه سطح الماء لضرورة دفع الخلاء.
The third: the rising of flesh in the cupping glass by suction. For we observe that when the cupping glass is placed on the flesh of a person's limbs and then suction is applied, the flesh rises inside the cupping glass. And it is only because, as much air as is sucked and extracted from it, that sucked and extracted air forcibly draws what fills it from the flesh, by necessity of repelling void and the obligation of the surfaces of bodies to be contiguous. And if we place the cupping glass on iron such that there is no passage for air to enter between them, then we apply suction, the iron does not rise – either because air does not come out of it, or because some of it comes out and the remainder expands and fills it. And if the cupping glass is placed on an anvil such that no passage remains, then strong suction is applied and the cupping glass is lifted, the anvil rises with it. The fourth: likewise, water rises in a tube. For if one end is immersed in water and the other is sucked, water rises to the mouth of the sucker, despite its weight and its nature requiring descent, not ascent. And that rising is only because the surface of air is contiguous with the surface of water due to the impossibility of void. So when the surface of air rises by suction, the surface of water follows it out of necessity to repel void.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents two additional sensory proofs against void: the cupping glass and water in a tube. The third proof describes the cupping glass (al-miḥjama): when placed on flesh and suction is applied, the flesh rises. The explanation is that as air is sucked out, the removed air forces the flesh to fill the space, driven by the necessity of avoiding void and the requirement that surfaces of bodies remain contiguous. The author adds a refinement: if the cupping glass is placed on smooth iron with no air entry, suction does not raise the iron; this is because air either does not exit or the remaining air expands. However, on an anvil, with no gap, strong suction can lift the anvil along with the glass. The fourth proof involves a tube: if one end is immersed in water and the other is sucked, water rises against its natural downward tendency. The reason is that the air surface is contiguous with the water surface; when suction raises the air surface, the water follows to prevent a void. Both proofs rely on the principle that nature abhors a vacuum, and they demonstrate that motion contrary to natural inclination (e.g., water rising) occurs only to avoid void.

Key Terms
المحجمة (al-miḥjama)
المص (al-maṣṣ)
الخلاء (al-khala')
السندان (al-sindān)
الأنبوبة (al-ubnūba)
سطح الهواء (saṭḥ al-hawā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Suction itself is the direct cause of the rising motion.
Correction: Suction only removes air; the rising is caused by the necessity to avoid a void, which forces matter to fill the emptied space.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why does water rise in a tube when one end is immersed and the other is sucked?
A) Because water naturally rises when heated.
B) Because the suction creates a force that pulls the water up.
C) Because the air surface is contiguous with the water surface, and when the air rises by suction, the water follows to prevent a void.
D) Because the water is lighter than air.

Key Takeaway
The sensory proofs of the cupping glass and water in a tube show that motion contrary to natural inclination occurs only to avoid a vacuum, demonstrating the impossibility of void.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The water in the tube rises against its natural inclination to fill the void left by the air. What 'voids' in your spiritual life are you leaving unfilled, assuming they will stay empty? Are you actively drawing near to Allah to fill those spaces, or are you letting other things rush in? Today, name one void and a specific action to fill it with dhikr or salah.
?
When you see everyday phenomena like suction or rising water, do you pause to see the hand of Allah behind the laws of nature? This lesson reveals that even simple physical motions obey a deeper order. How often do you rush past such signs without a thought? Commit to observing one natural event today (like rain or sunrise) and reflecting on the divine wisdom it displays.
?
The cupping glass forces the flesh to rise because nature cannot bear emptiness. In your heart, what empty philosophies or worldly pursuits are being sucked in because you haven't sealed yourself with faith? This lesson teaches that a vacuum will be filled. What will you actively choose to fill your mind and heart with to prevent spiritual void?

Ibn Sina's Definition of Sensible Qualities by Similitude
Ibn Sina defines sensible qualities as those that act by similitude and excludes weight and lightness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's criterion for sensible qualities: they act by producing a likeness of themselves in the recipient.

Core Idea
A sensible quality is one that replicates itself in what it affects.

In this passage, Ibn Sina defines sensible qualities in a very specific way: they are those that act by similitude. That is, they produce a copy of themselves in the object they affect. This criterion excludes weight and lightness, which cause motion but do not transmit their own nature. Understanding this definition is key to grasping Ibn Sina's classification of qualities and the physical basis of perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then the literal translation. Notice the compact style and the interplay between Ibn Sina's words and the commentator's clarifications.
2
Identify the definition and examples
Find where Ibn Sina states the condition 'if it acts by similitude' and note the examples of heat and blackness. What do these have in common?
Heat makes things hot; blackness leaves an image.
3
Contrast with heaviness
Ibn Sina says heaviness is unlike the sensible qualities because its action is movement, not heaviness itself. Why does this fail the 'similitude' test?
Heaviness does not make the adjacent body heavy; it only moves it.
4
Review the clarifications and objections
Work through the commentator's explanations about the 'image', the 'action on body', and the responses to objections about heat and heaviness. Pay attention to the distinction between direct and mediated action.
The key point: sensible qualities act directly by similitude, without intermediary.
5
Summarize the exclusion of weight and lightness
Imam al-Razi confirms that this definition explicitly removes weight and lightness from the category of sensible qualities. Reflect on why this matters for the classification of qualities in physics and metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن سينا : ( في الشفاء الكيف (إن فعل بالتشبيه) أي إِنَّ صدر عنه ما يشبهه (فمحسوس) كالحرارة، فإنها تجعل ما يجاور محلها حاراً، وكالسواد فإنه يلقي شبحه في العين وهو مثاله : بخلاف الثقل فإن فعله في الجسم هو التحريك، وليس ثقلا قال الإمام الرازي: هذا تصريح من ابن سينا بإخراج الثقل والخفة عن نوع الكيفيات المحسوسة. قوله : ( حذفاً لمؤونة (الترديد لا مؤونة لأن المقصود بالترديد ضبط الأقسام وسهولة الاستقراء فإنَّ القسم المرسل يحتاج إلى الاستقراء دون غيره . قوله : ( كالحرارة وكذا الحال في المذوقات والمشمومات والمسموعات، فإنه يتكيف الأعضاء التي فيها الحواس بكيفية مدركاتها . قوله : ( فإنه يلقي شبحه إلخ) ليس المراد منه إلقاء الصورة الإدراكية للسواد، لأنه يستلزم أن يكون جميع الإدراكات داخلاً في الكيفيات المحسوسة بل يتكيف العين بنفسه فإنَّ الناظر إلى الخضرة مثلاً إذا نظر إلى غيرها يحس لونه مخلوطاً بالخضرة لتكييف العين والخيال بهما . قوله : ( فإن فعله في الجسم أي جسمه كذا في الشفاء . قوله : (هو التحريك ) وأما مدافعة ما يجاوره بتحريك جسمه وإن كان فعله بالواسطة لكنه ليس يثقل إذ هو عبارة عن المدافعة الطبيعية. كذا قيل : وفيه أنَّ الحرارة في المجاور أيضاً كذلك والصواب أن يقال : لم يصدر عن الثقل المدافعة بلا واسطة بل بواسطة التحريك، والمراد أن يكون فعله التشبيه بلا واسطة . قوله : ( إذ لا يجوز إدخالهما في الكم إلخ) في الشفاء يظن بهما أنهما من باب الكمية. قوله: ( ولا يمكن إدخالهما إلخ) في الشفاء قد يظن بهما أنهما من باب القوة واللاقوة. قوله : ( الكيف إن فعل بالتشبيه (الخ قيل: إن أراد الحصر فلا يستقيم، لأن الحرارة تفعل التفريق أيضاً، وإن أراد الاطلاق، فالثقل يفعل شبهه في الحس المشترك عند الحس، كما يلقي السواد شبحه في العين. وأجيب : بأن تأدي الثقل إلى الحس المشترك فرع كونه محسوساً بالحس الظاهر، وذلك أول المسألة فتأمل . قوله : ( بخلاف الثقل (الخ من إخراج الثقل يفهم إخراج الخفة، ولهذا قال الإمام : وهذا تصريح من الشيخ بإخراج الثقل والخفة فإن قلت : الخفة مثلا إما مدافعة صاعدة أو مبدؤها وأياً ما كان فقد يعطي الجسم الملاقي لمحلها مدافعة أو مبدءها كاليد الموضوع على الزق المنفوخ فيه المسكن تحت الماء. قلت : الخفة المدافعة أو مبدأها الطبيعيان ولا يعطي الجسم الملاقي إياهما .
Ibn Sina said in al-Shifa: 'The quality, if it acts by similitude, meaning that what resembles it proceeds from it, then it is sensible, like heat, for it makes what is adjacent to its place hot, and like blackness, for it casts its image in the eye, and that is its likeness; unlike heaviness, for its action on the body is movement, not heaviness.' Imam al-Razi said: 'This is a clear statement from Ibn Sina excluding heaviness and lightness from the category of sensible qualities.' His saying: 'Removing the burden of alternation' — no burden, because the purpose of alternation is to fix the divisions and facilitate induction, for the absolute division requires induction without other. His saying: 'Like heat' — and likewise for tastes, smells, and sounds, for the organs containing the senses become qualified by the quality of their perceptibles. His saying: 'For it casts its image, etc.' — the intention is not that it projects the perceptual form of blackness, because that would necessitate that all perceptions are included in sensible qualities; rather, the eye becomes qualified by itself, for the beholder, when he looks at greenness and then looks at another color, senses his color mixed with greenness due to the qualification of the eye and the imagination by them. His saying: 'For its action on the body' — meaning his body, as in al-Shifa. His saying: 'It is movement' — as for repelling what is adjacent by moving its body, even if it is action by mediation, it is not heaviness, for it is natural repulsion. Thus it is said: and in it that heat in the adjacent is also so, and the correct thing is to say: no repulsion proceeds from heaviness directly, but mediately through movement, and the meaning is that its action be by similitude without mediation. His saying: 'Since it is not permissible to include them in quantity, etc.' — in al-Shifa, it is thought that they are from the category of quantity. His saying: 'And it is not possible to include them, etc.' — in al-Shifa, it is thought that they are from the category of power and impotence. His saying: 'The quality, if it acts by similitude, etc.' — it is said: if he intends restriction, it is not sound, because heat also causes separation; if he intends absolute, then heaviness does its likeness in the common sense at sensation, just as blackness casts its image in the eye. And it is answered: that the conveyance of heaviness to the common sense is a branch of its being sensible by the outer sense, and that is the beginning of the issue, so consider. His saying: 'Unlike heaviness, etc.' — from the exclusion of heaviness is understood the exclusion of lightness, and for this the Imam said: 'And this is a clear statement from the Shaykh excluding heaviness and lightness.' For if you say: lightness, for example, is either ascending repulsion or its origin, and whichever it is, it may give the body adjacent to its place the repulsion or its origin, like the hand placed on an inflated leather bag placed under water. I say: lightness is the natural repulsion or its origin, and it does not give the adjacent body either of them.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's definition of sensible qualities: they are those that act by producing a likeness of themselves in what they affect. Heat makes an object hot, blackness leaves an image. He contrasts this with heaviness, which causes movement but does not transmit heaviness. Imam al-Razi notes that this excludes weight and lightness from sensible qualities. The commentator then clarifies each phrase: 'removing the burden of alternation' means the classification via alternation is for ease; the 'image' is not a perceptual form but a physical qualification; 'action on the body' refers to the heavy body's own body; 'repulsion' is natural, not heaviness. He addresses an objection: if Ibn Sina meant to restrict, it fails because heat also separates; if he meant absolutely, then heaviness does produce a likeness in the common sense. The answer is that the common sense receives it because it is already sensible by the external senses. Finally, the exclusion of heaviness implies the exclusion of lightness, and an objection about lightness generating repulsion is answered: lightness is a natural tendency and does not confer that property on the adjacent object.

Key Terms
الكيف
التشبيه
المحسوس
الثقل
الخفة
الاستقراء
الترديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All physical properties are sensible qualities because they can be perceived.
Correction: Not all properties perceived are sensible in Ibn Sina's technical sense. He defines sensible qualities specifically as those that act by similitude—producing a likeness of themselves. Heaviness and lightness are perceived but they do not make adjacent objects heavy or light; they cause motion instead.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what distinguishes a sensible quality from others?
A) It is perceptible by the senses.
B) It acts by producing a likeness of itself.
C) It is quantitative.
D) It is a power.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina defines sensible qualities as those that act by producing a likeness of themselves, which excludes weight and lightness because they cause motion rather than replicating themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina says a sensible quality replicates itself in what it affects. Think of the qualities you display in your daily interactions—anger, patience, honesty. Do these qualities produce a likeness in those around you? Specifically, has your patience made others more patient, or has your anger ignited anger in others? What one quality can you intentionally cultivate today to leave a beneficial imprint on someone else?
?
Heaviness causes motion but does not make other bodies heavy. Consider your spiritual efforts: are you merely 'moving' through acts of worship without those acts transforming your inner state to resemble the Prophet's character? Name one ritual you perform (like prayer or charity) that has not yet produced a deep, abiding quality of humility or generosity in your heart. What will you change so that your deeds act by similitude, shaping your soul?

Second Classification of Qualities and the Similitude Objection
Second classification of qualities and objection against the similitude criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second classification of qualities into quantitative, bodily-natural, and psychic, and the objection that the claim that all sensible qualities act by similitude faces counterexamples like heaviness and lightness.

Core Idea
The classification of qualities is challenged: heaviness and lightness are sensible but don't act by similitude.

After the first classification, the author presents a second way to divide qualities. However, this division is immediately questioned: the criterion that all sensible qualities act by similitude seems false, as heaviness and lightness are sensible but do not act by similitude. This lesson unpacks the classification, the objection, and the response that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the flow from classification to objection to response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three categories of qualities
The passage distinguishes qualities related to quantity, to the body as natural (active/passive powers), and to the body as having a soul (psychic). Find these in the text.
The classification is part of the second way.
3
Understand the objection and the response
The objector argues that heaviness and lightness refute the claim that all sensible qualities act by similitude. The responder explains that the initial statement was just a method of regulation, and that if action by similitude is known, the quality is sensible.
The response also addresses Ibn Sina's doubt about wetness and dryness.

The category, and this as you see is a contradiction between his two statements. (Otherwise) if it does not act by similitude, then if it is related to quantity, that is what is specific to quantities; (otherwise) if it is not related to quantity, then (it belongs to the body), meaning its existence for the body is either in terms of it being a natural body only, which is active and passive power, i.e., preparedness, or (psychic), meaning in terms of it being a body possessing a soul, and it is specific to beings with souls. We said: why did you say that all sensible qualities act by similitude? That is not conceded; how (is it the case)? And this universal judgment is refuted by heaviness and lightness, as you know. And why did you say that other than them, i.e., non-sensible qualities, are not like that? That is, they do not act by similitude, for it is unknown. Also, Ibn Sina in his natural philosophy of al-Shifa admitted that he did not prove the action of wetness and dryness by similitude. Therefore, the mentioned division is not valid because it necessitates allowing wetness and dryness to depart from sensible qualities. His saying: (contradiction between his two statements) — no contradiction, because the purpose at first is merely to show the method of regulation, as he stated, and the purpose at the end is to establish that they are among sensible things. His saying: (and it is refuted, etc.) — you already know its refutation. His saying: (for it is unknown) — if it is said: his intention is that if it is known that its action is by similitude, then it is sensible, and if it is not known, etc., then this objection is refuted.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents a second way of classifying qualities. It distinguishes qualities that are related to quantity, those that belong to bodies as natural (active and passive powers), and those that belong to bodies as having souls. The objection ('we said') challenges Ibn Sina's earlier claim that all sensible qualities act by similitude, pointing to weight and lightness as counterexamples, and noting that Ibn Sina himself did not prove that wetness and dryness act by similitude. The commentator responds that there is no contradiction, and that the objection is refuted if we understand that the definition only applies when the action is known to be by similitude.

The Third Classification of Qualities and Its Objection
Classification of qualities with an objection to the act-sensible link.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational classification of qualities and the objection that active qualities are not necessarily sensible.

Core Idea
Not all active qualities are sensible—an exception challenges the classification.

In this passage, the author presents a third way to classify qualities, based on their relation to the soul, quantity, and activity. He then raises a critical objection to one of its divisions, showing that the classification may not be exhaustive. Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dichotomous structure and the objection at the end.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the three branches
Trace the divisions: first split (related to soul or not), second split (related to quantity or not), third split (preparedness or act). Understand what each branch covers.
The third split is within the 'not related to quantity' branch.
3
Analyze the objection
Focus on the final sentence. The objection challenges the claim that the 'act' branch equals sensible qualities. Why might a quality be active yet not sensible?
4
Reflect on classification completeness
Consider the implication: if a counterexample exists, the classification fails to capture all qualities. How might one defend or revise it?
This passage is a typical kalam critique of a philosophical taxonomy.

(الثالث) من وجوه الحصر وهو أيضاً مذكور في الشفاء ( أنْ يقال : الكيف) إما أن يتعلق بوجود النفس، وذلك بأن يكون للنفوس أو للأجسام من حيث إنها ذوات النفوس ( أولاً ) يتعلق بوجود النفس ( والثاني إما أن يتعلق بالكمية أو لا يتعلق بها. The second is either related to quantity or not related to it. The second is either preparedness or act. We said: why did you say that the last, i.e., the act, is the sensible qualities? Commentary
This is the third classification attempt, also from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. It divides qualities into those related to the soul (psychic qualities) and those not; those not related are either related to quantity or not; those not related to quantity are either active or passive (preparedness). The objection is that it does not follow that the active kind is always sensible, because a quality might be defined by its act without being sensed.

The Fourth Classification of Qualities and Its Rejection
Ibn Sina's fourth classification of qualities, with refutation and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth classification of qualities presented by Ibn Sina and why it was rejected due to an unproven premise.

Core Idea
The fourth classification collapses because its foundational premise is unproven.

In this passage, the author presents a fourth method of classifying qualities, taken from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. However, Ibn Sina himself refuted this classification. As we examine the classification tree, we will see why it fails and how the flaw is connected to earlier discussions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the classification tree
The passage divides qualities into two main branches (acting by similitude or not) and then subdivides the second branch. Find all divisions and note their labels.
The tree has three levels: first split, second split (relation to souls or bodies), third split (quantity or nature).
2
Identify the refutation
The author states the flaw is 'not hidden' and refers back to the second method: that it has not been proven that qualities act by similitude. Understand how this premise affects the entire classification.
The refutation is concise but decisive.
3
Examine the examples and objection
The commentator provides examples of psychic qualities (knowledge, power) and bodily qualities (life, pain) to illustrate the classification. Then he addresses an objection about a quality having two aspects, but dismisses it.
The objection is considered invalid because it reduces to the second division.

Heat's Primary Action: Rarefaction and Its Consequences
Defines heat's primary action as rarefaction and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how heat's primary action of rarefaction necessarily leads to both separation of heterogeneous parts and aggregation of homogeneous parts, and how these effects are categorized.

Core Idea
Heat's first action is rarefaction, which inevitably separates and then reunites parts.

This passage from a classical philosophical text explores the fundamental action of heat. It builds on earlier discussions, establishing that heat's primary effect is rarefaction, which then triggers a chain of separation and aggregation. Understanding this helps explain natural processes like the behavior of composite bodies exposed to fire.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the technical terms for rarefaction, separation, and aggregation.
Pay attention to the parenthetical clarifications in the original.
2
Identify heat's first action
The passage explicitly states that heat's first action is rarefaction (التصعيد) and upward movement. Confirm this in the text.
3
Trace the necessary consequences
See how rarefaction leads to separation of heterogeneous parts and then aggregation of homogeneous parts. The passage uses a concrete example with fire and a composite body.
The order matters: first separation, then aggregation.
4
Understand the two categories
The passage distinguishes between effects in the category of quality (rarefaction/condensation) and position (rarefaction/condensation). Notice how each is defined and opposed.
Quality deals with consistency (thinness/thickness), position with arrangement of parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على ما علم مما قررناه في تفسير الحرارة. وهو أنْ يقال: (الفعل الأول لها أي للحرارة هو (التصعيد) والتحريك إلى الفوق بسبب ما تفيده من الميل المصعد) والجمع والتفريق لازمان (له فإنه إذا حدثت الحرارة في الجسم المركب بمجاورة النار مثلا تحرك الأقبل للتصعيد قبل الأبطأ، وتحرك الأبطأ قبل العاصي فيلزم من هذا تفرق تلك الأجزاء المتخالفة ثم أجناسها بمقتضى طباعها كما مر. ( ولذلك ) أي ولما ذكرنا من اجتماعها مع أن الفعل الأول للحرارة هو التصعيد المستتبع للتفريق والجمع. ( وتحدث ) أي ومن أحوال الحرارة أنها تحدث ) تخلخلاً من باب الكيف، وهو رقة القوام، ويقابله باب الكيف وهو غلظ القوام . (و) تحدث أيضاً ( تكاثفاً من باب الوضع ) وهو اندماج الأجزاء المتحدة بالطبع واجتماعها بحيث يخرج الجسم الغريب عما بينها، ويقابله التخلخل من باب الوضع وهو أن تنتفش تلك الأجزاء ويداخلها الجسم الغريب لتحليله الكثيف وتصعيده اللطيف ( هذا نشر لما تقدم فإنَّ الحرارة تحلل الكثيف المتجمد فتفيد الجسم رقة القوام وتصعد اللطيف وتخرجه من بين أجزاء الكثيف فينضم اللطيف إلى جنسه وتجتمع أجزاء الكثيف أيضاً، فيحدث التكاثف من باب الوضع في كل منهما).
According to what we have established in the explanation of heat. It is said: (The first action of it, i.e., of heat, is rarefaction and upward movement due to the upward inclination it imparts). And aggregation and separation are necessary concomitants of it, for if heat occurs in a composite body by contact with fire, for example, the part more inclined to rarefaction moves before the slower, and the slower moves before the resistant, and from this follows the separation of those heterogeneous parts, then their kinds according to their natures as previously mentioned. (Therefore) i.e., because of what we mentioned of its conjunction with that the first action of heat is rarefaction entailing separation and aggregation. (And it produces) i.e., among the states of heat is that it produces rarefaction in the category of quality, which is thinness of consistency, and opposite to it is in the category of quality which is thickness of consistency. And it also produces condensation in the category of position, which is the fusion of parts united by nature and their gathering such that the foreign body exits from between them, and opposite to it is rarefaction in the category of position, which is that those parts expand and the foreign body enters into them, by its dissolving the dense and rarefying the fine. This is an elaboration of what preceded: heat dissolves the dense and frozen, thus imparting to the body thinness of consistency, and rarefies the fine and expels it from between the dense parts, so the fine joins its kind and the dense parts also come together, thus condensation in the category of position occurs in each of them.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage defines the primary action of heat and its necessary consequences. It begins by restating the established explanation: the first action of heat is rarefaction (التصعيد) and upward movement, due to the upward inclination it produces. Then it explains that separation (التفريق) and aggregation (الجمع) are necessary concomitants of this action. The reasoning is: when heat affects a composite body (like a body exposed to fire), the parts that are more prone to rarefaction move upward first, then the slower, then the resistant, causing heterogeneous parts to separate, and then their kinds (similar parts) come together according to their natures. The author then notes that this is why the first action of heat—rarefaction—entails separation and aggregation. Next, the passage describes two types of effects: rarefaction in the category of quality (تخلخل من باب الكيف), which is thinness of consistency, and its opposite is thickness of consistency (غلظ القوام). Heat also produces condensation in the category of position (تكاثف من باب الوضع), which is the fusion of parts that are naturally united, causing the foreign body to exit from between them. The opposite is rarefaction in the category of position, where the parts expand and the foreign body enters, dissolving the dense and rarefying the fine. The passage concludes by elaborating: heat dissolves the dense and frozen, making the body thin in consistency; it rarefies the fine and expels it from between the dense parts; the fine then joins its kind, and the dense parts also gather, resulting in positional condensation in each.

Key Terms
التصعيد
التفريق
الجمع
التخلخل
التكاثف
باب الكيف
باب الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heat only separates things or only joins them.
Correction: The passage shows that heat's primary action of rarefaction necessarily leads to both separation of heterogeneous parts and aggregation of homogeneous parts; it does both in a sequence.

Checkpoint

A) Separation of heterogeneous parts
B) Aggregation of homogeneous parts
C) Rarefaction and upward movement
D) Condensation in the category of position

Key Takeaway
Heat's fundamental action is rarefaction, which inevitably causes separation of unlike parts followed by gathering of like parts, with effects manifesting in both quality and position.

?
