Proof for the Cause of Resistance
The author provides a rational proof for the cause of resistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the cause of resistance (mabda' al-mudafa'a) is not known by sensation but requires rational proof, and how the example of two stones demonstrates its existence.

Core Idea
Resistance is sensed, but its cause must be proven by reason.

The author has already mentioned sensory examples like heavy stones and waterskins to illustrate resistance. But he now argues that these examples only prove the existence of resistance itself, not its underlying cause. To establish the cause, we need a rational argument, which he proceeds to provide through a thought experiment with two stones.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between resistance and its cause
Notice that the author begins by stating that the resistance itself is sensible, but its cause (mabda' al-mudafa'a) is not. This sets up the need for a proof.
The word 'mabda' can be translated as 'principle' or 'origin'.
2
Understand the thought experiment
Read the author's example: two stones of different sizes thrown from the same hand with the same force over the same distance. Ask yourself: why would they differ in speed?
3
Eliminate alternative explanations
The author systematically rules out the agent, external impediments in the distance, and internal resistance (assuming no cause). Observe how each is eliminated.
4
Recognize the logical necessity
Since the stones do differ in speed, there must be something internal that varies. That something is the cause of resistance, which the author identifies as al-i'timad (reliance).
This conclusion is reached by process of elimination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الذي ذكروه إنما يتم في نفس المدافعة فإنها محسوسة معلومة الوجود بالضرورة دون مبدأ المدافعة فإنه ليس محسوساً بل يحتاج في وجوده إلى دليل فلذلك قال وأما إثبات أمر يوجبه أي يوجب المدافعة على تذكير ضمير المصدر فلأنه لولاه أي لولا ذلك الأمر الذي يوجبها لم يختلف في السرعة والبطء الحجران المرميان من يد واحدة في مسافة واحدة بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر وإذ ليس بالضرورة فيهما مدافعة إلى خلاف جهة الحركة حتى تكون مدافعة الكبير أقوى فتوجب بطء الحركة ومدافعة الصغير أضعف فلا توجبه ولا مبدأها أي وليس أيضا على ذلك التقدير مبدأ المدافعة فيجب أن لا يختلف حركتاهما أصلاً لأن هذا الاختلاف لا يكون باعتبار الفاعل لأنه متحد فرضاً ولا باعتبار معاوق خارجي في المسافة لاتحادهما ولا باعتبار معاوق داخلي إذ ليس فيهما مدافعة ولا مبدأها ولا معاوق داخلي غيرهما فوجب تساويهما في السرعة أو
And this which they mentioned is only valid concerning the resistance itself, for it is sensible, known to exist by necessity, but not concerning the cause of resistance (mabda' al-mudafa'a), for it is not sensible but rather needs proof for its existence. Therefore he said: 'As for affirming something that necessitates it (yujibuhu), i.e., necessitates resistance, with the masculine pronoun referring to the source (masdar), then because if it were not for it, i.e., if it were not for that thing which necessitates it, the two stones thrown from one hand in one distance with one force would not differ in speed and slowness when they differ in smallness and largeness, and since there is not necessarily in them resistance towards the opposite direction of movement such that the resistance of the larger is stronger and thus necessitates slowness of movement and the resistance of the smaller is weaker and thus does not necessitate it, nor its cause (mabda'uha), i.e., also on that assumption there is no cause of resistance, then it would be necessary that their two movements do not differ at all, because this difference cannot be due to the agent since it is assumed to be one, nor due to an external impediment in the distance since it is the same for both, nor due to an internal impediment since there is in them neither resistance nor its cause nor any other internal impediment. So they must be equal in speed or...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial distinction: the sensory examples (heavy stone, waterskin) only prove the existence of resistance (al-mudafa'a) itself, not its cause (mabda' al-mudafa'a). The existence of the cause requires a rational proof. The author then provides that proof: consider two stones thrown from the same hand with the same force over the same distance, differing in size. If there were no cause of resistance (mabda' al-mudafa'a), then there would be no internal factor to account for a difference in speed. The agent is the same, the external resistance (air) is the same, and since we are assuming no internal resistance or its cause, the stones should move at the same speed. But we observe that they differ. Therefore, there must be something internal (the mabda' al-mudafa'a) that causes the larger stone to resist more, slowing it down. This establishes the existence of a cause of resistance, i.e., al-i'timad, as an existent attribute distinct from the resistance itself.

Key Terms
مبدأ المدافعة
سبب
سرعة
بطء
حجر
معاوق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory examples like heavy stones falling slower already prove the existence of al-i'timad (the cause of resistance).
Correction: The author clarifies that sensory examples only prove the effect (resistance), not its cause. A rational proof is needed to establish the cause.

Checkpoint

B) Because there must be a cause of internal resistance that differs between them
C) Because the air resistance acts differently on each
D) Because the hand imparts different force

Key Takeaway
The cause of resistance (mabda' al-mudafa'a) is not perceptible but must be inferred rationally from observed differences in motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the cause of resistance is not sensed but proven by reason. Where in your own life do you rely solely on what you perceive while ignoring the unseen causes that reason demands? What would it cost you to start seeking those causes?
?
If you truly accept that every observed effect has a hidden cause known only through rational inference, how would that reshape the way you interpret your own hardships and blessings? Are you willing to trace every trial back to its true cause, even when it demands more of you than mere observation?
?
The proof forces you to admit that difference demands a cause. When you see disparity in your own spiritual state—between what you know and what you do—do you stop at the surface or do you press reason to find the internal cause? What might you find if you did not settle for the obvious?

Proof from Two Stones and al-Rāzī's Response
Al-Jurjānī presents a proof for a cause of resistance and al-Rāzī's counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument for a principle of resistance in bodies, al-Rāzī's objection that nature suffices, and the ensuing debate on terminology.

Core Idea
The speed difference of two stones implies an internal resisting principle, but is that principle simply nature itself?

Al-Jurjānī devises a thought experiment: two stones of different sizes thrown with equal force. If no internal resistance existed, their speeds would match. Observation says they differ, so a resisting principle must exist. But al-Rāzī counters that nature itself provides this resistance, stronger in larger bodies. The debate continues over what to call this principle and whether it is a distinct quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Start by reading the passage from the beginning up to where al-Rāzī's response begins. Note the assumptions: same hand, same force, same distance, stones differ only in size. The argument eliminates external factors and internal resistance other than a principle, concluding that without such a principle the speeds would be equal.
The proof is a classic argument by elimination.
2
Examine al-Rāzī's counter-argument
Read al-Rāzī's response. He argues that nature (al-ṭabīʿa) itself is the internal resistant, and that the nature of a larger body is stronger because it is a power divided throughout the body. Therefore, no separate principle like mayl or iʿtimād is needed.
Al-Rāzī is not denying resistance, but its cause.
3
Understand the gloss on the response
The first gloss explains that al-Rāzī's answer refutes the claim that there is no internal resistant other than the stones themselves. The second gloss addresses the terminological issue: whether the principle should be called mayl or iʿtimād, and whether it is a quality (kayf) or substance (jawhar).
The gloss is the commentator's clarification.
4
Reflect on the terminological debate
Consider why naming matters. The objector says mayl and iʿtimād are far-fetched for nature. The commentator argues that if nature is a substance, calling it a quality is problematic, but those who identify mayl with nature don't concede this. The debate shows the intersection of physics and ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثبات أمر يوجبه) أي يوجب المدافعة على تذكير ضمير المصدر ( فلأنه لولاه ) أي لولا ذلك الأمر الذي يوجبها (لم يختلف ) في السرعة والبطء ( الحجران المرميان من يد واحدة ) في مسافة واحدة بقوة واحدة ( إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر وإذ ليس بالضرورة (فيهما مدافعة إلى خلاف جهة الحركة ) حتى تكون مدافعة الكبير أقوى فتوجب بطء الحركة ومدافعة الصغير أضعف فلا توجبه ) ولا مبدأها ) أي وليس أيضا على ذلك التقدير مبدأ المدافعة فيجب أن لا يختلف حركتاهما أصلاً، لأن هذا الاختلاف لا يكون باعتبار الفاعل لأنه متحد فرضاً ولا باعتبار معاوق خارجي في المسافة لاتحادهما، ولا باعتبار معاوق داخلي إذ ليس فيهما مدافعة ولا مبدأها ولا معاوق داخلي غيرهما، فوجب تساويهما في السرعة أو البطء . وأجاب عنه الإمام الرازي: بأن الطبيعة معاوقة للحركة القسرية، ولا شك أن طبيعة الأكبر أقوى لأنها قوة سارية في الجسم منقسمة بانقسامه، فلذلك كانت حركته أبطأ فلم يلزم مما ذكر أن يكون للمدافعة مبدأ مغاير للطبيعة حتى يسمى بالميل والاعتماد وأما تسميتها بهما فبعيدة جداً . قوله : ( وأجاب عنه إلخ منع لقوله : ولا معاوق داخلي غيرها . قوله : ( وأما تسميتها إلخ) دفع لما يقال : المقصود إثبات مبدأ المدافعة أعم من أن تكون الطبيعة أو غبرها يعني إطلاق الميل والاعتماد على الطبيعة بعيد وفيه أن البعد من حيث اللغة مسلم ولا يضر. ومن حيث الاصطلاح ممنوع وما قيل في وجه البعد من أن الطبيعة جوهر فلا يكون من مقولة الكيف ففيه أن كون الميل بمعنى مبدأ المدافعة من الكيف غير مسلم عند من يقول : بأنه نفس الطبيعة وأنه لو تم هذا الوجه لدل على الامتناع لا على البعد .
As for proving something that necessitates it, meaning necessitates resistance (al-mudāfaʿa), (the author says): because if not for it, i.e., if not for that thing which necessitates it, the two stones thrown from one hand over one distance with one force would not differ in speed and slowness, if they differ in smallness and bigness, and there is not necessarily in them resistance opposite to the direction of motion, so that the resistance of the larger would be stronger and thus cause slowness of motion, and the resistance of the smaller would be weaker and not cause it. And there is no principle of it (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa) either, i.e., on that assumption there is no principle of resistance, so their motions would necessarily not differ at all, because this difference cannot be attributed to the agent since it is assumed to be one, nor to an external resistant in the distance because of their unity, nor to an internal resistant since there is no resistance or its principle in them, nor any internal resistant other than them. So they would be necessarily equal in speed or slowness. And Imam al-Rāzī answered him: by saying that nature (al-ṭabīʿa) opposes forced motion (al-ḥaraka al-qasriyya), and there is no doubt that the nature of the larger is stronger because it is a power pervading the body and divided by its division, therefore its motion is slower. So it does not follow from what was mentioned that resistance has a principle different from nature, to the extent that it is called inclination (al-mayl) and reliance (al-iʿtimād). And as for naming them by these two terms, it is very far-fetched. [Gloss:] His statement: 'and he answered him etc.' is a refutation of his statement: 'and no internal resistant other than them'. [Gloss:] His statement: 'and as for naming them etc.' is a rebuttal of what is said: the purpose is to prove a principle of resistance, whether it is nature or something else. That is, applying the terms inclination and reliance to nature is far-fetched; but the distance in terms of language is conceded and does no harm, while in terms of convention it is denied. And what is said in justification of the distance, that nature is a substance (jawhar) and therefore does not belong to the category of quality (kayf), is met with: that inclination in the sense of the principle of resistance being a quality is not conceded by those who say it is nature itself, and even if this argument were valid, it would indicate impossibility (imtināʿ) not merely distance (buʿd).
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a principle (mabdaʾ) that necessitates resistance (al-mudāfaʿa) in bodies, followed by al-Rāzī's critique and a discussion of terminology. The proof begins by assuming two stones thrown with the same force over the same distance, differing only in size. If there were no cause of resistance, their speeds would be equal because the agent is the same, the medium is the same, and there is no internal resistance. Observation contradicts this: the larger stone moves slower. This shows there must be a cause of resistance that is stronger in the larger stone. Al-Rāzī responds that this cause is simply nature itself: nature opposes forced motion, and the nature of a larger body is stronger because it is more extended and divided. Therefore, no separate entity called 'inclination' (mayl) or 'reliance' (iʿtimād) is needed. The commentator then addresses the objection that the term 'mayl' is usually a quality, but nature is a substance. He argues that this is not conceded by those who identify mayl with nature, and that even if true, it would show impossibility, not mere inadequacy of terminology. The glosses clarify that al-Rāzī's answer refutes the claim that there is no internal resistant other than the stones, and that the debate is about the nature of the principle, not its existence.

الطبيعة (al-ṭabīʿa)
الميل (al-mayl)
الاعتماد (al-iʿtimād)
الحركة القسرية (al-ḥaraka al-qasriyya)
جوهر (jawhar)
كيف (kayf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the term 'mayl' (inclination) must refer to a separate physical quality distinct from nature, and that al-Rāzī's response fails to account for the observed difference.
Correction: Al-Rāzī shows that nature itself can produce the effect, and the gloss clarifies that identifying mayl with nature is a valid position, though terminologically debated.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Rāzī argue that the difference in speed between two stones does not require a separate principle of resistance (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa)?
A) Because the larger stone has a stronger nature that opposes forced motion more.
B) Because the two stones are identical in all respects except size.
C) Because resistance is impossible in forced motion.
D) Because the agent is the same for both stones.

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether the principle of resistance is a distinct quality or nature itself is a central issue in classical physics, with al-Rāzī arguing that nature alone can explain the observed difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall short in obeying Allah, do you excuse yourself by saying 'it's just my nature'? This passage shows that nature itself is the resistant. What would it mean to transform your nature rather than accommodate it?
?
The proof from the stones reveals that internal resistance shows itself in visible differences. What visible signs in your spiritual life reveal an internal resistance slowing your progress toward Allah? Will you continue to let that resistance define you, or will you seek help to overcome it?
?
Al-Rāzī argued that nature is the cause of slower motion. If your nature is inclined toward sin, what is the source of your spiritual motion toward good? Is it Allah alone, or do you rely on something within yourself? Commit today to relying solely on Allah's guidance and mercy to move you forward.

The Proof That Up and Down Are Real Directions
Al-Jurjani argues that up and down are real directions using natural tendencies and non-reversibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that up and down are the only two natural directions among the six absolute directions.

Core Idea
Up and down are fixed by nature, not by human orientation.

In this passage, al-Jurjani establishes that only up and down are real directions because they are naturally distinct. He provides two pieces of evidence: the natural tendencies of elemental bodies and the fact that these directions do not change when a person is inverted. This foundational argument sets the stage for later discussions on natural inclination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: definition, then two proofs.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
Locate the proof from elemental bodies (fire/air rise, water/earth fall) and the proof from inversion (no reversal of up/down).
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does it matter that up and down are the only real directions? It grounds the concept of natural inclination (i'timad).
4
Consider the counterarguments
The next lesson will address an objection about relativity; for now, absorb the main proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل
الفوق والتحت أعني من الجهات المطلقة جهتان حقيقيتان لأنهما جهتان متمایزتان بالطبع فإنَّ بعض الأجسام العنصرية بطبعها تطلب الفوق وتهرب عن التحت كالنار والهواء وبعضها بالعكس كالأرض والماء ،وأيضاً ، فهاتان الجهتان لا تتبدلان أصلاً فإن القائم إذا صار منكوساً لم يصر ما يلي رأسه فوقاً وما يلي رجله تحتاً، بل صار رأسه من تحت ورجله من فوق وكان الفوق والتحت بحالهما، وما ذكر من حال المستلقي يخرج الفوق أو التحت عن كونه فوقاً أو تحتاً بل يصير وجهه إلى الفوق وقفاه إلى التحت نعم يتصف التحت والفوق حينئذ بوصفين آخرين اعتباريين أعني كونهما قداماً ،وخلفاً، وأما باقي الجهات فلا تمايز بينهما بالطبع، وهي متبدلة بحسب الفرض كما مر. وقد يقال : إذا فسر الفوق والتحت بما يلي السماء والأرض لم يتصور فيهما تبدل بخلاف ما إذا فسر بمايلي رأس الإنسان وقدمه بالطبع فإنهما يتبدلان حينئذ، كما إذا قام شخصان على طرفي قطر واحد من الأرض فإن رأس كل واحد منهما وقدمه على المجرى الطبيعي، مع أن الجانب الذي يلي رأس أحدهما يلي قدم الآخر، فيكون ذلك الجانب فوقاً بالقياس إلى الأول وتحتاً بالقياس إلى الثاني. ويجاب بأنَّ قولنا : بالطبع ليس صفة للقدم والرأس بل هو متعلق بالفعل المذكور ومعناه أنَّ لرأس كل شخص وقدمه نسبة طبيعية مع الجهة في الولي والقرب . ولا شك أنا إذا فرضنا قدم أحد هذين الشخصين حيث رأس الآخر لم يكن على المجرى الطبيعي، بل كان ذلك انتكاساً له، وإذا ثبت أنَّ الجهة الحقيقية اثنتان فالاعتماد الطبيعي أيضاً. كما سيأتي
قوله : (ليس صفة للقدم والرأس بأن يكون ظرفاً مستقراً واقعاً موقع الحال عنهما قوله : (بل هو متعلق إلخ) أي ظرف لغو يفيد التقييد به كون الولي والقرب طبيعياً . قوله : ( وإذا ثبت إلخ) بيان لارتباط قوله : واعلم إلى قوله بل الحق بما قبله من بيان
أحكام الاعتماد.
قيل : هي مقعر فلك القمر. والأول هو الصحيح لأن الإشارة إذا تعدت من فلك القمر كان إلى جهة الفوق قطعاً لكونها آخذة من جهة التحت متوجهة إلى ما يقابلها .
قوله : ( إذ يمكن اعتبار انتهاء الإشارة والحركة إليها فإنك إذا أشرت إلى طرف المكعب كسطح من سطوحه مثلاً فإنه ينتهي إشارتك إليه وإذا فرضنا حركة جسم فيه فإنه إذا نفذ فيه المتحرك وتحرك تنتهي حركته الممتدة من ذلك السطح النافذ هو فيه إلى سطحه الآخر
المقابل .
قوله : ( ومعناه أن لرأس كل شخص إلخ) قيل : حق العبارة على هذا التوجيه أن يقال : ما
yليه رأس الإنسان وقدمه بالطبع، فليتأمل.
The third observatory – the third purpose: on i'timad (inclination), which is inclination. Up and down, meaning from among the absolute directions, are two real directions because they are two directions naturally distinct. For some elemental bodies naturally seek up and flee from down, like fire and air, and some the opposite, like earth and water. Also, these two directions do not change at all. For if a standing person becomes inverted, what is next to his head does not become up and what is next to his foot does not become down; rather his head becomes below and his foot above, and up and down remain as they are. What is mentioned about the lying-down person does not remove up or down from being up or down; rather his face becomes toward up and his back toward down. Yes, down and up are then described by two other relational attributes, namely being front and back. As for the remaining directions, there is no natural distinction between them, and they change according to assumption, as was mentioned. And it may be said: if up and down are interpreted as what is next to the sky and the earth, then no change is conceivable in them, unlike if interpreted as what is next to a person's head and foot by nature, for then they change. For example, if two persons stand at the two ends of one diameter of the earth, the head and foot of each are on the natural course, yet the side that is next to the head of one is next to the foot of the other, so that side is up relative to the first and down relative to the second. The answer is that our saying 'by nature' is not a description of the foot and head, but it is related to the mentioned action, meaning that each person's head and foot have a natural relation with the direction in terms of proximity and closeness. There is no doubt that if we suppose the foot of one of these two persons where the head of the other is, it is not on the natural course; rather that is an inversion for him. And when it is established that the real directions are two, then the natural inclination is also two, as will come. His saying: (not a description of the foot and head) meaning that it is an adverbial phrase of state... His saying: (rather it is related etc.) meaning a loose adverbial that provides restriction... His saying: (and when it is established etc.) is an explanation of the connection... It was said: it (the direction of up) is the concave of the sphere of the moon. And the first (interpretation) is correct, because if the indication extends beyond the sphere of the moon, it is definitely toward the direction of up, since it starts from the direction of down and heads toward its opposite. His saying: (since it is possible to consider the termination of indication and motion to it...). His saying: (and its meaning is that for each person's head etc.) It was said: the correct phrase for this explanation is to say: what is next to a person's head and foot by nature, so reflect.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a proof that 'up' and 'down' are the only truly real directions among the six directions. The author begins by stating that up and down are two real directions because they are naturally distinct. He gives two pieces of evidence: first, elemental bodies have natural tendencies toward up (like fire and air) or down (like earth and water). Second, these directions are non-reversible: if a person is inverted, his head is still below and his feet above, so up and down remain fixed. He then notes that other directions (front, back, right, left) are not naturally distinct and change with perspective. An objection is raised: if up and down are defined relative to sky and earth they are fixed, but if defined relative to a person's head and foot they change, as shown by two persons on opposite sides of the earth, where the same direction is up for one and down for the other. The author answers that 'by nature' does not describe the head and foot themselves but the relation of the head and foot to the direction; thus relative inversion does not negate the absolute reality of up and down. The conclusion is that since there are only two real directions, natural inclination (i'timad) is also only two (upward and downward). The passage then includes a brief aside: it is said that up is the concave of the lunar sphere, but the first definition (what is above the earth) is correct because pointing beyond the moon is definitely toward up. The commentary that follows elaborates on the wording of the answer.

فوق
تحت
جهة حقيقية
بالطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All six directions are equally real and relative.
Correction: Only up and down are real directions because they are naturally distinct and non-reversible.

Key Takeaway
Up and down are the only real directions because they are naturally distinct and non-relative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that up and down are absolute because they are fixed by nature, independent of your perspective. Where in your life do you anchor your sense of direction (spiritually or morally) on something absolute like this, rather than on shifting circumstances or your own orientation? What would it mean to invert your priorities and still recognize that truth remains unchanged?
?
The author argues that even when a person is inverted, up and down do not change—they remain fixed, and only the labels relative to the body shift. Think of a time when you felt completely 'turned upside down' by events. Did you recognize that some truths (like the reality of Allah's commands or the nature of good and evil) remained absolute, not relative to your situation? Or did you lose your sense of direction? How can you cultivate that unwavering anchor?
?
Consider the natural tendencies of the elements: fire goes up, earth goes down. What is the 'natural tendency' of your soul? Do you find yourself drawn upward toward spiritual realities or pulled downward by worldly attachments? What specific, concrete step will you take today to align your actions with your true nature as a servant of Allah?

Defending the Absoluteness of Up and Down
Al-Jurjani answers an objection about relativity and defines up as the concave of the lunar sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the absoluteness of up and down against the objection of relativity based on different observers and how he defines the direction of up.

Core Idea
'By nature' describes the relation, not the body part, preserving absoluteness.

An objection arises: if up and down are defined relative to a person's head and foot, they become relative because two persons facing opposite sides of the earth would have opposite up directions. Al-Jurjani answers by clarifying that 'by nature' modifies the relation of head and foot to the direction, not the head and foot themselves. He then adds a definitional note: up is the concave of the lunar sphere. This refinement upholds the reality of up and down.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand why defining up/down by head and foot might lead to relativity when two persons stand on opposite sides of the earth.
2
Study the answer
Note how 'by nature' is interpreted to refer to the relation between body parts and direction, not the body parts themselves.
3
Examine the definitional note
Read the note about up being the concave of the lunar sphere and why the first definition (relative to sky/earth) is preferred.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The answer and definition together support the conclusion that there are only two real directions, hence two natural inclinations.

The third observatory – the third purpose: on i'timad (inclination), which is inclination. Up and down, meaning from among the absolute directions, are two real directions because they are two directions naturally distinct. For some elemental bodies naturally seek up and flee from down, like fire and air, and some the opposite, like earth and water. Also, these two directions do not change at all. For if a standing person becomes inverted, what is next to his head does not become up and what is next to his foot does not become down; rather his head becomes below and his foot above, and up and down remain as they are. What is mentioned about the lying-down person does not remove up or down from being up or down; rather his face becomes toward up and his back toward down. Yes, down and up are then described by two other relational attributes, namely being front and back. As for the remaining directions, there is no natural distinction between them, and they change according to assumption, as was mentioned. And it may be said: if up and down are interpreted as what is next to the sky and the earth, then no change is conceivable in them, unlike if interpreted as what is next to a person's head and foot by nature, for then they change. For example, if two persons stand at the two ends of one diameter of the earth, the head and foot of each are on the natural course, yet the side that is next to the head of one is next to the foot of the other, so that side is up relative to the first and down relative to the second. The answer is that our saying 'by nature' is not a description of the foot and head, but it is related to the mentioned action, meaning that each person's head and foot have a natural relation with the direction in terms of proximity and closeness. There is no doubt that if we suppose the foot of one of these two persons where the head of the other is, it is not on the natural course; rather that is an inversion for him. And when it is established that the real directions are two, then the natural inclination is also two, as will come. His saying: (not a description of the foot and head) meaning that it is an adverbial phrase of state... His saying: (rather it is related etc.) meaning a loose adverbial that provides restriction... His saying: (and when it is established etc.) is an explanation of the connection... It was said: it (the direction of up) is the concave of the sphere of the moon. And the first (interpretation) is correct, because if the indication extends beyond the sphere of the moon, it is definitely toward the direction of up, since it starts from the direction of down and heads toward its opposite. His saying: (since it is possible to consider the termination of indication and motion to it...). His saying: (and its meaning is that for each person's head etc.) It was said: the correct phrase for this explanation is to say: what is next to a person's head and foot by nature, so reflect.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a proof that 'up' and 'down' are the only truly real directions among the six directions. The author begins by stating that up and down are two real directions because they are naturally distinct. He gives two pieces of evidence: first, elemental bodies have natural tendencies toward up (like fire and air) or down (like earth and water). Second, these directions are non-reversible: if a person is inverted, his head is still below and his feet above, so up and down remain fixed. He then notes that other directions (front, back, right, left) are not naturally distinct and change with perspective. An objection is raised: if up and down are defined relative to sky and earth they are fixed, but if defined relative to a person's head and foot they change, as shown by two persons on opposite sides of the earth, where the same direction is up for one and down for the other. The author answers that 'by nature' does not describe the head and foot themselves but the relation of the head and foot to the direction; thus relative inversion does not negate the absolute reality of up and down. The conclusion is that since there are only two real directions, natural inclination (i'timad) is also only two (upward and downward). The passage then includes a brief aside: it is said that up is the concave of the lunar sphere, but the first definition (what is above the earth) is correct because pointing beyond the moon is definitely toward up. The commentary that follows elaborates on the wording of the answer.

Misconception: Up and down are relative because they change based on where you stand on earth.
Correction: Up and down are absolute; the change is only in the relative assignment of head and foot, not in the directions themselves.

Key Takeaway
The absoluteness of up and down is preserved by understanding 'by nature' as referring to the natural relation of body parts to directions, and up is defined as the direction away from the earth towards the sky beyond the lunar sphere.

?

?

The Three Opinions on I'timad and Their Coexistence
Al-Amidi defines i'timad and discusses the possibility of opposite inclinations coexisting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's definition of i'timad (inclination), the problem of opposite inclinations coexisting, and the three competing interpretations theologians have proposed.

Core Idea
Opposite inclinations can coexist, and theologians disagree on how.

In this passage, al-Amidi tackles a subtle issue in physics and theology: can two opposite inclinations exist in the same body? He first defines i'timad, then gives concrete examples showing that they can. He then presents the three main positions theologians have taken on the nature of these inclinations.

Guided Path
1
Define i'timad and the problem
Read the opening sentence. i'timad is defined as 'inclination.' Note the statement that opposites cannot coexist, yet examples show they do.
This sets up the philosophical tension.
2
Analyze the two examples
Examine the heavy stone and the rope examples. In each, two opposite inclinations are present. Understand how they illustrate the coexistence.
These are empirical observations.
3
Identify the three opinions
Al-Amidi lists three theological opinions: unification, multiplicity with opposition, multiplicity without opposition. Note how each explains the coexistence.
This is the doctrinal core of the passage.
4
Reflect on plausibility
Consider al-Amidi's remark that the third opinion is not less plausible than the first. Think about why theologians might favor one over another.
This connects to methodological preferences in kalam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل لامتناع اجتماع المتضادين (و) لكنها قد تجتمع لوجهين. الأول أن من جذب حجراً ثقيلاً إلى فوق فإنه يجد فيه مدافعة هابطة) وهو ظاهر ( والمتعلق به) أي بذلك الحجر ( من أسفل الجاذب له إِليه) أي إلى الأسفل ( يجد فيه مدافعة صاعدة ضرورة) فإنه يحس منه اعتماداً إلى جهة الفوق وميلاً غالباً له إليها. ( الثاني أنَّ الحبل الذي يتجاذبه اثنان) متقاومان (إلى جهتين فإنه يجد كل واحد منهما (فيه) اعتماداً و ( مقاومة إلى خلاف جهته فقد اجتمع فيه اعتمادان إلى جهتين وبمثل ذلك يعرف اجتماع الاعتمادات إلى الجهات الست في جسم واحد ثم قال الأمدي : ولو قلنا بالتعدد غير من تضاد أي لو قلنا: إنَّ الاعتمادات متعددة لكنها ليست متضادة فيجوز اجتماعها ( لم يكن هذا القول ( أبعد من القول بالاتحاد الذي اختاره القاضي فصارت الأقوال في الاعتمادات ثلاثة الاتحاد والتعدد مع التضاد وبدونه.
The third observatory - the third aim: concerning i'timad (dependence/inclination), which is inclination. Because of the impossibility of the coexistence of opposites, but they may coexist for two reasons. First: He who pulls a heavy stone upward finds in it a downward resistance, and that is apparent. The object attached to it (i.e., that stone) from below the one pulling it toward him (i.e., downward) finds in it an upward resistance necessarily. For he perceives from it an inclination toward the upward direction and a prevailing tendency toward it. Second: The rope that is pulled by two persons of equal strength toward two directions — each of them finds in it an inclination and a resistance toward the opposite of his direction. Thus two inclinations toward two directions have come together in it. By the like of this one knows the coming together of inclinations toward the six directions in one body. Then al-Amidi said: If we say multiplicity without opposition — i.e., if we say that the inclinations are multiple but they are not opposites, so their coexistence is permissible — this statement would not be further from the statement of unification which al-Qadi chose. Thus the opinions concerning inclinations became three: unification, multiplicity with opposition, and multiplicity without opposition.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines i'timad as inclination and addresses a key problem: how can two opposite inclinations coexist in one body? Al-Amidi first states the general rule that opposites cannot coexist, but then provides two examples where they do. The first example is a heavy stone pulled upward: the stone experiences a downward resistance due to its natural heaviness, while the person pulling feels an upward resistance from the stone. The second example is a rope pulled by two equally strong persons in opposite directions; each feels an inclination and resistance toward the other's direction. These examples show that two opposite inclinations can coexist in one body. Al-Amidi then extends this to inclinations toward all six directions in a single body. He then reports three opinions among theologians on how to interpret this: (1) unification (that there is only one inclination that manifests in different directions), (2) multiplicity with opposition (multiple inclinations that are opposite), and (3) multiplicity without opposition (multiple inclinations that are not truly opposite, thus can coexist). The passage ends by noting that the last opinion is not less plausible than the first.

Key Terms
الاعتماد (i'timad)
الميل (mayl)
المدافعة (mudafa'a)
المتضادان (al-mutadaddan)
الجهات الست (al-jihat al-sitt)
الاتحاد (al-ittihad)
التعدد مع التضاد (al-ta'addud ma'a al-tadadd)
التعدد بغير تضاد (al-ta'addud bidun tadadd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposite inclinations cannot coexist in the same body.
Correction: Al-Amidi provides two counterexamples showing that opposite inclinations can coexist, and theologians debated the nature of this coexistence.

Checkpoint

D) Five

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you face a situation where you feel both wanting to do good and feeling lazy, do you excuse yourself by saying 'I'm conflicted' or do you actively recognize the coexistence of opposite tendencies as a real phenomenon? What does that mean for your responsibility to act?
?
Al-Amidi says multiplicity without opposition is not less plausible. Have you ever ignored a valid perspective because it seemed 'further' from what you assume? What is one area of your deen where you might be dismissing a valid opinion due to superficial distancing?

The Two Rulings of Natural Inclination and Their Proof
Establishes the two rulings and provides a detailed proof for the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rulings for natural inclination and the logical proof that a body lacking natural inclination cannot move coercively or voluntarily.

Core Idea
Natural inclination governs all motion: without it, no natural, coercive, or voluntary motion is possible.

This passage introduces the foundational rulings for natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī). The first ruling is straightforward: a body without natural inclination does not move naturally. However, the second ruling—that such a body cannot move coercively or voluntarily either—requires a detailed proof. We will follow the reductio argument step by step, which uses a comparison of motions with different inclinations to establish that every motion has a definite speed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the opening sentence that states both rulings. The first ruling is immediate; the second is the subject of the proof.
The passage says: 'As for natural inclination, they established two rulings for it.'
2
Understand the definition of natural motion
The passage defines natural motion as having natural inclination as its proximate principle. This is key to the first ruling.
3
Follow the reductio proof for the second ruling
The proof assumes the contrary, then shows contradictions using the comparison of bodies with different inclinations moving the same distance.
Pay attention to the ratios of motions and inclinations.
4
Grasp the conclusion about speed limits
The proof concludes that every motion must have a specific limit of speed, which refutes the assumption that a body without natural inclination can move coercively.
This conclusion is used to support the second ruling.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : (جامع بين المسألتين) أي يفيد ثبوتهما معاً. قوله : ( أن كل حركة إلخ هذه المقدمة إلى قوله فإن كانت الحركة نفسانية غير مذكورة في كلام ذلك البعض بل المذكور ما نقلناه في بحث امتناع الخلاء. قوله : ( فإذا فرضت إلخ ) وإن كانت المفروضة مستحيلة فإنَّ إمكان فرضها كاف لنا في إثبات أن كل حركة نسبت إليها كانت موصوفة بحد من السرعة والبطء، فاندفع ما قيل: إنا لا نسلم إمكان وقوع حركة أخرى تقطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان مع نصف ذلك الزمان مع أنه لم يقتصر على وقوعها في النصف فقط بل ضم إليها وقوعها في الضعف أيضاً. ولا شك في إمكانه على أنا نقول : إمكان وقوع حركة أخرى نصفها في تلك المسافة كاف لنا في المطلوب لأنها إما واقعة في مثل زمانها، أو في أقل منه أو في أكثر منه فهي مساوية للحركة الأولى في حد من السرعة أو أسرع منه أو أبطأ فلا يمكن حركة إلا في حد من السرعة.

نفسها وكونه مبدأ قريباً للحركة الطبيعية بالنسبة إلى الطبيعة إذ لو أريد به نفس المدافعة لاحتيج في إثبات المطلوب إلى ضم مقدمة أخرى. وهي أنَّ الميل لا يمكن بدون مبدئه ثم هذه المقدمة وإن صحت إذا أريد بالمبدأ ما يعم الطبيعة لكن لا يتم التقريب لأن المقصود هاهنا إثبات الحكم المذكور لكيفية يكون الجسم بها مدافعاً لما يمنعه. وأما إذا أريد بالمبدأ نفس تلك الكيفية صحتها بحث لجواز أن يكون مبدأ لمدافعة نفس الطبيعة، بلا توسط ميل ثم لا يخفى ورود مثل هذا البحث على التوجيه الأول أيضاً فليتأمل.
As for natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī), they established two rulings for it. The first: that the one lacking it (i.e., lacking natural inclination, or rather its principle) does not move naturally—this is apparent—since there is no meaning to natural motion except that whose proximate principle is natural inclination; and he also does not move coercively or voluntarily. For if the one lacking the principle of natural inclination were to move by a coercive force, for example, in a certain distance in a time (because it is impossible to traverse a divisible distance in an instant, since the cutting of some of it is prior to the cutting of all of it), and let that time be assumed as an hour, and [it is supposed] that the one possessing the principle of natural inclination moves with that motive force in that specified distance and traverses it in more than that time due to the presence of an obstacle to motion—that is, the principle of natural inclination—and let that greater time be ten hours. Then for another, i.e., for another body whose inclination is a tenth of the inclination of the first body, that it moves in that distance with that motive force and traverses it in an hour as well, for the ratio of the two motions is like the ratio of the two opposing inclinations, which is one-tenth in the assumed example. So the motion with the slight obstacle is like the motion without obstacle in speed and slowness because they traversed one distance in one time. And you have already known the like of it, including the consideration in the issue of the void (al-khalāʾ); so transfer it to here. And some have in this context a comprehensive statement combining the two issues, summarized as: Every motion must necessarily have a specific limit of speed and slowness because it inevitably occurs over a distance and in a time. So if another motion is supposed to traverse that distance in half that time or double it, the first motion would be slower on the first estimation and faster on the second; thus no motion can exist except within a limit of speed.

His saying: 'combining the two issues' means it benefits the affirmation of both together. His saying: 'every motion, etc.' This premise up to his saying 'if the motion is psychic' is not mentioned in the statement of that person; rather what is mentioned is what we transmitted in the discussion of the impossibility of the void. His saying: 'so if it is assumed, etc.'—even if the assumed [motion] is impossible, the possibility of assuming it is sufficient for us to prove that every motion relative to it is described by a limit of speed and slowness. So what was said is refuted: 'We do not admit the possibility of the occurrence of another motion that traverses that distance in half that time'—and he did not confine it to occurrence in half only, but included occurrence in double as well. There is no doubt about its possibility. Moreover, we say: the possibility of the occurrence of another motion half of it in that distance is sufficient for our purpose, because it either occurs in a time equal to its time, or less than it, or more than it; thus it is equal to the first motion in a limit of speed, or faster than it, or slower. So there cannot be any motion except in a limit of speed.

Itself and its being a proximate principle of natural motion with respect to nature. For if by it was meant the very act of repulsion (al-mudāfaʿa), then, in proving the desired conclusion, another premise would need to be added—namely, that the inclination cannot exist without its principle. Then, although this premise is sound if by 'principle' is meant what includes nature, the argument is not complete because the purpose here is to prove the mentioned ruling concerning how the body can repel what hinders it. But if by 'principle' is meant that very quality, its validity is debatable, for it is possible that the principle of repulsion be nature itself, without the mediation of an inclination. Then it is not hidden that a similar objection occurs on the first interpretation as well; so let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-commentary section establishing two rulings for natural inclination (الميل الطبيعي). The first ruling is straightforward: a body lacking the principle of natural inclination does not move naturally, because natural motion is defined by having that principle as its proximate cause. The second ruling, that such a body also cannot move coercively or voluntarily, is the main focus. The proof proceeds by reductio: assume the contrary, i.e., a body lacking natural inclination moves coercively over a distance in some time. But a body with natural inclination, moving with the same force but hindered by its own natural tendency, takes longer (e.g., ten hours vs. one hour). Then a third body with weaker inclination (one-tenth) moves the same distance in the same time as the first (one hour), showing that motion with a slight obstacle is like motion without obstacle in speed. This leads to a contradiction because the original coercive motion would have to have a definite speed, but the comparison shows it is impossible. The commentary then elaborates on the definition of 'principle' (مبدأ) and defends the proof against possible objections, emphasizing that every motion must have a specific speed relative to others. The final part discusses a subtlety: whether the 'principle' of natural inclination is the act of repulsion itself or the underlying quality, noting that different interpretations affect the proof's completeness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a body without natural inclination could still move by force or will, because it has no internal resistance.
Correction: The proof shows that such a body cannot move coercively because any motion must have a definite speed relative to others, and the comparison with bodies that have inclination demonstrates an inconsistency.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the comparison between bodies with different inclinations moving the same distance?
A) To prove that natural inclination is necessary for any motion
B) To prove that a body lacking natural inclination cannot move by coercion or will
C) To prove that all motions have a definite speed
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
A body lacking natural inclination cannot move by any means—natural, coercive, or voluntary—because motion inherently requires a specific speed that only an inclination can provide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that without an inner principle of inclination, no motion—physical or voluntary—is possible. Consider your own spiritual life: do you rely on external pressure (e.g., guilt, fear) to move toward good, or have you cultivated a genuine inner inclination? If you lack that inclination, what would it take for you to acquire it, given that force alone is useless?
?
Every motion has a specific limit of speed and slowness. Reflect on the pace of your own personal growth. Are you trying to force rapid change while neglecting the gradual cultivation of inner dispositions? How does this lesson challenge the 'quick fix' mentality in your relationship with Allah?
?
The reductio assumes a body with no inclination and shows it cannot move. In your life, are there areas where you have 'no inclination'—no desire or natural pull toward obedience? How do you reconcile this with the command to act? Will you wait for inclination to appear, or is there another way?

Refining the Principle of Natural Inclination
Discusses the definition of 'principle' of natural inclination and its implications for the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced debate about whether the 'principle' of natural inclination is the act of repulsion itself or the underlying quality, and how this affects the proof.

Core Idea
The definition of 'principle' in natural inclination determines the completeness of the proof.

After establishing the rulings, the text delves into a critical discussion about the precise meaning of 'mabda' (principle) in relation to natural inclination. The outcome of this discussion affects whether the proof for the second ruling is complete. We will explore the two interpretations and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
The passage discusses whether 'mabda' refers to the act of repulsion (al-mudāfaʿa) itself or the quality that gives rise to it.
This distinction is subtle but crucial.
2
Understand the objection
If 'mabda' means the act of repulsion, an additional premise is needed: that inclination cannot exist without its principle. This premise may be problematic.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
If 'mabda' means the quality, then it is possible that nature itself is the principle of repulsion without the mediation of inclination. This would also create a gap in the proof.
4
Evaluate the overall argument
The passage concludes that similar objections arise for both interpretations, and the issue requires careful thought.

Source Text

أما الميل الطبيعي فأثبتوا له حكمين الأول : أنَّ العادم (له أي للميل الطبيعي بل لمبدئه ( لا يتحرك بالطبع وهو ظاهر) إذ لا معنى للحركة الطبيعية إلا ما مبدؤها القريب هو الميل الطبيعي ( ولا ) يتحرك أيضاً ( بالقسر والإرادة إذ لو تحرك العادم لمبدأ الميل الطبيعي بقوة

قسرية مثلا ) في مسافة ما في زمان ( لامتناع قطع المسافة المنقسمة في آن لما قطع بعضها مقدم على قطع كلها ( وليكن ) ذلك الزمان بالفرض (ساعة والذي ) أنَّ مبدأ ( الميل) الطبيعي أن يتحرك بتلك القوة المحركة ( في تلك المسافة ) المعينة ويقطعها ( في أكثر من ذلك الزمان لوجود العائق عن الحركة وهو مبدأ الميل ( الطبيعي ) وليكن ذلك الزمان الأكثر ) عشر ساعات فلآخر أي فلجسم آخر ( ميله عشر ميل) الجسم (الأول) أن يتحرك في تلك المسافة بتلك القوة المحركة ويقطعها (في ساعة أيضاً، إذ نسبة الحركتين كنسبة الميلين) المعاوقين وهي بالعشر في المثال المفروض ( فتكون الحركة مع المعاوق ) القليل ( كهي لامعة ) في السرعة والبطء لأنهما قطعتا مسافة واحدة في زمان واحد ) وقد عرفت مثله بما فيه ) من النظر ( في مسألة الخلاء فانقله إلى (هاهنا ولبعضهم في هذا المقام كلام جامع بين المسألتين وملخصه أن كل حركة لا بد أن تكون على حد معين من السرعة والبطء لأنها لا محالة تكون على مسافة وفي زمان فإذا فرضت حركة أخرى تقطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان أو في ضعفه . كانت الحركة الأولى أبطأ من على التقدير الأول وأسرع منها على التقدير الثاني فلا يمكن أن توجد حركة ما إلا

قوله : (جامع بين المسألتين) أي يفيد ثبوتهما معاً. قوله : ( أن كل حركة إلخ هذه المقدمة إلى قوله فإن كانت الحركة نفسانية غير مذكورة في كلام ذلك البعض بل المذكور ما نقلناه في بحث امتناع الخلاء. قوله : ( فإذا فرضت إلخ ) وإن كانت المفروضة مستحيلة فإنَّ إمكان فرضها كاف لنا في إثبات أن كل حركة نسبت إليها كانت موصوفة بحد من السرعة والبطء، فاندفع ما قيل: إنا لا نسلم إمكان وقوع حركة أخرى تقطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان مع نصف ذلك الزمان مع أنه لم يقتصر على وقوعها في النصف فقط بل ضم إليها وقوعها في الضعف أيضاً. ولا شك في إمكانه على أنا نقول : إمكان وقوع حركة أخرى نصفها في تلك المسافة كاف لنا في المطلوب لأنها إما واقعة في مثل زمانها، أو في أقل منه أو في أكثر منه فهي مساوية للحركة الأولى في حد من السرعة أو أسرع منه أو أبطأ فلا يمكن حركة إلا في حد من السرعة.

نفسها وكونه مبدأ قريباً للحركة الطبيعية بالنسبة إلى الطبيعة إذ لو أريد به نفس المدافعة لاحتيج في إثبات المطلوب إلى ضم مقدمة أخرى. وهي أنَّ الميل لا يمكن بدون مبدئه ثم هذه المقدمة وإن صحت إذا أريد بالمبدأ ما يعم الطبيعة لكن لا يتم التقريب لأن المقصود هاهنا إثبات الحكم المذكور لكيفية يكون الجسم بها مدافعاً لما يمنعه. وأما إذا أريد بالمبدأ نفس تلك الكيفية صحتها بحث لجواز أن يكون مبدأ لمدافعة نفس الطبيعة، بلا توسط ميل ثم لا يخفى ورود مثل هذا البحث على التوجيه الأول أيضاً فليتأمل.
As for natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī), they established two rulings for it. Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-commentary section establishing two rulings for natural inclination (الميل الطبيعي). Proof That Every Motion Has a Specific Limit of Speed
Proof that every motion has a specific speed, with objection response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that every motion must have a determinate speed and how the mere possibility of a hypothetical motion suffices for the proof.

Core Idea
Every motion has a specific speed because it can be compared to a hypothetical faster or slower motion.

This passage presents a concise proof that every motion must have a specific degree of speed or slowness. The author shows that any motion occurs over a distance in a time, and by supposing another motion covering the same distance in a different time, the original motion is relatively slow or fast. The commentary then addresses an objection about the possibility of such a hypothetical motion, affirming that mere possibility is enough.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get a sense of the overall argument and the objection-response structure.
Focus on understanding the logical flow, not every word.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that the proof starts with the fact that every motion occurs over a distance and takes time. This is the foundation for comparing motions.
3
Understand the hypothetical reasoning
The author imagines another motion covering the same distance in half the time or double the time. This comparison shows that the original motion has a specific speed relative to the hypothetical one.
Hypothetical motions are allowed even if impossible; only their possibility matters.
4
See the objection and response
The objector denies the possibility of such a hypothetical motion. The commentator replies that even if the motion is impossible, its mere possibility is sufficient for the proof. He also gives a simpler alternative: a motion covering half the distance in the same time.
This strengthens the argument by not relying on actual existence.
5
Reflect on the proof's strength
Consider why the commentator insists that possibility suffices. This shows that the proof does not depend on empirical existence but on rational necessity.
This is a common method in kalam: using hypotheticals to establish necessary truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولبعضهم في هذا المقام كلام جامع بين المسألتين وملخصه أن كل حركة لا بد أن تكون على حد معين من السرعة والبطء لأنها لا محالة تكون على مسافة وفي زمان فإذا فرضت حركة أخرى تقطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان أو في ضعفه كانت الحركة الأولى أبطأ من على التقدير الأول وأسرع منها على التقدير الثاني فلا يمكن أن توجد حركة ما إلا على حد معين من السرعة والبطء. قوله : (جامع بين المسألتين) أي يفيد ثبوتهما معاً. قوله : ( أن كل حركة إلخ هذه المقدمة إلى قوله فإن كانت الحركة نفسانية غير مذكورة في كلام ذلك البعض بل المذكور ما نقلناه في بحث امتناع الخلاء. قوله : ( فإذا فرضت إلخ ) وإن كانت المفروضة مستحيلة فإنَّ إمكان فرضها كاف لنا في إثبات أن كل حركة نسبت إليها كانت موصوفة بحد من السرعة والبطء، فاندفع ما قيل: إنا لا نسلم إمكان وقوع حركة أخرى تقطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان مع نصف ذلك الزمان مع أنه لم يقتصر على وقوعها في النصف فقط بل ضم إليها وقوعها في الضعف أيضاً. ولا شك في إمكانه على أنا نقول : إمكان وقوع حركة أخرى نصفها في تلك المسافة كاف لنا في المطلوب لأنها إما واقعة في مثل زمانها، أو في أقل منه أو في أكثر منه فهي مساوية للحركة الأولى في حد من السرعة أو أسرع منه أو أبطأ فلا يمكن حركة إلا في حد من السرعة.
And some of them have in this place a speech that combines the two issues, and its summary is that every motion must necessarily be upon a specific limit of speed and slowness, because it inevitably occurs over a distance and in a time. So if another motion is supposed to cover that same distance in half that time or in double it, the first motion would be slower than the other on the first estimation and faster than it on the second estimation. Thus no motion can exist except upon a specific limit of speed and slowness. He said: (Combining the two issues) meaning it benefits establishing both together. He said: (That every motion etc. — this introduction up to his statement: 'for if the motion is psychic' is not mentioned in the speech of that person, rather what is mentioned is what we transmitted in the discussion of the impossibility of void. He said: (So if it is supposed etc.) even if the supposed motion is impossible, indeed the possibility of supposing it is sufficient for us to prove that every motion relative to it was described by a limit of speed and slowness. Thus what was said is repelled: 'We do not concede the possibility of the occurrence of another motion covering that distance in half that time' along with half that time, and that he did not restrict to its occurrence in the half only but also included its occurrence in the double. There is no doubt about its possibility. Moreover, we say: The possibility of the occurrence of another motion covering half of it in that distance is sufficient for us for the desired end, because it either occurs in the same time as it, or in less than it, or in more than it; so it is equal to the first motion in a limit of speed, or faster than it, or slower than it. Thus no motion can exist except in a limit of speed.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and then responds to an objection. The author first summarizes a argument that every motion must have a specific degree of speed or slowness. The reason is that any motion occurs over a distance in a time. If we hypothetically consider another motion covering the same distance in half the time or in double the time, the original motion will be relatively slower or faster, so it must have a determinate speed. The commentator then explains that this proof comes from a certain author and addresses an objection: the objector denies the possibility of such another motion. The commentator replies that even if the supposed motion is impossible, its mere possibility is enough to establish that every motion has a specific speed relative to that hypothetical one. Moreover, the possibility of a motion covering half the distance in the same time is clearly possible, and that suffices. Thus the proof stands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof requires that another motion actually exists to compare.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that mere possibility of the hypothetical motion is enough to establish that every motion has a specific speed.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator respond to the objection that the hypothesized other motion might be impossible?
A) He concedes the objection and modifies the proof.
B) He argues that even the possibility of such a motion is enough to establish that every motion has a specific speed.
C) He denies that the hypothesized motion is impossible.
D) He says the proof only works for actual motions.

Key Takeaway
Every motion must have a specific speed, because even a hypothetical comparison is enough to assign it a relative limit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one area of your life where your spiritual 'speed'—the pace of your efforts in worship, charity, or self-improvement—could be compared to a hypothetical faster motion. If you imagined covering the same goal in half the time, what would that reveal about your current limit? Are you content to stay at that limit, or will you take one concrete action today to accelerate?
?
The lesson argues that even a hypothetical comparison suffices to assign a limit. In your relationship with Allah, do you ever use hypothetical excuses like 'If I had more time, I would pray more'? The mere possibility of a different pace exposes the actual limit of your devotion. Are you willing to admit that your current practice shows a specific limit of your commitment? What would it mean to push that limit starting right now?

Distinction between Psychic and Natural/Compulsory Motion
Defines psychic motion and natural/compulsory motion and the need for a mu'aWiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between psychic motion, which originates from consciousness and will and determines its own speed, and natural/compulsory motion, which lacks consciousness and requires an obstacle to determine its speed.

Core Idea
Psychic motion is self-determined by will; natural motion needs an obstacle.

This passage from al-Ījī's al-Mawāqif lays out a key distinction in the philosophy of motion. It explains that psychic motion, arising from consciousness and will, can determine its own speed, whereas natural and compulsory motions require an external obstacle to set their pace. This distinction underpins discussions about causality and the soul's role in movement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and the translation to get an overview of the definitions and the role of consciousness and obstacles in motion.
Focus on the core definitions rather than the detailed clarifications at first.
2
Identify the two types of motion
Find the definitions of psychic motion and natural/compulsory motion. Note what each requires for speed determination: psychic uses imagination and will; natural/compulsory needs an obstacle.
Psychic motion is linked to consciousness (shu'ur) and will (irada).
3
Understand the reasoning behind the difference
Recognize that consciousness enables self-determination, while unconscious motion cannot vary speed without external factors. The passage explains that nature lacks the consciousness needed to base different limits of speed and slowness.
This explains why natural motions are uniform in speed unless hindered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل على حد معين من السرعة والبطء فإن كانت الحركة نفسانية أي صادرة عن شعور وإرادة جاز أن تحدد النفس حالها من السرعة والبطء بأن يتخيل ملاءمة حد من حدودها وينبعث عنها الميل بحسب ذلك الحد فيترتب عليه الحركة السريعة أو البطيئة وإن كانت الحركة طبيعية أو قسرية احتاجت في تحديد حالها من الإسراع والإبطاء إلى معاوق . وذلك لأن الطبيعة لا شعور لها حتى يمكن استناد الحدود المختلفة التي قوله : ( أي صادرة إلخ ) سواء كانت على وتيرة واحدة أولا فيخرج عنها الحركات النباتية وتدخل في الطبيعية وليس المراد بها المعنى المتعارف الشامل للحركات النباتية . قوله : ( وينبعث عنها ) أي عن الملاءمة المتخيلة الميل المسمى بالإرادة في الحيوان أو المدافعة بحسب ذلك الحد المتخيل ملاءمته . قوله : ( وإن كانت الحركة طبيعية أي صادرة بلا شعور وإرادة سواء كانت على وتيرة واحدة كما في الأجسام البسيطة أو لا كما في النبات . قوله : ( لا شعور لها أي شعوراً يترتب عليه تعيين حد من السرعة والبطء وهو الشعور قوله : ( فإن كان الحركة نفسانية أي صادرة عن شعور وإرداة الحركة النفسانية قد تخص بالإرادية فالطبيعية التي تقابلها تفسر حينئذ بما يصدر من غير شعور وإرادة، وقد يجعل أعم منه أحد ومن قسمي الطبيعة أعني ما لا يكون على وتيرة واحدة لاختصاصه بذوات الأنفس وبهذا الاعتبار يسمى حركة النبات نفسانية وتخص الطبيعية حينئذ بما يصدر على نهج واحد دون شعور وإرادة وغير المحتاج في تحديد حال الحركة إلى المعاوق هو النفساني بالمعنى الأخص فلذا فسر الحركة النفسانية بما ذكر [؟]
The Third Observatory - The Third Objective: On Inclination, which is motion upon a specific limit of speed and slowness. If the motion is psychic, i.e., originating from consciousness and will, it is permissible for the soul to determine its state of speed and slowness by imagining the suitability of one of its limits, and the inclination arises from that imagined suitability, and the fast or slow motion results accordingly. If the motion is natural or compulsory, it needs an obstacle (mu'aWiq) to determine its state of acceleration and deceleration. That is because nature has no consciousness such that the different limits could be based on it. His saying: 'i.e., originating etc.' — whether it is on a uniform pattern or not, so plant motions are excluded and enter into natural motion, and the intended meaning is not the common meaning inclusive of plant motions. His saying: 'and arises from it' — i.e., from the imagined suitability, the inclination called will in animals or aversion according to that imagined limit of suitability. His saying: 'If the motion is natural' — i.e., originating without consciousness and will, whether it is on a uniform pattern, as in simple bodies, or not, as in plants. His saying: 'It has no consciousness' — i.e., a consciousness that would result in determining a limit of speed and slowness, and that is consciousness. His saying: 'If the motion is psychic' — i.e., originating from consciousness and will. Psychic motion may be specifically identified with voluntary motion; then natural motion opposite to it is interpreted as what originates without consciousness and will, and it may be made more general, one of the two divisions of nature, namely what is not on a uniform pattern because it is specific to living beings, and in this respect plant motion is called psychic, and natural motion is then specified as what issues in a uniform manner without consciousness and will. That which does not need an obstacle in determining the state of motion is the psychic in the most specific sense; therefore, psychic motion is interpreted as mentioned [؟].
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between psychic motion (حركة نفسانية) and natural/compulsory motion (حركة طبيعية أو قسرية). It states that psychic motion originates from consciousness (شعور) and will (إرادة), allowing the soul to determine its speed by imagining a suitable limit, resulting in an inclination that produces motion. Natural or compulsory motion lacks such consciousness, so it requires an obstacle (معاوق) to determine its speed. The commentary then clarifies the definitions: 'psychic' excludes plant motions (which are considered natural); the imagined suitability gives rise to will or aversion; natural motion occurs without consciousness and will, whether uniform or not; the lack of consciousness means no determination of speed; and psychic motion in the specific sense (voluntary) does not need an obstacle, while natural and compulsory motions do. The passage thus establishes a key distinction in the causal determination of motion.

Key Terms
حركة نفسانية
حركة طبيعية
حركة قسرية
معاوق
شعور
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All motion requires consciousness to determine its speed.
Correction: Only psychic motion (from consciousness and will) is self-determined; natural and compulsory motions lack consciousness and require external obstacles to set their speed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what determines the speed of psychic motion?
A) An external obstacle (mu'aWiq)
B) The soul's will based on imagined suitability
C) The inherent nature of the body
D) The coercer's force

Key Takeaway
Psychic motion is self-determined through consciousness and will, while natural and compulsory motions rely on external obstacles to set their speed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you living your life by psychic motion—consciously choosing your pace and direction—or are you drifting like natural motion, letting obstacles dictate your speed? What one area of your life needs you to take the reins of your will today?
?
When do you rely on an external 'obstacle' (like pressure from others or circumstances) to change your speed, instead of internally deciding to slow down or accelerate? What would it cost you to start making that decision yourself?
?
The passage says instinctive movements (like plants) are considered natural, lacking consciousness. Where in your own actions are you operating on autopilot, without conscious awareness? How can you inject awareness into that habit starting tomorrow?

Objection on the Condition of Vision and Second Proof that Darkness is Privative
Objection regarding the condition of vision and a second proof for the privative nature of darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that darkness might be a real obstacle around the visible object is answered and how the second proof uses the imagined absence of light to show darkness is privative.

Core Idea
What we perceive as darkness is not a real thing but an absence of light, as the closed-eye analogy shows.

In this passage, the author first considers an objection: perhaps the condition for vision is light surrounding the visible object, so darkness around it could be a real obstacle. He then provides a second proof that darkness is privation, using the thought experiment of a body devoid of light and the analogy of closing one's eyes. This solidifies the earlier conclusion by showing that our perception of darkness is imaginary, not real.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from objection to proof.
Parentheses indicate original text; they are not editorial additions.
2
Identify the objection and its premise
Find the objection: the condition of vision might be light surrounding the visible object. On this view, darkness could be a real obstacle around the object, causing the difference in vision.
The author says this statement is not far-fetched.
3
Follow the second proof and its analogy
Read the second proof: if we imagine a body devoid of light with no other attribute, what we perceive as darkness is not a real sensation. The analogy of closing the eyes reinforces this: when we close our eyes, we imagine seeing blackness, but we see nothing.
Thus darkness is not a real thing but a privation.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Ask yourself: why is it important to prove darkness is privative? How does this proof address the objection?
This secures the earlier conclusion that darkness is not a positive entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن شرط الرؤية ضوء محيط بالمرئي لا الضوء مطلقاً ولا الضوء المحيط بالمرئي، فقد يكون العائق ) عن الرؤية ( ظلمة تحيط به أي بالمرئي لا الظلمة المحيطة بالرائي ولا الظلمة مطلقاً (لم يكن هذا القول ( بعيداً) وحينئذ تكون الظلمة أمراً موجوداً عائقاً اختلاف حال الجالس والخارج في الرؤية كما ذكر. وقد يستدل على كونها عدمية بأنا إذا قدرنا خلو الجسم عن النور من غير انضياف صفة آخر إليه لم يكن حاله إلا هذه الظلمة التي نتخيلها أمرا محسوسا في الهواء وليس هناك أمر محسوس ألا ترى أنا إذا غمضنا العين كان حالنا كما إذا فتحناها في الظلمة الشديدة ولا شك أنا لا نرى في حال التغميض شيئاً في جفوننا بل لنا في هذه الحالة أنا لا نرى شيئاً فنتخيل أنا نرى كيفية كالسواد فكذا الحال في تخيلنا الظلمة أمراً محسوساً.
That the condition of vision is light surrounding the visible object, not light absolutely, nor light surrounding the viewer. So the obstacle to vision might be darkness surrounding it, i.e., the visible object, not the darkness surrounding the viewer, nor darkness absolutely. (This statement would not be far-fetched.) And then darkness would be an existing thing that obstructs, causing the difference in the state of the one sitting and the outside in vision as mentioned. And it may be inferred that it is privative by that if we suppose the body devoid of light without the addition of any other attribute to it, its state would be nothing but this darkness that we imagine as a sensed thing in the air, but there is no sensed thing. Do you not see that if we close our eyes, our state is as if we open them in intense darkness? And there is no doubt that we see nothing in the state of closure in our eyelids; rather, in this state we see nothing, so we imagine that we see a quality like blackness. So likewise is the case in our imagining darkness as a sensed thing.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a second proof. The objection states that the condition for vision might be light surrounding the visible object, not absolute light or light surrounding the viewer. On this view, darkness could be a real obstacle surrounding the object, causing the difference in vision described. The answer is that this is not far-fetched. The passage then gives a second proof: if we imagine a body devoid of light with no other attribute, what we perceive as darkness is not a real sensation but an illusion. Analogously, when we close our eyes, we imagine seeing blackness, but we see nothing. Thus darkness is not a real entity but a privation. This proof reinforces the original conclusion by showing that our perception of darkness as something sensed is merely imaginary.

Key Terms
شرط الرؤية
ضوء محيط
ظلمة محيطة
عدمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Darkness is a real, positive entity that we sense.
Correction: The second proof shows that what we perceive as darkness is merely the absence of light; it is not a real sensed thing, as illustrated by the closed-eye analogy where we imagine blackness but see nothing.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof that darkness is privation?
A) The cave example
B) The Quranic verse
C) The imagined absence of light and the closed-eye analogy
D) The branch about stars

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses the thought experiment of a lightless body and the closed-eye analogy to demonstrate that darkness is merely the absence of light, not a real quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in the darkness of ignorance or sin, do you realize that what you perceive as a real obstacle may actually be just an absence of truth? How will you seek the light of knowledge today?
?
You often imagine that the darkness around you—whether physical or spiritual—is a real force blocking your path. But this lesson teaches that darkness is merely the absence of light. What 'darkness' in your life have you been treating as a real entity, when in fact you just need to invite light? What one step will you take to bring light into that area today?
?
When you close your eyes, you see nothing but imagine blackness. In the same way, you may imagine that certain sins or doubts are real barriers, but they are just absences of faith. Are you going to keep imagining, or will you open your eyes to the truth by seeking knowledge and repentance?
