The Objection on Insipidity as a Taste: A Kalam Analysis
Discusses and resolves an objection about counting insipidity among tastes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against counting insipidity among tastes and the response based on the distinction between real and unreal insipidity, along with the classification of tastes by temperature.

Core Idea
Insipidity is not absence but a genuine taste — defended by its coexistence with bitterness.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a subtle objection: some scholars argued that insipidity (tufāha) is merely the absence of taste and should not be counted among tastes, similar to how the absolute proposition is counted among modalized ones. The passage then presents the counter-argument that insipidity in its unreal sense is a simple taste, and evidence from the coexistence of bitterness and insipidity in chicory refutes the objection. It further enriches the discussion by classifying tastes into hot and cold categories, providing a detailed analysis of their properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'tufāha' and 'ḥarāfah'.
2
Identify the central objection
Understand why some scholars thought insipidity is not a taste. They analogized it to the 'absolute' proposition in logic.
The objection equates insipidity with absence of taste.
3
Examine the response
See how the commentator refutes the objection by distinguishing real and unreal insipidity, and using the example of chicory to show coexistence of tastes.
The coexistence of bitterness and insipidity in chicory invalidates the objection.
4
Analyze the enumeration of tastes
Note the classification of hot and cold tastes and the reasoning behind the order. Look at the evidence from chemistry and fruit ripening.
This enumeration underscores that insipidity is a genuine taste among others.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this debate illustrates the precision of Islamic theological reasoning and its engagement with empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وقد توهم بعضهم أن المعدود في الطعوم هو التفاهة بمعنى عدم الطعم . قال : وإنما عدوها منها كما عدت المطلقة في الموجهات ولذلك تركها الإمام الرازي رحمه الله فقال: من قوله : ( ومسيخاً) المسيخ من اللحم ما لا طعم له قوله : ( وأجزاء صغاراً) العبارة بالواو لا بأو كما في بعض النسخ لأنه تفسير لقوله زنجاراً كما نقل عن الشارح.
بسائط الطعوم ثمانية. وذكر بعضهم أنه المعدود فيها هو التفاهة الغير الحقيقية. فإنها طعم بسيط. ورد عليه بأن هذا يبطله ما ذكره من اجتماع المرارة والتفاهة في الهندباء، وقد ذكروا أن أسخن الطعوم الحرافة ثم المرارة ثم الملوحة لأن الحريف أقوى على التحليل من المر، ثم المالح كأنه مر مكسور برطوبة باردة لما عرفت من حدوث الملوحة ويدل أيضاً على تأخر الملوحة عن المرارة في السخونة أن البورق والملح المر أسخن من الملح المأكول. وأبرد الطعوم العفوصة ثم القبض ثم الحموضة. فإنَّ الفواكه التي تحلو تكون أولاً عفصة شديدة البرد فإذا اعتدلت قليلاً قليلاً بإسخان الشمس مالت إلى القبض ثم الى الحموضة ثم تنتقل إلى الحلاوة والحامض، وإن كان أقل برداً من العفص لكنه في الأغلب أكثر تبريداً منه لشدة غوصه بسبب لطافته .
Some have imagined that what is counted among tastes is insipidity in the sense of absence of taste. He said: They only count it among them as the absolute is counted among modalized propositions, and for that reason Imam al-Razi abandoned it. He said: from his statement: (and tasteless) al-masīkh is meat without taste; his statement: (and small parts) the expression is with wāw not with 'aw as in some copies because it is an interpretation of his word 'zanjār' as transmitted from the commentator. The simple tastes are eight. And some mentioned that what is counted among them is unreal insipidity, for it is a simple taste. And it was objected that this is invalidated by what they mentioned of the coexistence of bitterness and insipidity in chicory. And they mentioned that the hottest of tastes is pungency, then bitterness, then saltiness, because the pungent is stronger in analysis than the bitter, then the salty as if it is bitter broken by a cold moisture, as you know from the occurrence of saltiness. And also indicative of the lateness of saltiness after bitterness in heat is that borax and bitter salt are hotter than edible salt. And the coldest of tastes is astringency, then astringent-puckering, then sourness. For fruits that become sweet are first intensely cold astringent, then when they gradually moderate by the heating of the sun they incline to astringent-puckering, then to sourness, then they transition to sweetness and sourness, although it is less cold than astringency but is often more cooling due to its intense penetration because of its fineness.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and discussion about whether insipidity (tufāha) should be counted among the tastes. Some scholars thought that insipidity in the sense of 'absence of taste' is counted only by analogy (like the absolute proposition counted among modalized ones), and therefore Imam al-Razi omitted it. The commentator then notes that the simple tastes are eight, and others hold that it is unreal insipidity (a simple taste) that is counted. The objection is raised that this is refuted by the coexistence of bitterness and insipidity in chicory (hindibā'), implying that if insipidity were a taste, it would not coexist with another taste. The passage then lists the hottest tastes (pungency, bitterness, saltiness) with reasons based on their analytical power, and the coldest tastes (astringency, astringent-puckering, sourness), illustrating with the ripening of fruits from astringent to sour to sweet. The argument follows the commentator's order: first the objection, then the response implied by the enumeration showing that insipidity can coexist as a distinct taste.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Insipidity (tufāha) is merely the absence of taste and should not be counted among tastes.
Correction: Insipidity in its unreal sense is a genuine simple taste, as demonstrated by its coexistence with bitterness in chicory and its inclusion in the enumeration of tastes.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The debate over insipidity shows the importance of precise definitions in kalam, and the classification of tastes illustrates the empirical grounding of Islamic science.

?
The debate over insipidity may seem trivial, but it reflects the rigor required to avoid error in aqeedah. What area of your faith are you most careless about precise definitions? How might that carelessness affect your certainty?

The Rational Order of Hot and Cold Tastes
Enumerates and explains the ranked sequence of tastes by heat and cold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional ranking of tastes by their inherent heat and cold, and the rationale behind each classification.

Core Idea
Tastes have a rational hierarchy of hotness and coldness based on their properties.

This passage from al-Mawaqif presents a systematic classification of tastes according to their degrees of heat and cold. It lists the hottest and coldest tastes in descending order and provides supporting evidence and a vivid example from fruit ripening. Understanding this ranking helps grasp how medieval scholars used rational analysis to categorize sensory qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
First read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice the two main sections: hottest tastes and coldest tastes.
2
Identify the two categories and their order
Locate the lists: hottest tastes (3 items) and coldest tastes (3 items). Note how each item is ranked and the reasoning given.
Pungency > bitterness > saltiness; astringency > astringent-puckering > sourness.
3
Understand the reasoning behind saltiness
The passage explains that saltiness is like bitterness but broken by cold moisture. Evidence from borax and bitter salt shows bitterness is hotter than saltiness.
This connects to the concept of 'analysis' (تحليل) – the ability to dissolve substances.
4
Examine the fruit ripening example
Read how fruits transition from cold astringency to sweetness, passing through astringent-puckering and sourness. This illustrates the gradual change from cold to hot.
The sun's heat moderates the fruit's coldness step by step.
5
Reflect on the subtle point about sourness
Note the comment that sourness, though less cold in essence, often feels more cooling due to its penetrating fineness. This shows the classification is not merely about temperature but about effect.
This helps avoid oversimplifying the concept of 'coldness' in tastes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكروا أن أسخن الطعوم الحرافة ثم المرارة ثم الملوحة لأن الحريف أقوى على التحليل من المر، ثم المالح كأنه مر مكسور برطوبة باردة لما عرفت من حدوث الملوحة ويدل أيضاً على تأخر الملوحة عن المرارة في السخونة أن البورق والملح المر أسخن من الملح المأكول. وأبرد الطعوم العفوصة ثم القبض ثم الحموضة. فإنَّ الفواكه التي تحلو تكون أولاً عفصة شديدة البرد فإذا اعتدلت قليلاً قليلاً بإسخان الشمس مالت إلى القبض ثم الى الحموضة ثم تنتقل إلى الحلاوة والحامض، وإن كان أقل برداً من العفص لكنه في الأغلب أكثر تبريداً منه لشدة غوصه بسبب لطافته .
And they mentioned that the hottest of tastes is pungency, then bitterness, then saltiness, because the pungent is stronger in analysis than the bitter, then the salty as if it is bitter broken by a cold moisture, as you know from the occurrence of saltiness. Also indicative of the lateness of saltiness after bitterness in heat is that borax and bitter salt are hotter than edible salt. And the coldest of tastes is astringency, then astringent-puckering, then sourness. For fruits that become sweet are first intensely cold astringent, then when they gradually moderate by the heating of the sun they incline to astringent-puckering, then to sourness, then they transition to sweetness and sourness, although it is less cold than astringency but is often more cooling due to its intense penetration because of its fineness.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage enumerates the ranking of tastes according to their inherent heat and cold. It first lists the hottest: pungency (ḥarāfa) is strongest in analyzing (dissolving) substances, then bitterness (marāra), then saltiness (mulūḥa), which is considered like bitterness broken by cold moisture. Evidence for saltiness being less hot than bitterness is that borax and bitter salt are hotter than common salt. Then it lists the coldest: astringency (ʿufūṣa), then astringent-puckering (qabḍ), then sourness (ḥumūḍa). The explanation uses the example of fruit ripening: initially intensely cold astringent, then as the sun heats it, it becomes astringent-puckering, then sour, then sweet. Interestingly, sourness, though less cold than astringency, often feels more cooling due to its penetrating nature because of its fineness. The passage follows a clear enumerative structure, listing each taste with a brief justification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Coldness of tastes is solely about temperature; sourness is simply less cold than astringency.
Correction: Sourness is less cold in essence but can be more cooling in effect due to its penetrating fineness, as stated in the passage.

Checkpoint

B) Because borax is hotter than edible salt

D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Tastes are rationally ranked by heat and cold, with pungency hottest, then bitterness, saltiness; astringency coldest, then astringent-puckering, then sourness, explained by physical properties and illustrated by fruit ripening.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that cold tastes can be subtle—sourness feels more cooling despite being less cold in essence. What 'cold' habits in your heart—like pride or negligence—might be more penetrating than you realize because of their subtlety? Are you willing to expose them to the warmth of sincere repentance?
?

Are you embracing the pungent moments of life as purifying agents, or do you resist them? What would it mean to trust Allah's order even when it burns?

Transition from Sourness to Sweetness: An Objection and Answer
Addresses an objection about the possibility of sweetness after sourness in fruit ripening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the possibility of sweetness after sourness in fruit ripening and the answer that explains it through changes in moisture and density.

Core Idea
Sweetness after sourness requires densification, not just rarefaction.

This passage delves into a specific objection about the sequence of taste development in ripening fruits. The objection claims that once sourness occurs, the fruit becomes finer and cannot become dense enough to bear sweetness. The answer reveals a more nuanced process involving loss of moisture and subsequent densification. Understanding this argument highlights the precision required in kalam discussions of natural processes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the objection and the answer.
2
Identify key physical concepts
Notice the concepts of 'dense body', 'fine body', 'heating by the sun', and 'wateriness of the body'. Understand how these relate to the tastes.
The objection assumes that sweetness requires a dense body.
3
Understand the answer's mechanism
The answer explains that continued heating reduces wateriness, causing both rarefaction (loss of water) and densification (concentration of solids), making the body receptive to sweetness.
This is a dual process: rarefaction and densification occur together.
4
Note the commentator's stylistic observation
The commentator points out that the author uses the verb 'tantaqil' (transition) here, whereas for earlier stages he used different verbs. This indicates a deliberate change to emphasize the transition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثم تنتقل إلى الحلاوة قيل : ينبغي أن لا يجوز الانتقال إلى الحلاوة بعد الحموضة لما تقرر من أنَّ حامل الحلاوة هو الجسم الكثيف، وهو من جهة إسخان الشمس صار لطيفاً، ولذا حصل الحموضة قبلها بل صار ألطف بسبب الاعتدال قليلا قليلاً بالإسخان وقد يجاب : بأنه لما كثر إسخان الشمس بعد الحموضة قل مائية الجسم فحصل التخفيف والتكثيف فيه فصار قابلاً للحلاوة، ولذا غير الشارح الأسلوب. وقال: ثم تنتقل بذكر الانتقال ولم يذكره في القبض والحموضة.
His statement: (then they transition to sweetness) It was said: It is not permissible for there to be a transition to sweetness after sourness, because it is established that the bearer of sweetness is the dense body, and it, by virtue of the sun's heating, became fine, and therefore sourness occurred before it; rather it became finer due to moderation little by little by the heating. And it may be answered: because when the sun's heating increased after sourness, the wateriness of the body decreased, so rarefaction and densification occurred in it, and it became receptive to sweetness. And therefore the commentator changed the style, and said: 'then they transition' mentioning the transition, whereas he did not mention it in astringency and astringent-puckering.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the sequence of taste development in ripening fruits. The objection states that after sourness, the body becomes finer due to the sun's heat, so it should not become dense enough to bear sweetness (which requires density). The answer is that after sourness, continued heating reduces the water content (moisture), causing the body to undergo both rarefaction and densification: as water evaporates, the remaining parts become denser and thus receptive to sweetness. The commentator notes that the author changed the wording to 'then they transition' (using the verb 'tantaqil') to emphasize the transition, which was not used for the previous steps (astringency to astringent-puckering to sourness). This shows the author's precision in indicating a change in the process.

Key Terms
الانتقال
الحموضة
الحلاوة
الجسم الكثيف
إسخان الشمس
مائية الجسم
التخفيف
التكثيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The transition from sour to sweet is a simple continuation of rarefaction (making the fruit finer).
Correction: In fact, after sourness, increased heating reduces moisture, causing both rarefaction (water loss) and densification (concentration of solids), which together enable sweetness.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what enables the transition from sourness to sweetness?
A) The body becomes finer and lighter
B) The sun's heat continues to increase
C) The body loses moisture and becomes denser
D) The sweetness is already present but hidden

Key Takeaway
The transition from sour to sweet involves both rarefaction and densification, not simply further rarefaction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever faced a situation where you felt you were becoming weaker or more 'refined' (like the fruit becoming sour), but then Allah brought about a strengthening ('densification') that led to a sweet outcome? How does this natural process challenge your assumptions about how growth happens, and will you trust His plan even when the process seems contradictory?
?
This lesson shows that apparent opposites (rarefaction and densification) can occur simultaneously through a single cause. Is there an area in your life where you are resisting a transition because it seems logically impossible? How can the principle here—that deeper causes can harmonize opposites—change your perspective?
?
When you experience a 'sour' trial, do you believe that the same heat (difficulty) that thins you can also thicken you for a future sweetness? What specific trial are you currently facing, and how can you actively look for the concealed densification (strengthening) within it?
